Jihad — Armed Conflict Rules

Ruling on Banu Taghlib's Jizya and Their Mawali
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the tax status of Taghlibi clients.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the taxation of Banu Taghlib's mawali and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute between the majority and Imam Zufar.

Core Idea
The mawla of a Taghlibi pays jizya and kharaj, not double zakat, despite his master's leniency.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school concerning Banu Taghlib, a Christian Arab tribe, and the legal status of their freed slaves (mawali). The key is distinguishing between the lenient double-zakat system for Taghlibis themselves and the standard jizya/kharaj system for their clients. We will explore the proof texts and the reasoning behind the majority view versus the minority view of Imam Zufar.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the two parts: Taghlibi pays double zakat; his mawla pays jizya and kharaj.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opposing view
Imam Zufar holds that the mawla also pays double zakat.
Zufar is a student of Abu Hanifa.
3
Examine Zufar's dalil
Zufar relies on the hadith 'Mawla al-qawm minhum' (the client of a people is from them).
This hadith is hasan sahih.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The hadith is limited to the prohibition of sadaqa (e.g., for Hashimi clients). The double zakat is a leniency (takhfif) not involving humiliation, so it does not extend.
Consensus (ijma) indicates that a lower client does not follow a higher client in all matters.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: A freed Christian slave of a Taghlibi pays jizya/kharaj (majority) or double zakat (Zufar).
The majority view is relied upon.

And it is answered, as in al-Hidaya and others: The rich person is eligible to take sadaqa in general, but his wealth prevents him from taking it. Since this preventer is not found in his client, it is not prohibited for him, unlike the Hashimi, for he is not eligible for sadaqa, because his noble lineage and high status prevent him from taking it, so his client becomes like him in this virtue. [3] His statement: and kharaj; as for 'ushr, its expenditure is the expenditure of zakat as has passed in its place. [4] His statement: and their gift to the imam; it is mentioned in al-Jawhara that it is permissible for the imam to accept a gift from disbelievers if they believe that our fighting is for religion, not for worldly gain, on condition that the giver is not hoped to become a believer if his gift is rejected. If that is the case, the imam rejects his gift hoping for his faith. It has been established in the authentic books: 'The Prophet accepted gifts from disbelievers more than once.'
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya, a commentary on al-Wiqaya, discusses the legal status of Banu Taghlib, a Christian Arab tribe, and their mawali (clients) in relation to jizya and kharaj. The author first states the ruling: The Taghlibi (a member of Banu Taghlib) pays double the zakat of Muslims (2.5% on wealth and 5% on land) instead of jizya, as per the pact of 'Umar. However, his mawla (client) pays jizya and kharaj like any other dhimmi. This is analogous to the client of a Qurayshi, who also pays jizya and kharaj despite his master being exempt from jizya. The author notes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Zufar (a student of Abu Hanifa) holds that the mawla should also pay double zakat like his master, based on the hadith 'Mawla al-qawm minhum' (the client of a people is from them). The Hanafi response distinguishes between the Taghlibi's double payment (which is a leniency, not a humiliation) and jizya/kharaj (which are humiliating). The client does not share in this leniency. However, the hadith is applied in the prohibition of sadaqa: the client of a Hashimi is treated like the Hashimi in being prohibited from taking sadaqa, because prohibitions are established by similarities. The passage then discusses the expenditure of jizya, kharaj, the wealth of Taghlibis, and gifts to the imam. The hadith is limited to the prohibition of sadaqa only. A potential objection is raised: if prohibitions are based on similarities, then the client of a rich person should also be prohibited from taking sadaqa. The answer: the rich person is prevented by his wealth, which does not apply to his client, whereas the Hashimi is prevented by his noble lineage, which his client shares. Finally, it mentions that 'ushr (tithe on land) is spent like zakat, and gifts to the imam from disbelievers are permissible under certain conditions.

Key Terms
الجزية (al-jizya): A tax imposed on non-Muslims living under Muslim rule, in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.
الخراج (al-kharaj): A tax on land owned by non-Muslims or conquered land.
مولى (mawla): A client or freed slave; here it refers to the freed slave of a Taghlibi or Qurayshi.
ضعف (di'f): Double; the Taghlibi pays double the zakat of a Muslim (i.e., 5% on land instead of 2.5%, and 2.5% on other wealth instead of 1.25%).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, what does the mawla (freed slave) of a Taghlibi pay?
A) Double zakat like his master
B) Jizya and kharaj like other dhimmis
C) Nothing at all
D) Jizya only, no kharaj

Key Takeaway
The mawla of a Taghlibi pays jizya and kharaj, not double zakat, because the leniency for Taghlibis does not extend to their clients.

The Scope of the Hadith on Mawali of Hashimites and Sadaqah Prohibition
Commentary on the scope of the hadith regarding mawali and sadaqah prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school restricts the application of the hadith 'the mawla of a people is one of them' to the prohibition of sadaqah for the mawla of a Hashimi, and distinguish this from other areas like marriage compatibility.

Core Idea
The mawla of a Hashimi is like a Hashimi only in the prohibition of sadaqah, not in all legal aspects.

The hadith 'the mawla of a people is one of them' is often cited to extend the rulings of a tribe to their clients. However, Hanafi jurists interpret this hadith narrowly, applying it only to the prohibition of receiving zakah. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this restriction, addressing objections and clarifying the underlying principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Determine the legal status of the mawla of a Hashimi regarding sadaqah: the mawla is treated like a Hashimi and thus prohibited from taking sadaqah.
2
Understand the scope limitation
Recognize that this ruling is limited to the prohibition of sadaqah and does not extend to other areas such as marriage compatibility (kafa'ah) or jizya.
Consensus that the lower mawla does not fully join the higher mawla supports this limitation.
3
Analyze the objection
Examine the objection that the hadith is absolute, so limiting it is arbitrary. The response relies on consensus to restrict its application.
4
Examine the principle of 'prohibitions by resemblances'
Understand why this principle does not make the mawla of a rich person prohibited from sadaqah: the rich person is eligible for sadaqah but prevented by wealth, while the Hashimi is inherently ineligible due to lineage.
5
Apply to other cases
Confirm practical differences: the mawla of a Qurayshi is not considered compatible in marriage, and the mawla of a Muslim Christian must pay jizya.

I say: The indication of consensus that the lower mawla does not join the higher mawla indicates the non-consideration of its absoluteness. [2] His saying: Because prohibitions... etc.; it is objected to him that if prohibitions were established by resemblances, then it would necessitate that taking sadaqah would be prohibited for the mawla of a rich person just as it is prohibited for the rich person, and that is not so by agreement. And it is answered about it as in al-Hidayah and others: The rich person is eligible to take sadaqah in general, but it is prevented from him due to his wealth; when this preventing factor is not found in his mawla, it does not become prohibited for him, unlike the Hashimi, for he is not eligible for sadaqah because his nobility of essence and majesty of status prevent him from taking it, so his mawla joins him in this merit. Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rule regarding the expenditure of jizya, kharaj, and gifts from non-Muslims, with a focus on the status of the mawla (client) of a Hashimi. The author clarifies that the hadith making the mawla like the Hashimi applies only to the prohibition of sadaqah (charity), not to other areas such as compatibility in marriage (kafa'ah) or the obligation of jizya on the mawla. The commentator addresses an objection: if the hadith is absolute, why limit it? He replies that consensus shows the lower mawla does not fully join the higher mawla, so the absoluteness is not upheld. Another objection concerns the principle that prohibitions are established by resemblances: if that were absolute, the mawla of a rich person would also be prohibited from sadaqah. The response distinguishes the rich person from the Hashimi: a rich person is eligible for sadaqah in general but prevented by wealth, whereas a Hashimi is inherently ineligible due to his noble lineage, so his mawla shares that ineligibility. The passage then clarifies that 'ushr (tithe from agricultural land) is distributed like zakah, not like kharaj. Finally, it discusses gifts to the Imam from non-Muslims: permissible if it shows the disbelievers understand the war is for religion and if rejecting the gift would not harm hopes for their faith. The Prophet's acceptance of gifts is cited as precedent.

Key Terms
مولى القوم (mawla al-qawm): The client or freed slave of a tribe; someone who has a patronage relationship with the tribe.
حرمة الصدقة (hurmat al-sadaqah): The prohibition of taking obligatory charity (zakah) for certain individuals like Hashimis and their mawali.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the mawla of a rich person not prohibited from taking sadaqah, while the mawla of a Hashimi is?
A) Because the rich person's mawla is rich himself.
B) Because the rich person is eligible for sadaqah but prevented by wealth, whereas the Hashimi is ineligible due to lineage.
C) Because the hadith only applies to Hashimis, not to the rich.
D) Because the mawla of a rich person converted to Islam.

Key Takeaway
The mawla of a Hashimi is like a Hashimi only in the prohibition of sadaqah, not in other legal areas such as marriage compatibility or jizya, due to the distinction between lineage-based and wealth-based ineligibility.

Permissible Uses of Funds from Non-Muslims Without War
Listing and explaining permissible uses of funds taken from non-Muslims without war

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the permissible expenditures from funds taken from non-Muslims without war, and distinguish them from spoils taken in war.

Core Idea
Peace treaty funds can fortify borders, build bridges, support mosques, and sustain scholars.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies how funds obtained from non-Muslims through peace agreements (without war) may be spent. Unlike war spoils (ghanima), these funds have a broader scope for public welfare, including infrastructure and religious institutions. Understanding this distinction is essential for the correct allocation of state resources in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of funds
Understand that 'what is taken without war' refers to wealth from peace treaties before military engagement, as defined in the text.
See the commentary's reference to an-Nahr.
2
List the explicit examples
Note from the text: fortifying border fortresses (thughur), building qanatara (bridges/aqueducts), and supporting scholars.
3
Recognize the expansion by analogy (qiyas)
The commentary extends these to mosques, cisterns, ribats, digging canals, and expenses for mosque functions (imamate, adhan).
This is based on the principle of public benefit (maslaha).
4
Understand the principle of sufficiency (kifayah)
For scholars, only need-based support is allowed, not excess.
The word 'kifayah' indicates moderation.
5
Note the legal category
These expenditures are mubah (permissible) and fall under public interest as per Hanafi fiqh.
Contrast with war spoils which have fixed shares.

Commentary
This passage explains the permissible expenditures from funds taken from non-Muslims through peace agreements (without war), as distinguished from spoils taken in war. The commentator first clarifies that 'what is taken without war' refers specifically to wealth taken by peace treaty before the army engages, as defined in an-Nahr. The examples given are: (1) Fortifying border fortresses (thughur) — spending on troops and construction to protect Muslim frontiers. This shows that public welfare and religious education are legitimate uses of these funds.

ثغر (thaghr): A border fortress or frontier outpost adjacent to non-Muslim lands.
قنطرة (qantara): A structure built over water for crossing, like a bridge or aqueduct.
كفاية العلماء (kifayat al-'ulama): Providing sufficient support for scholars of religious sciences and their students.

Permissible Expenditures from Jizyah Funds
Expansion of permissible uses of treaty-derived funds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on permissible uses of funds taken from non-Muslims without war (jizyah) and identify the conditions for each use.

Core Idea
Jizyah funds may be used for public works and scholar support, but not beyond actual need.

In this passage, the Hanafi school details the permissible expenditures of wealth acquired from non-Muslims through peace treaties (jizyah). The commentator expands each item—frontier defense, bridges, mosques, and sufficiency for scholars—with analogies and conditions. Understanding these allocations is crucial for the imam's governance of public funds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Understand that the passage addresses wealth taken by the imam from non-Muslims under a peace treaty before hostilities—this is jizyah, not war spoils.
The term 'without war' (bila harb) specifies treaty-based acquisition.
2
Examine the first expenditure: frontier defense
Filling thughur (plural of thaghr) means spending on armies and fortifications at the borderlands adjacent to enemy territory.
This includes salaries for guards and building/repairing forts.
3
Analyze the analogical expansion: arches, bridges, and beyond
Building a qantarah (arch) is explicitly mentioned; the commentator analogizes this to mosques, water troughs, ribats, and digging major canals. This shows the principle of qiyas (analogy) extends the ruling to all public works.
Al-Barjandi's explanation confirms that 'jisr' covers any crossing, built or otherwise.
4
Understand the condition on scholar stipends
Kifayat al-ulama (sufficiency of scholars) covers religious scholars and students, but the word 'kifaya' (sufficiency) indicates the amount should not exceed actual need—preventing waste.
This distinguishes ongoing support from fixed infrastructure projects.
5
Synthesize the overall ruling
The imam may use jizyah funds for public welfare: defense, infrastructure, religious institutions, and scholar support, with the condition that the latter is limited to need.
All these categories fall under the general objective of preserving the faith and the state (maslaha).

Categories Eligible for State Stipends in Hanafi Fiqh
Lists categories eligible for state stipends and explains their inclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories entitled to state stipends (rizq) in Hanafi fiqh and explain why children's stipends are considered independent grants rather than inheritance.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, state stipends cover fighters, judges, administrators, and their dependents as independent entitlements, not inheritable.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on who is eligible for state stipends from the Bayt al-Mal. The passage expands the definition of 'workers' to include various administrative and religious roles, and clarifies that fighters' stipends cover their families. A key point from Abu Yusuf is that children of entitled persons receive their own stipend as a dependent benefit, not as inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Read the matn and list: judges (al-qudat), workers (al-ummal), fighters (al-muqatilah), and their children (al-dharari).
These are the primary recipients from the state treasury.
2
Expand the definition of 'workers'
The commentator explains that 'workers' includes scribes, appraisers, coast guards, preachers, muhtasibs, muftis, and unpaid teachers. Recognize the broad scope of administrative and religious roles.
Any public servant fulfilling a necessary role may be included.
3
Understand the scope for fighters
The stipend for fighters (rizq al-muqatilah) covers both their own expenses and those of their families (nafqat ayalihim). This ensures the fighter can focus on jihad without financial worry.
The stipend is not just personal; it supports the fighter's household.
4
Analyze the ruling on children
Abu Yusuf states that when a person has a fixed stipend, his children also receive a stipend as dependent, and it does not lapse upon his death. This is an independent grant for the children, not inheritance, because stipends are not inheritable.
This ruling ensures continued support for families of those serving the state.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the allocation of state stipends (rizq) from the Bayt al-Mal. The matn lists four categories: judges (al-qudat), workers (al-ummal, including scribes, appraisers, coast guards, preachers, muhtasibs, muftis, and unpaid teachers), fighters (al-muqatilah, with their own and family expenses), and their children (al-dharari). عطاء (ata'): Annual stipend registered in the diwan for eligible persons; not inheritable.

Forfeiture of Annual Stipend Upon Death Mid-Year
Ruling on loss of annual stipend if recipient dies mid-year

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on forfeiture of the annual stipend when a recipient dies mid-year and explain the reasoning behind the cutoff.

Core Idea
Death before mid-year forfeits the stipend; death after mid-year triggers a recommendation to give it to the heirs.

This passage addresses a specific question about the annual stipend ('ata') from the Bayt al-Mal: if a registered recipient dies during the year, does the stipend get paid to his heirs? The Hanafi jurists clarify that the stipend is a grant for the living fighter or beneficiary, not inherited property. However, a compassionate concession is made if death occurs near the end of the year. Let's examine the ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Read the first sentence: 'And whoever dies in the middle of the year is deprived of the stipend.' Note that 'whoever dies' refers to those entitled to a share from the Bayt al-Mal, such as fighters registered in the diwan.
The ruling is specific to the annual stipend, not monthly salary.
2
Understand the time cutoff
The phrase 'in the middle of the year' is the key condition. The commentators explain that if death occurs before the midpoint, the stipend is forfeited entirely; if after, it is recommended to give it to the heirs.
The cutoff is not a fixed date but relative to the annual cycle.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Fakhr al-Islam in 'Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir' states that the specification of mid-year is because at the end of the year it is recommended to give the stipend to the heirs. Before mid-year, nothing is given except to the extent of need (ghanā').
The reason is based on both legal principle (non-inheritance) and consideration for the family.
4
Clarify the nature of the stipend
Al-Fath explains that 'ata' is an annual registration in the diwan, unlike the monthly salary (jamakiyya). The stipend is not a regular wage but an annual grant.
This distinction affects how forfeiture is treated.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a fighter registered for a 1000 dirham annual stipend. If he dies in the 8th month, the ruling says the stipend is forfeited (for the year). If he dies in the 11th month, it is recommended to give the amount to his heirs.
The 'recommendation' is not obligatory but a sunnah gesture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن مات في نصف السنة حرم من العطاء. [٤] قوله : ومن مات ؛ أي ممن ذكر ممن له سهم من بيت المال. [0] قوله : في نصف السنة ؛ قال فخرُ الإسلام في «شرح الجامع الصغير»: إنما خص نصف السنة ؛ لأنّ عند آخرها يستحب أن يصرف ذلك إلى ورثته، فأما قبل ذلك فلا إلا على قدر غنائه. [٦] قوله : حرم من العطاء ؛ قال في «الفتح» : هو ما يثبت في الديوان باسم كلّ من ذكرنا من المقاتلة وغيرهم، وهو كالجامكية في عرفنا إلا أنها شهرية، والعطاء سنوي. انتهى. وقيد بنصف السنة ؛ لأنه لو مات في آخر السنة يستحب صرف ذلك إلى قريبه. ينظر : «مجمع الأنهر».
And whoever dies in the middle of the year is deprived of the stipend. [4] His saying: 'And whoever dies' – i.e., from among those mentioned who have a share in the state treasury. [0] His saying: 'in the middle of the year' – Fakhr al-Islam said in 'Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir': He specified the middle of the year because at its end it is recommended to give it to his heirs, but before that it is not given except to the extent of his need. [6] His saying: 'deprived of the stipend' – he said in 'al-Fath': It is what is registered in the diwan in the name of each of those we mentioned from fighters and others, and it is like the monthly salary in our custom except that it is monthly, but the stipend is annual. End. And it is conditioned to the middle of the year because if he dies at the end of the year, it is recommended to give it to his relative. See: 'Majma' al-Anhur'.

Commentary
This passage states a rule regarding the annual stipend (ata') from the Bayt al-Mal: if a recipient dies in the middle of the year, he forfeits the stipend for that year. The commentator explains that 'middle of the year' is specified because if death occurs near the end of the year, it is recommended (mustahab) to give the stipend to his heirs. This is based on Fakhr al-Islam's opinion in 'Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir' and the ruling in 'al-Fath'. The stipend is an annual grant registered in the government registry, similar to a monthly salary (jamakiyya) but paid yearly. Thus, the rule balances the principle that the stipend is not inherited with the recommendation to support the family if the recipient dies late in the year.

Key Terms
نصف السنة (nisf al-sanah): The middle of the year; used as a cutoff: if death occurs before this point, stipend is forfeited; if after, it is recommended to give it to heirs.
عطاء (ata'): Annual stipend registered in the diwan for eligible persons; not monthly salary.

Key Takeaway

Killing an Apostate Without Invitation to Islam
Inter-madhhab dispute on the requirement to invite an apostate before execution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing an apostate before presenting Islam, explain the Shafi'i counterargument, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence of the dispute).

Core Idea
Killing an apostate without invitation is makruh tanzih, not haram, because the dawah has already reached him.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on executing an apostate without first inviting him to return to Islam. The key question is whether presenting Islam (ard al-Islam) is obligatory or merely recommended. The Hanafi school holds it is mustahabb, so killing before invitation is disliked but incurs no penalty. The Shafi'i view, according to one narration, requires a three-day respite. Understanding the reasoning behind each position will sharpen your ability to assess divergent legal proofs.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: killing an apostate before presenting Islam is makruh tanzih (slightly disliked) and carries no liability for blood money or expiation. The killer merely abandoned a recommended act.
The base text phrase 'without compensation' (bila daman) is key.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
According to a narration of Imam al-Shafi'i, the imam must give the apostate three days to repent, and killing before that is prohibited.
The Hanafi commentary notes that the sounder Shafi'i view is immediate execution if repentance does not occur.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i justification (for the three-day delay) is that apostasy may stem from doubt, so a period for reflection is needed to allow the apostate to reconsider.
This is a rational argument (dalil aqli) about human psychology.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the fame and spread of Islam's truth is sufficient; the apostate already received the dawah. Therefore, no waiting period is necessary, though if he requests a delay, it is recommended to grant it to remove any pretext.
The Hanafi school gives weight to the universality of the prophetic message.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, execution can be immediate (though disliked); under the Shafi'i narration, it must be delayed by three days.
This difference affects the timing of implementation of the hadd penalty.

And killing him before presenting [Islam] is leaving the recommended [act] without compensation. Because he is deserving of death due to apostasy. And according to al-Shafi'i, the Imam must give him three days' respite, and it is not permissible to kill him before that. It is mentioned in al-Bada'i' and Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir and others: If he comes with the two testimonies but does not dissociate [from his previous religion], he is not judged to be Muslim until he dissociates from the religion he returned to and states that he has entered Islam; because it is possible that he dissociated from Judaism and entered Christianity. And Ibn al-Humam mentioned in his treatise al-Musayarah: The condition of dissociation is for the application of Islamic rulings upon him, not for the confirmation of faith between him and Allah. For if he believes in the universality of the message and testifies, he is a believer in the sight of Allah at that moment. [1] His statement: 'and killing him' means: if the apostate is killed before presenting Islam to him, he [the killer] has abandoned the recommended act and committed something makruh tanzihan; because presenting is recommended, not obligatory. It is only obligatory if the call has not reached him. Its analogy is the call at the time of fighting, as previously mentioned. [2] His statement: 'without compensation' means: the killer is not liable for blood money or expiation; because he killed someone who is deserving of death, so his killing is permissible. And there is no [penalty] for committing a permissible act. At most, he has abandoned the recommended act. [3] His statement: 'and according to al-Shafi'i...' and this is one narration from him; the sound view of his school is that if he repents immediately [he is spared], otherwise he is killed. The justification for this narration is that the apostasy of a Muslim is apparently due to a doubt, so a period is necessary to allow for reflection. The response: The arrival of the call to him and the fame of the truth of the religion of Islam is sufficient, so there is no need for respite. Yes, if he requests a respite, it is recommended to grant it in order to remove the argument [against him].
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with an inter-madhhab dispute. The author, commenting on the base text, explains that killing an apostate before inviting him to Islam is makruh tanzihan (disliked but not sinful) and carries no diyah or kaffarah for the killer. The killer has merely abandoned a mustahabb (recommended) act, because presenting Islam to the apostate is recommended, not obligatory. The exception is if the apostate never received the message of Islam, in which case presenting Islam becomes obligatory. The author then contrasts the Shafi'i position: according to one narration, the Imam must give the apostate three days to repent, and killing him before that is unlawful. The Hanafi response is that the apostate's knowledge of Islam's truth (due to the dawah having reached him) makes a waiting period unnecessary, though if he requests a delay, it is recommended to grant it to remove any pretext.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): A person who has left Islam and has not repented.
عرض الإسلام (ard al-Islam): Presenting the option of Islam to the apostate, inviting him to return to it before execution.
ضمان (daman): Liability for compensation, such as blood money (diyah) or expiation (kaffarah) for wrongful killing.

D) It is mubah with no dislike

Key Takeaway
Killing an apostate before inviting him to Islam is makruh tanzih, not sinful, because the apostate is already deserving of death and the dawah has reached him; no compensation is due.

The Apostate's Property: Suspended Ownership or Continuous?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the apostate's property: suspended vs. continuous ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the apostate's ownership and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) on his transactions.

Core Idea
An apostate's ownership is suspended according to Abu Hanifa, but remains intact according to his two disciples.

When a Muslim apostatizes, what happens to his property? Does he immediately lose ownership, or does it remain? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that ownership ceases in a suspended manner, while his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, argue that ownership continues until death or definitive separation. The practical difference (thamarah) is crucial for the validity of any transactions the apostate might perform during this period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Central Issue
Pinpoint the exact legal question: what is the status of the apostate's ownership of his property after apostasy and before his return, death, or joining the abode of war?
The issue does not concern what happens after death or return—both sides agree ownership either reverts or finalizes.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Abu Hanifa holds that ownership ceases 'suspended' (zawaal mawquf)—neither fully present nor fully gone, pending the apostate's fate. If he returns to Islam, ownership reinstates; if he dies or is killed or joins dar al-harb with a judicial ruling, the cessation is final and certain legal consequences follow (e.g., mudabbar and umm walad become free, debts become due).
This suspension is unique to Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn reject it.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that ownership does not cease at all until the apostate is actually killed or dies. Until that moment, he remains a legally responsible person (mukallaf) with needs, so his ownership continues like any other person under a death sentence (e.g., one sentenced to qisas or stoning).
They analogize the apostate to a person under qisas, not to a warring person.
4
Analyze the Proofs (Dalil Aqli)
Abu Hanifa's proof: The apostate is a 'harbi' (warring person) subdued under the authority of the Islamic state. Being destined for execution justifies cessation of ownership. However, since he may return to Islam, the cessation is suspended. The Sahibayn's proof: The apostate remains a mukallaf with ongoing legal capacity; ownership is tied to need and remains until actual death.
Both proofs are based on analogy (qiyas), but the analogies differ: harbi vs. mukallaf under qisas.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
The practical difference appears in the apostate's transactions (e.g., selling property, freeing slaves). According to Abu Hanifa, any transactions are suspended (mawquf)—if he returns to Islam, they become valid; if he dies, they become void. According to the Sahibayn, transactions are valid and immediately binding until his death.
This affects third parties who deal with the apostate.

[٢]قوله : وإن مات ؛ يعني إن مات المرتد في دار الإسلام، أو قتله الإمام على ردّته، أو التحق بدار الحرب، وحكم باللحاق زال ملكه. من أما في الصورتين الأوليين فظاهر. وأما في الصورة الثالثة ؛ فلأنه لما التحق بدار الحرب وحكم الحاكم بلحاقه صار أهل الحرب، وهم أموات في حق أحكام الإسلام ؛ لانقطاع ولاية الإلزام كانقطاعها عن الموتى، فصار اللحاق كالموت ، وإنّما قيد بالحكم باللحاق ؛ لأنه لا يستقر لحاقه إلا بقضاء القاضي ؛ لاحتمال العود إلينا ، فلما حكم الحاكم بلحوقه سقط اعتباره ، كذا في «الهداية».
And his ownership over his property ceases in a suspended manner. If he becomes Muslim, it returns. And if he dies, or is killed, or joins the abode of war, and it is judged [so], his mudabbar and umm walad become free, and his debt becomes due. [1] His statement: 'and his ownership ceases': means the apostate's ownership of all that he owned in his Islam ceases in a suspended cessation. It is stated in al-Hidayah: They said: This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), his ownership does not cease; because he is a legally responsible person in need, so until he is killed his ownership remains, like the one sentenced to stoning or qisas. The dispute is only in the cessation due to these three [events] being limited to the present moment according to the two, and being based on the time of occurrence of apostasy according to him (Abu Hanifa). And the practical consequence appears in his transactions: according to the two, they are valid before [return to] Islam; according to him, they are suspended due to the suspension of his properties. [2] His statement: 'and if he dies': meaning if the apostate dies in the abode of Islam, or the Imam kills him for his apostasy, or he joins the abode of war and the judge rules concerning his joining, his ownership ceases. As for the first two scenarios, it is obvious. As for the third scenario: because when he joins the abode of war and the judge rules concerning his joining, he becomes [like] the people of war, and they are like dead with respect to the rulings of Islam, due to the cutting off of the authority of obligation just as it is cut off from the dead. So joining is like death. And it is only restricted by the judge's ruling because his joining is not established except by the judge's decision, due to the possibility of his return to us. So when the judge rules concerning his joining, its consideration falls away. As in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ownership of an apostate's property. The base text states that the apostate's ownership ceases in a suspended manner: if he returns to Islam, it reverts; if he dies, is killed, or joins dar al-harb and the judge rules accordingly, his mudabbar and umm walad become free and his debt becomes due. The commentary clarifies that this is the position of Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that ownership does not cease because the apostate remains a legally responsible person until execution, like a person sentenced to stoning. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the apostate is effectively a warring person subject to our authority, and his ownership should cease; however, because his return to Islam is possible, we suspend the issue. The practical difference (thamarah) lies in the apostate's transactions: according to the Sahibayn, any transactions he performs are valid; according to Abu Hanifa, they are suspended pending the outcome. The commentary then explains the three scenarios that finalize the cessation: death, execution, or joining dar al-harb with a judicial ruling. The last scenario is equated to death due to the severance of the authority of Islamic law over him.

Key Terms
زوال ملك موقوف (zawaal milk mawquf): The cessation of ownership that is suspended pending the apostate's return to Islam or his death/flight. If he returns, ownership reinstates; otherwise, it is finalized.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death (tadbir). In this context, if the apostate's ownership is finalized, the mudabbar becomes free.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears a child to her master, becoming free upon his death. Here, she becomes free when the apostate's ownership ceases definitively.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) They are valid and binding.
B) They are void ab initio.
C) They are suspended (mawquf) pending the outcome.
D) They are valid only if the apostate returns to Islam.

Key Takeaway
The apostate's property status is a matter of intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa holds suspended cessation, the Sahibayn hold continuous ownership — the practical consequence affects the validity of his transactions.

The Apostate's Ownership: Suspended Cessation or No Cessation?
States the ruling and intra-Hanafi proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on whether the apostate's ownership ceases, and explain the qiyas argument that underlies Abu Hanifa's view.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: ownership ceases suspendedly; Sahibayn: ownership remains—the difference is whether the apostate is analogized to a captive harbi or a convict.

This lesson examines the foundational intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the legal status of an apostate's property. The passage first states the ruling as a suspended cessation, then immediately reveals the disagreement: Abu Hanifa argues via qiyas with a captive harbi, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) reject this and treat the apostate like any other legally responsible person. Understanding this dispute is key to appreciating the Hanafi school's internal dynamism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Read the opening sentence: the apostate's ownership ceases 'suspendedly' (zawaal mawquuf). Note the three events that make it final and the consequences for dependents and debts.
This is the summary statement; the details come next.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The commentary cites al-Hidayah: Abu Hanifa says ownership ceases suspendedly; the two said (Sahibayn) say it does not cease at all. Identify each position.
Sahibayn are Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's qiyas
Abu Hanifa analogizes the apostate to a captive harbi (asl) whose ownership is lost. The illa is being a 'subdued enemy combatant' (harbi maqhur). The apostate is far', and the hukm is cessation, but suspended because return is hoped for.
Use the qiyas panel to map the structure.
4
Understand the thamarah (practical effect)
The passage from al-Bada'i' explains: according to Sahibayn, the apostate's transactions during apostasy are valid upon return; according to Abu Hanifa, they are suspended. Note why this matters for daily transactions.
This is the real-world consequence of the theoretical difference.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
Although Abu Hanifa's view is presented first, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Sahibayn: ownership does not cease during apostasy. The passage's structure and later practice confirm this.
Zahir al-Riwayah supports Sahibayn.

وعند أبي يوسف : المعتبر كونه وارثاً حال الحكم باللحاق.
وثمرة هذا الاختلاف أنه لو كان له وارث عند اللحاق فارتد أو مات عند الحكم به لم يرث عند أبي يوسف له خلافا لمحمد الله له ، ولو كان له قريب كافر عند لحاقه فأسلم (۲) قبل الحكم به ورث عند أبي يوسف الله خلافاً لمحمد الله له ، كذا في حواشي الهداية)) () [٣] قوله : وكسب ردّته ؛ أي ما كسبه حال ارتداده وأما ما كسبه في كفره غير ارتداده بأن كان كافراً أصليّاً وكسب مالاً ثمّ أسلم ثم ارتد فمات أو قتل فهو لورثته المسلمين ؛ لأن الذي كسبه طول عمره حال كفره صار ملكاً لـه حين إسلامه فصار ككسب الإسلام، كذا ذكره يوسف جلبي في منهيات «ذخيرة العقبى»(۳) أخـذا مـن شروح «الهداية» وغيرها. Book of Jihad / Chapter of the Apostate. / And his ownership of his property ceases suspendedly; if he embraces Islam it returns; and if he dies, or is killed, or joins Dar al-Harb, and it is judged [that he joined], his mudabbar and umm walad are freed, and any debt upon him becomes due. / [2] (And his ownership of his property ceases suspendedly; if he embraces Islam it returns; and if he dies, or is killed, or joins Dar al-Harb, and it is judged that he joined, his mudabbar and umm walad are freed, and any debt upon him becomes due) – for he is in the ruling of a dead person, so a deferred debt becomes due upon the death of the debtor. / [1] His statement: 'and his ownership ceases' – meaning the ownership of the apostate over all that he owned in his Islam ceases, a suspended cessation. It is said in al-Hidayah: They said: this is according to Abu Hanifa; and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): his ownership does not cease, because he is a legally responsible person in need, so until he is killed his ownership remains like a person sentenced to stoning or retaliation. And for him (Abu Hanifa): He is a war captive subdued under our control until he is killed, and there is no killing except by weapons, so this necessitates the cessation of his ownership and his property-ownership; however, he is called to Islam by compulsion, and his return is hoped for, so we suspend his affair. If he embraces Islam, this occurrence is considered as if it had not been in respect of this ruling, and he becomes as if he had never ceased to be Muslim, and the cause (apostasy) does not take effect. End. And in al-Bada'i': There is no disagreement that if he embraces Islam his properties remain his ownership, and that if he dies, or is killed, or joins [Dar al-Harb] his ownership ceases. The disagreement is only about the cessation by these three [events]: according to the two (Sahibayn) it is limited to the time of the event, and according to him (Abu Hanifa) it goes back to the time of apostasy. The practical effect appears in his transactions: according to the two they are valid before embracing Islam, and according to him they are suspended pending his ownership. / [2] His statement: 'or dies' – meaning if the apostate dies in Dar al-Islam, or the Imam kills him for his apostasy, or he joins Dar al-Harb and judgment of joining is passed, his ownership ceases. / As for the first two cases, it is obvious. / As for the third case: because when he joins Dar al-Harb and the judge passes judgment on his joining, he becomes one of the people of war, and they are like dead in respect of the rulings of Islam, because the authority of enforcement is cut off like it is cut off from the dead. So joining is like death. It is restricted to judgment of joining because his joining is not established except by the judge's ruling, due to the possibility of his returning to us. When the judge rules on his joining, consideration of that possibility falls away. Thus in al-Hidayah. / (1) al-Hidayah (2:165). (2) Bada'i' al-Sana'i' (7:136). (3) al-Hidayah (2:166). / 482 / 'Umda al-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya / And the earnings of his Islam (go) to his Muslim heir, and the earnings of his apostasy (go) to the state treasury. And according to al-Shafi'i, his property remains suspended as it was. / (And the earnings of his Islam) to his Muslim heir, and the earnings of his apostasy (go) to the state treasury – this is according to Abu Hanifa; and according to the two, both are for his Muslim heir. / [1] His statement: 'his property remains' – i.e., in the case of joining Dar al-Harb, because his joining is a type of absence, so it resembles absence in Dar al-Islam, and absence has no effect, even if cutoff, on the cessation of ownership as they stated in the chapter of the lost person. Our evidence has already been mentioned, and by it the answer to this proof is known. / [2] His statement: 'earnings of his Islam' – i.e., after his death, actual or legal, his property is divided among his heirs. So what he earned during his Islam goes to his Muslim heir. The heir is considered at the time of joining in the apparent narration, which is the saying of Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), because joining is the cause, and the judgment [of the judge] establishes the cause to cut off the possibility of return. / According to Abu Yusuf: the consideration is the heir at the time of judgment of the joining. / The fruit of this difference: if he had an heir at the time of joining who apostatized or died at the time of judgment, he does not inherit according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad; and if he had a non-Muslim relative at the time of joining who embraced Islam before the judgment, he inherits according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. Thus in the marginalia of al-Hidayah. / [3] His statement: 'earnings of his apostasy' – i.e., what he earned during his apostasy. As for what he earned in his disbelief other than his apostasy, such as if he was an original disbeliever and earned wealth, then embraced Islam, then apostatized and died or was killed, it goes to his Muslim heirs, because what he earned throughout his life during his disbelief became his ownership at the time of his Islam, so it becomes like earnings of Islam. Thus mentioned by Yusuf Jilabi in the 'Munyat' of 'Dhakhirat al-'Uqba', taken from the commentaries of al-Hidayah and others. / [4] This – i.e., that the earnings of Islam are for the Muslim heirs and the earnings of apostasy are fay' (state treasury).
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on the apostate (murtaad) in the Book of Jihad explains the ruling on an apostate's property. It is a legal discussion with multiple layers of ikhtilaf (disagreement) within the Hanafi school and with Imam al-Shafi'i. The passage begins by stating the ruling that the apostate's ownership of his property ceases in a suspended manner (zawaal mawquuf). If he returns to Islam, his ownership is restored as if the apostasy never happened. If he dies, is executed for apostasy, or joins a hostile territory (Dar al-Harb) with a judge's ruling to that effect, then his ownership ceases permanently. The author then explains that this suspended cessation is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. The two companions (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) hold that ownership does not cease at all during the apostate's life, as he remains a legally responsible person (mukallaf) in need of property. They analogize him to a person sentenced to death by stoning or retaliation, who still retains ownership until death. Abu Hanifa's proof is that the apostate is a war captive (harbi) subdued under Muslim authority, and his situation necessitates the cessation of ownership and property-ownership. However, since he is invited to Islam and his return is hoped for, the cessation is made suspended. If he returns, the interim is treated as if it never occurred. The practical consequence (thamarah) of this difference appears in the apostate's transactions: according to Sahibayn, transactions during apostasy are valid upon return to Islam; according to Abu Hanifa, they are suspended pending the fate of his ownership.

The passage then clarifies the three scenarios that cause final loss of ownership: death, execution, or joining Dar al-Harb. Joining Dar al-Harb is treated like death because those in Dar al-Harb are considered "dead" in the rulings of Islam due to the impossibility of enforcing Islamic law over them. The judge's ruling is required because the mere act of joining might be reversible; once the judge rules, the possibility of return is cut off.

Next, the passage discusses the distribution of the apostate's earnings. According to Abu Hanifa, earnings during his Islam go to his Muslim heir, and earnings during his apostasy go to the state treasury (fay'). According to Sahibayn, both categories go to the Muslim heir. The text also notes Imam al-Shafi'i's view that the property remains suspended even after joining Dar al-Harb, comparing it to an extended absence (ghaybah). The Hanafi response relies on the difference between absence within Dar al-Islam (where ownership persists) and joining a hostile territory (where the authority of Islamic law is severed).

Finally, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the moment at which the heir is determined for inheritance. Imam Muhammad considers the time of joining (al-haaq) as the determining moment, because joining is the cause and the judge's ruling merely confirms it. Abu Yusuf considers the time of the judge's ruling (al-hukm bil-lahaq) as the determining moment, since the joining is not definitively established until the ruling. The practical difference (thamarah) is illustrated: if the apostate had an heir at the time of joining who apostatized or died before the ruling, that heir does not inherit according to Abu Yusuf but does according to Imam Muhammad. Conversely, if a non-Muslim relative becomes Muslim after joining but before the ruling, that relative inherits according to Abu Yusuf but not according to Imam Muhammad.

The passage also clarifies that if a person was originally a disbeliever, earned wealth, then became Muslim, then apostatized and died or was killed, all his wealth (including what he earned as a disbeliever) goes to his Muslim heirs. This is because his Islam made him owner of all his prior earnings, so they are treated like earnings of Islam.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtaad): A person who leaves Islam after having been a Muslim. In this passage, the rulings concern his property.
زوال موقوف (zawaal mawquuf): Suspended cessation; ownership is considered to have ceased but can revert upon return to Islam.
فيء (fay'): Property that becomes state treasury, e.g., from enemies. In this context, the apostate's property becomes fay' upon final loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does the apostate's ownership cease?
A) Because the apostate is a captive harbi subdued under Muslim control.
B) Because the apostate is legally dead as soon as he apostatizes.
C) Because the apostate's dependents need the property.
D) Because the Quran explicitly states the property is lost.

Key Takeaway
The apostate's ownership is a matter of ikhtilaf; the mufta bihi (Sahibayn) holds that it does not cease, providing legal stability for transactions.

Jihad — Armed Conflict Rules
intermediate
10 min
The Final Loss: Death, Execution, or Joining Dar al-Harb
Details the three final events and the inter-madhhab dispute with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why joining Dar al-Harb is legally equivalent to death, and contrast the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection that it is like prolonged absence.

Core Idea
Joining Dar al-Harb with a judge's ruling severs Islamic authority, making the apostate like dead—his property becomes fay'.

This lesson focuses on the three events that cause the final loss of the apostate's ownership: death, execution, or joining Dar al-Harb. The passage explains why joining Dar al-Harb is analogized to death, addresses the Shafi'i objection that it is merely a form of absence, and provides the Hanafi rebuttal based on the severance of legal authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three events
The passage states: 'if he dies, or is killed, or joins Dar al-Harb and it is judged [that he joined], his mudabbar and umm walad are freed, and any debt upon him becomes due.' Note that the third event requires a judge's ruling.
The first two are clear; the third is debated.
2
Understand why joining Dar al-Harb is like death
The commentator explains: joining Dar al-Harb cuts off the authority of Islamic law (wilayat al-ilzam), similar to death. The judge's ruling is needed because the apostate might return; once ruled, the possibility is eliminated.
This is a qiyas: the apostate becomes like a harbi (enemy) who is beyond Islamic jurisdiction.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that joining Dar al-Harb is like prolonged absence (ghaybah), which does not affect ownership, as established in the chapter of the missing person (al-mafquud).
This is an important inter-madhhab ikhtilaf.
4
Trace the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes between absence within Dar al-Islam (where law still applies) and joining a hostile territory (where law cannot be enforced). The apostate's voluntary choice to join the enemy severs his connection to Islamic legal authority permanently.
The key difference: absence in Dar al-Islam allows potential legal enforcement; joining Dar al-Harb does not.
5
Determine the thamarah
Practical difference: under Hanafi law, once the judge rules, the property is immediately distributed as fay' and to heirs. Under Shafi'i law, the property remains suspended until the apostate's death or return.
This affects the timing of estate distribution.

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on the apostate (murtaad) in the Book of Jihad explains the ruling on an apostate's property. It is a legal discussion with multiple layers of ikhtilaf (disagreement) within the Hanafi school and with Imam al-Shafi'i. The passage begins by stating the ruling that the apostate's ownership of his property ceases in a suspended manner (zawaal mawquuf). If he returns to Islam, his ownership is restored as if the apostasy never happened. If he dies, is executed for apostasy, or joins a hostile territory (Dar al-Harb) with a judge's ruling to that effect, then his ownership ceases permanently. The author then explains that this suspended cessation is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. The two companions (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) hold that ownership does not cease at all during the apostate's life, as he remains a legally responsible person (mukallaf) in need of property. They analogize him to a person sentenced to death by stoning or retaliation, who still retains ownership until death. Abu Hanifa's proof is that the apostate is a war captive (harbi) subdued under Muslim authority, and his situation necessitates the cessation of ownership and property-ownership. However, since he is invited to Islam and his return is hoped for, the cessation is made suspended. If he returns, the interim is treated as if it never occurred. The practical consequence (thamarah) of this difference appears in the apostate's transactions: according to Sahibayn, transactions during apostasy are valid upon return to Islam; according to Abu Hanifa, they are suspended pending the fate of his ownership.

The passage then clarifies the three scenarios that cause final loss of ownership: death, execution, or joining Dar al-Harb. Joining Dar al-Harb is treated like death because those in Dar al-Harb are considered "dead" in the rulings of Islam due to the impossibility of enforcing Islamic law over them. The judge's ruling is required because the mere act of joining might be reversible; once the judge rules, the possibility of return is cut off.

Next, the passage discusses the distribution of the apostate's earnings. According to Abu Hanifa, earnings during his Islam go to his Muslim heir, and earnings during his apostasy go to the state treasury (fay'). According to Sahibayn, both categories go to the Muslim heir. The text also notes Imam al-Shafi'i's view that the property remains suspended even after joining Dar al-Harb, comparing it to an extended absence (ghaybah). The Hanafi response relies on the difference between absence within Dar al-Islam (where ownership persists) and joining a hostile territory (where the authority of Islamic law is severed).

Finally, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the moment at which the heir is determined for inheritance. Imam Muhammad considers the time of joining (al-haaq) as the determining moment, because joining is the cause and the judge's ruling merely confirms it. Abu Yusuf considers the time of the judge's ruling (al-hukm bil-lahaq) as the determining moment, since the joining is not definitively established until the ruling. The practical difference (thamarah) is illustrated: if the apostate had an heir at the time of joining who apostatized or died before the ruling, that heir does not inherit according to Abu Yusuf but does according to Imam Muhammad. Conversely, if a non-Muslim relative becomes Muslim after joining but before the ruling, that relative inherits according to Abu Yusuf but not according to Imam Muhammad.

The passage also clarifies that if a person was originally a disbeliever, earned wealth, then became Muslim, then apostatized and died or was killed, all his wealth (including what he earned as a disbeliever) goes to his Muslim heirs. This is because his Islam made him owner of all his prior earnings, so they are treated like earnings of Islam.

Key Terms
دار الحرب (Dar al-Harb): Territory under non-Muslim rule that is in a state of war or potential war with Muslims. Joining it with a judge's ruling severs Islamic legal authority over the apostate.
حكم باللحاق (hukm bil-lahaq): Judge's ruling that the apostate has joined Dar al-Harb. Required because the factual joining might not be definitive; the ruling eliminates the possibility of return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a judge's ruling required for the third event (joining Dar al-Harb) to cause final loss of ownership?
A) Because the apostate might have been forced to join.
B) Because the apostate might return to Islam before the ruling.
C) Because the judge's ruling is needed to establish the fact securely and eliminate the possibility of return.
D) Because the Shafi'i objection requires a ruling to override suspension.

Key Takeaway
Joining Dar al-Harb with a judge's ruling legally transforms the apostate into a dead person for property purposes; property becomes fay'.

Why the Apostate's Marriage and Slaughter Are Invalid — Defining Milla
Ruling with objection and answer on apostate's marriage and slaughter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the apostate's marriage and slaughter, explain the definition of milla, and apply it to distinguish between valid and invalid acts.

Core Idea
An apostate's marriage and slaughter are invalid because they lack a valid religious community (milla).

This passage clarifies why the Hanafi school rules that a man who apostatizes from Islam cannot have a valid marriage or perform ritual slaughter. The key concept is milla — the religious community that recognizes marriage as a lasting institution. We will address an important objection and see how the Hanafis define milla to include Jews, Christians, Magians, and polytheists in limited contexts, but exclude the apostate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and its Basis
The matn states that the apostate's marriage and slaughter are invalid. The reason: they depend on the existence of milla, and the apostate has no milla.
The ruling is clear: batil (invalid).
2
Understand the Objection
An objector argues: if milla means Islam, then the marriage and slaughter of People of the Book would be invalid (but they are valid); if milla means any divine religion, then marriage of Magians and polytheists among themselves would be invalid (but it is valid). Thus the reasoning seems flawed.
The objection targets the coherence of the definition.
3
Analyze the Answer
The answer redefines milla as a faith that recognizes marriage in a way that inheritance can occur and procreation is attained. Apostates do not meet this because the male apostate is killed and the female imprisoned, so no lasting marital relationship is possible.
Milla is not simply Islam or any divine law, but a religion that sustains marriage.
4
Apply the Definition
Using this definition, marriage and slaughter of People of the Book, Magians, and polytheists among themselves are valid because their religions recognize marriage as a lasting institution. The apostate's marriage is invalid because his condition prevents a lasting union.
The focus is on the social and legal effects of marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١] قوله : وبطل نكاحه وذبحه ؛ لأنهما يعتمدان قيام الملة، ولا ملة للمرتد. وأورد عليه : بأن الملة إن كان المراد بها الإسلام انتقض بنكاح أهل الكتاب وذبائحهم، فإنهما صحيحان مع عدم الإسلام، وإن كان المراد بها الملة السماوية مطلقاً انتقض بنكاح المجوس والمشركين فيما بينهم، فإنّه صحيح ولا ملة سماوية لهم. وأجيب عنه : بأن المراد بالملة ما يتدينون به نكاحاً يقرون عليه، بحيث يجري التوارث بينهما، ويحصل الغرض من النكاح من التوالد والتناسل، والمرتدان ليسا بهذه المثابة ، فإنّ المرتد يقتل والمرتدة تحبس. [٢] قوله : وصح ; أما صحة الاستيلاد ; فلأنه لا يفتقر إلى حقيقة الملك بدليل أنه يصح في جارية الابن ; فلأن يصح من المرتد ،أولى، وصورتُه : أن تجيء أمته بولد فيدعيه يثبت نسبه منه، ويرث ذلك الولد منه مع ورثته، وتصير الجارية أم ولد له، وأما صحة الطلاق ; فلأنه لا يفتقر إلى قيام الولاية الكاملة، بدليل أنه يصح من العبد ; فلأن يصح من المرتد أولى، كذا في «البحر» وغيره. (1) ينظر: «البحر الرائق» (٥ : ١٤٦).
He said: And his marriage and his slaughter are invalid; because they depend on the existence of religious community (milla), and the apostate has no milla. And an objection was raised against him: that if by milla is meant Islam, it is contradicted by the marriage of the People of the Book and their slaughter, for they are valid despite the absence of Islam; and if by milla is meant divine law absolutely, it is contradicted by the marriage of Magians and polytheists among themselves, for it is valid and they have no divine law. And it was answered: that what is meant by milla is what they follow as religion in marriage that they are confirmed upon, such that inheritance occurs between them, and the purpose of marriage, procreation and reproduction, is attained; and the two apostates are not in this state, for the apostate is killed and the apostate woman is imprisoned. He said: and correct; as for the validity of istilad (acknowledging paternity of a slave-concubine's child), it does not require actual ownership, evidenced by its validity in the son's slave-girl; so it is more valid from the apostate. Its scenario: when his slave-girl gives birth and he claims the child, his lineage is established from him, that child inherits from him along with his other heirs, and the slave-girl becomes umm walad for him. As for the validity of divorce, it does not require complete authority, evidenced by its validity from a slave; so it is more valid from the apostate. As mentioned in al-Bahr and others. (1) See: al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (5:146).
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn that states the apostate's marriage and slaughter are invalid, while his divorce and istilad are valid. The commentator first addresses the ruling that marriage and slaughter are invalid, giving the reason that they depend on the existence of a valid religious community (milla), which the apostate lacks. An objection is raised: if milla means Islam, then the marriage and slaughter of People of the Book (Jews and Christians) would be invalid, but they are valid; if milla means any divine religion, then marriage of Magians and polytheists among themselves would be invalid, but it is valid. The answer is that milla here means a religious system that recognizes marriage in a way that inheritance can occur and the purpose of marriage (procreation) is achieved. Apostates do not meet this criterion because the male apostate is to be killed and the female imprisoned, so no lasting marital relationship is possible. The commentator then explains the validity of istilad (acknowledging paternity of a child from a slave-concubine). Istilad does not require full ownership because it is valid even if the slave-girl is owned by one's son. Therefore, it is valid from the apostate. The scenario is that if the apostate's slave-girl gives birth and he claims the child, the lineage is established, the child inherits, and the slave-girl becomes umm walad (mother of the master's child). Similarly, divorce is valid from the apostate because it does not require complete authority (wilayah kamilah); it is even valid from a slave, so it is more valid from the apostate, who is free. The commentator cites al-Bahr al-Ra'iq as his source.

Key Terms
ملة (milla): The religious community or faith upon which marriage is based; in this context, a faith that acknowledges the validity of marriage with inheritance and procreation.
ذبح (dhabh): Ritual slaughter of an animal for food.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is an apostate's marriage invalid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the apostate is no longer a believer, and valid marriage requires both spouses to be believers.
B) Because marriage depends on a religious community (milla) that recognizes lasting marriage with inheritance and procreation, and the apostate lacks such a milla.
C) Because apostasy is a crime punishable by death, so the apostate cannot maintain a marriage contract.
D) Because the apostate is legally considered dead and all contracts become void.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines milla broadly to include any religion that recognizes marriage as a lasting institution with inheritance and procreation, excluding apostates.

Validity of the Apostate's Istilad and Divorce — Analogical Proof
Ruling with analogical proof for istilad and divorce of an apostate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the apostate's istilad and divorce, and apply the analogical proofs used to derive these rulings.

Core Idea
Istilad and divorce are valid from an apostate because they require less authority than marriage.

While marriage and slaughter are invalid for the apostate, his other actions like acknowledging paternity of a child from his slave-girl (istilad) and divorcing his wife are valid. The Hanafi school uses analogical reasoning (qiyas) to prove these rulings. In this lesson, we will examine the two analogies: istilad is valid from an apostate because it is even valid from a son's slave-girl, and divorce is valid because it is even valid from a slave. This shows that these acts do not require the same degree of authority as marriage.

Guided Path
1
State the Rulings
The matn states that the apostate's istilad (acknowledging paternity of a slave-concubine's child) and divorce (talaq) are valid.
These rulings contrast with the invalidity of marriage and slaughter.
2
Examine the Proof for Istilad
The proof is: istilad does not require full ownership (haqiqat al-milk) because it is valid even when the slave-girl belongs to the son. If it is valid in that case, it is more valid from the apostate, who owns the slave-girl himself.
The analogy: asl = istilad from son's slave-girl, far' = istilad from apostate, illa = absence of full ownership requirement.
3
Examine the Proof for Divorce
The proof is: divorce does not require complete authority (wilayah kamilah) because it is valid from a slave (who lacks full authority). If it is valid from a slave, it is more valid from a free apostate.
The analogy: asl = divorce by a slave, far' = divorce by apostate, illa = absence of complete authority requirement.
4
Understand the Scenario of Istilad
The text describes the scenario: the apostate's slave-girl gives birth, he claims the child. The child's lineage is established, the child inherits from the apostate, and the slave-girl becomes umm walad (mother of the master's child).
This shows the legal effects of valid istilad.
5
Contrast with Marriage
Marriage requires a valid milla and complete authority, which the apostate lacks. Divorce and istilad do not require these, so they remain valid.
The difference lies in the conditions (shurut) required for each act.

Source Text

Paying Debts of an Apostate from His Earnings
Intra-Hanafi dispute on debt repayment priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the priority of debt repayment from an apostate's earnings and explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Earnings during Islam are owned; earnings during apostasy are not — so debts are paid from the former first.

When an apostate dies with debts, from which assets should they be paid? Abu Hanifa distinguishes between property acquired during Islam and during apostasy, while his two disciples treat both equally. This intra-madhhab dispute turns on the apostate's legal capacity to own property (ahliyyat al-milk) and the rights of heirs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of earnings
Look for kash al-Islam (earnings during Islam) and kash al-ridda (earnings during apostasy) in the text. Understand the basis for distinguishing them.
The text presents two arguments (wajh) for Abu Hanifa's position.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's first argument
The first argument states that kash al-Islam is owned and will be inherited, but debt takes precedence over heirs. Kash al-ridda is not owned because apostasy nullifies capacity to own, so it is only used if no other source exists.
Analogized to a dhimmi dying with no heir — his wealth goes to the Muslim treasury but debts are paid first.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's second argument
The second argument says that kash al-Islam is the right of the heirs while kash al-ridda is the apostate's exclusive right, so debt should be paid from the latter first unless it is insufficient.
This argument gives a different priority: kash al-ridda first, then kash al-Islam.
4
Examine the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that both earnings are owned and subject to inheritance, so debts are paid from both equally.
Their reasoning: both types are property (milk) and can be inherited.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah)
Under Abu Hanifa, heirs may only receive kash al-Islam after debts, and kash al-ridda may not be used unless necessary. Under Sahibayn, both are used proportionally.
The dispute affects how much the heirs ultimately inherit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجه الثاني : إن كسب الإسلام ملكه حتى يخلفه الوارث فيه، ومن شرط هذه الخلافة الفراغ عن حق الوارث، فيقدّم الدِّين عليهم. أما كسب الردّة فليس بمملوك له لبطلان أهلية الملك بالردّة عنده، فلا يقضى دينه منه إلا إذا تعذر قضاؤه من محل آخر، فحينئذ يقضى منه كالذمي إذا مات ولا وارث له يكون ماله الجماعة المسلمين، ولو كان عليه دين يقضى منه ، كذا هاهنا. وجه الثالث : إنّ كسب الإسلام حق الورثة ، وكسب الردّة خالص حقه، فكان قضاء الدين منه أولى إلا إذا تعذر إن لم يف به ، فحينئذ تقضى من كسب الإسلام. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : تقضى ديونه من الكسبين؛ لأنهما جميعاً ملكه حتى يجري الإرث فيهما». انتهى.
The second aspect: His earnings during Islam are his property, such that the heir inherits them, and a condition of this inheritance is being free from the right of the heir, so the debt is given precedence over them. As for his earnings during apostasy, they are not owned by him due to the nullification of legal capacity for ownership by apostasy according to him, so his debt is not paid from them unless repayment from another source is impossible, then it is paid from them, like the dhimmi who dies with no heir, his property goes to the community of Muslims, and if he had a debt, it is paid from it; similarly here. The third aspect: The earnings during Islam are the right of the heirs, and the earnings during apostasy are his exclusive right, so repaying debt from them is more appropriate unless it is insufficient, then it is paid from the earnings during Islam. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: His debts are paid from both types of earnings, because both are his property, such that inheritance is executed in both. End.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding from which assets an apostate's debts should be paid. Two arguments (wajh thani and wajh thalith) are presented, likely representing the position of Abu Hanifa. The first argument (wajh thani) distinguishes between kash al-Islam (earnings during Islam) and kash al-ridda (earnings during apostasy). Kash al-Islam is owned by the apostate and will pass to his heirs, but the debt is a prior claim, so it is paid first from this wealth. Kash al-ridda is not considered owned by him because his legal capacity to own (ahliyyat al-milk) is invalidated by apostasy according to Abu Hanifa. Therefore, it is only used for debt repayment if no other source is available, analogous to a dhimmi who dies with no heir: his wealth goes to the Muslim treasury, but if he has a debt, it is paid first. The second argument (wajh thalith) states that kash al-Islam is the right of the heirs, while kash al-ridda is his exclusive right, so it is more appropriate to pay debts from the latter, unless insufficient, then from kash al-Islam. This also represents Abu Hanifa's view. Then the text presents the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn): debts should be paid from both types of earnings indiscriminately, because both are his property and will be inherited. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf where Abu Hanifa holds a distinction, while the Sahibayn treat them equally. The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi (relied upon).

Key Terms
كسب الإسلام (kash al-Islam): Earnings or property acquired by the apostate during his period of Islam.
كسب الردة (kash al-ridda): Earnings or property acquired by the apostate during his period of apostasy.
أهلية الملك (ahliyyat al-milk): Legal capacity to own property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, from which earnings should the debts of an apostate be paid first?
A) From his earnings during apostasy (kash al-ridda)
B) From his earnings during Islam (kash al-Islam)
C) From a combination of both equally
D) Debts are not paid from either

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa distinguishes between kash al-Islam (owned, primary for debt) and kash al-ridda (not owned, secondary), while the Sahibayn treat both as equally owned for debt repayment.

Apostate's Transactions: Suspension vs. Immediate Effect
Intra-Hanafi dispute on apostate transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's rulings on apostate transactions and explain the rational basis for each position.

Core Idea
An apostate's transactions are suspended until death or return to Islam according to Abu Hanifa; Sahibayn see them effective immediately.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah presents a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: are the transactions of an apostate valid, invalid, or suspended? The matn rules that most transactions are suspended (mawquf), but the commentary reveals that this is only Abu Hanifa's view. The Sahibayn hold them effective (nafidh). Understanding this ikhtilaf illuminates how legal capacity (ahliyyah) and ownership (milk) interact in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus points
The passage lists four categories: marriage and slaughter are invalid by consensus; divorce and istilad are valid by consensus; mufawadah is suspended by consensus; the remaining transactions are disputed.
Pay attention to which rulings are agreed upon (ittifaq) and which are contested (ikhtilaf).
2
Locate the point of disagreement
For the remaining transactions (sale, purchase, gift, loan, lease, tadbir, kitabah, will), Abu Hanifa holds them suspended, while the Sahibayn hold them effective.
The dispute is about whether apostasy creates a defect in legal capacity that suspends transactions.
3
Analyze the rational arguments
Sahibayn argue that the apostate is mukallaf, so he has ahliyyah, and his ownership is intact before death, hence transactions are effective. Abu Hanifa counters that the apostate is like a harbi under our control, so his ownership is suspended; his deserving death due to loss of isma creates a defect in ahliyyah.
The core of the ikhtilaf is whether apostasy affects ahliyyah and milk.

Likewise, ownership is present before his death. Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal status of various transactions of an apostate. First, it states the matn: the apostate's mufawadah partnership, sale, purchase, gift, loan (i'arah), lease (ijarah), tadbir (post-death manumission), kitabah (contractual manumission), and will (wasiyyah) are suspended (mawqufah). If he returns to Islam, they become effective; if he dies, is killed, or joins the enemy territory (dar al-harb) and is judged accordingly, they become invalid. Then a parenthetical repeat clarifies the consensus points: marriage and slaughter are invalid by consensus; divorce and istilad are valid by consensus; mufawadah is suspended by consensus; and the remaining transactions (sale, purchase, etc.) are suspended according to Abu Hanifa but effective (nafidh) according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn). The commentator then explains the reasoning. First, he addresses a potential question: since marriage is invalidated by apostasy, how can divorce be valid? The answer is that there are two scenarios where apostasy does not immediately dissolve the marriage: when the wife apostatizes but the husband does not (the marriage is dissolved immediately, so divorce is possible only if the husband apostatizes? Actually, the text gives two scenarios: one where the apostate himself divorces his wife—but the marriage is dissolved by apostasy, so divorce can only occur if it is before the dissolution? The commentator says the sharh (commentary) mentioned two scenarios that show the occurrence of divorce. The text then explains that the suspension of mufawadah is by consensus because mufawadah requires equality between partners in capital, authority, and religion, and there is no equality between a Muslim and an apostate. The suspension of the remaining transactions is only according to Abu Hanifa, not the Sahibayn. The effective view of the Sahibayn is based on the apostate having legal capacity (ahliyyah) because he is addressed by the law (mukallaf); if he lacked capacity, he would not be required to be killed. Additionally, he has ownership (milk) before death. Abu Hanifa's counterargument is that the apostate is like a harbi (enemy combatant) under our control, so his ownership is suspended, and his transactions based on it are suspended. Moreover, his deserving death due to the nullification of his 'isma (legal protection) creates a defect in his legal capacity.

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufawadah): A type of partnership where both partners are equal in capital, authority, and religion.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; a transaction that is neither immediately effective nor invalid, but contingent on a future event (e.g., return to Islam or death).
نافذ (nafidh): Immediately effective and legally binding.
أهلية (ahliyyah): Legal capacity to acquire rights and obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is invalid immediately.
B) It is suspended, depending on his return to Islam or death.
C) It is valid and effective immediately.
D) It is valid only if made with another apostate.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa rules apostate transactions as suspended due to defective legal capacity and ownership; the Sahibayn rule them effective based on intact ahliyyah and milk.
