Suspended Transactions of an Apostate: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on suspension of apostate's transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa suspends the apostate's transactions while the Sahibayn deem them effective, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
An apostate's transactions are suspended — only return to Islam makes them effective; otherwise they become void.

When a Muslim leaves Islam (apostasy), the legal capacity becomes complex. This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute: should the apostate's transactions be suspended or immediately effective? The answer hinges on whether apostasy creates a defect in legal capacity (ahliyyah) and ownership.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling on most transactions of the murtadd (excluding those with consensus).
The passage lists sale, purchase, gift, loan, lease, tadbir, kitaba, will.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold the opposite view: transactions are effective (nafidh).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Sahibayn's reasoning: the murtadd has legal capacity (ahliyyah) because he is addressed by the law (mukhatab) and his ownership remains until death.
Abu Yusuf likens him to a sound person; Muhammad likens him to a sick person whose transactions are still valid.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa argues that the murtadd is a harbi (enemy) subdued under Muslim control, so his ownership is suspended, causing a defect in ahliyyah.
The killing obligation due to apostasy indicates a break in isma (protection).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the murtadd sells something before returning to Islam, Abu Hanifa considers the sale suspended and void if he dies; the Sahibayn consider it effective and binding.

Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of apostasy on various transactions. The base ruling states that the murtadd's transactions (mufawada, sale, purchase, gift, loan, lease, tadbir, kitaba, will) are suspended. If he returns to Islam, they become effective; if he dies, is killed, or joins dar al-harb and the judge rules accordingly, they become void. The commentator clarifies that marriage and dhabh (slaughtering an animal) are void by consensus, divorce and istilad are valid by consensus, and mufawada is suspended by consensus. The remaining transactions are suspended according to Imam Abu Hanifa but effective according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The reasoning: Abu Hanifa argues that the murtadd is a harbi under our control, so his ownership is suspended, causing a defect in his legal capacity (ahliyyah). The Sahibayn argue that his legal capacity remains intact because he is mukhatab (addressed by legal obligations) and his ownership remains until death. Therefore, his transactions should be effective like from a sound person (Abu Yusuf) or like from a sick person (Muhammad). The passage also addresses the concern that divorce from a murtadd might seem contradictory since marriage is dissolved by apostasy, but the commentator explains two scenarios where divorce can still occur: (1) if the apostate divorces his wife while she is in iddah, or (2) if both apostatize together, then divorce and then both return to Islam, the marriage remains intact and divorce takes effect.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): A Muslim who abandons Islam by word or action.
مفاوضة (mufāwaḍah): A type of partnership where two parties are equal in capital, profit, and authority.
موقوف (mawqūf): Suspended pending a condition; here, the transaction is neither immediately valid nor void but depends on the apostate's return to Islam or his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is void immediately.
B) It is valid and effective immediately.
C) It is suspended; if he returns to Islam it becomes effective, otherwise void.
D) It is valid but requires a new offer and acceptance.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between void, valid, and suspended acts for an apostate, with Abu Hanifa ruling that most transactions are suspended until he returns to Islam or dies.

Return of the Apostate: Before and After Judicial Ruling
Ruling on apostate's return and intra-Hanafi debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal consequences of an apostate's return to Islam before versus after the judge's ruling, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of transactions during apostasy.

Core Idea
An apostate's return before the judge's ruling restores him to his original state; after the ruling, wealth is inherited but reclaimable by decree.

This passage from al-Hidayah examines the pivotal role of the judge's ruling (ḥukm) in apostasy cases. It distinguishes the return before ruling (as if no apostasy) from after ruling (wealth with heirs but reclaimable). The commentary also presents an intra-Hanafi debate between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the validity of transactions during apostasy, unpacking the underlying reasoning based on the likelihood of the apostate's return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the base ruling: if the apostate returns as a Muslim before the judge's ruling (ḥukm), it is as if he never apostatized. If after, his wealth is with his heirs, but he can take it back. Note the key distinction: timing relative to the judge's ruling.
The judge's ruling declares the apostate a ḥarbī (enemy combatant), which transforms his property status.
2
Understand the contrast with other capital crimes
The text compares to a fornicator or premeditated murderer who are executed. Their execution is a penalty for a crime, not due to corruption of protection (ʿiṣma). Therefore, their transactions remain valid. In apostasy, the ruling affects the apostate's ʿiṣma, so his transactions are impacted.
This distinction helps clarify why the apostate's case is unique.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute (Sahibayn)
Within the Hanafi school, Abu Yusuf holds that the apostate's transactions during apostasy are valid as from a sound person (because he is likely to return to Islam, like an apostate woman who is not killed). Muhammad holds they are valid as from a sick person (because the apostate is unlikely to return, like a terminally ill patient whose transactions are restricted).
This disagreement is an example of legitimate ikhtilāf based on different presumptions about the apostate's future.
4
Explore the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilāf)
If the apostate makes a sale during apostasy and then returns before the ruling, the sale is void (the base ruling). But if we apply Abu Yusuf's view, the sale is valid even if he later returns? Actually, the base ruling says before ruling it is as if no apostasy, so the sale is void. The dispute applies after the ruling? The text says 'according to them (the two)' referring to the validity of transactions during apostasy before any return. The thamarah: if the apostate does not return, Abu Yusuf says the sale is fully effective; Muhammad says it is effective but limited (like a sick person's transactions).
The judge's ruling does not retroactively affect pending transactions; the dispute is about their initial validity.
5
Examine specific sub-rulings
The commentary details: if the apostate returns before the ruling, his mudabbars and umm walad are not freed, his debts are not forfeited, and he can annul dispositions made by the heir. If after, he cannot reclaim earnings from apostasy (they become fay'), and manumissions executed by the judge remain effective, but he retains walā'.
These nuanced rules illustrate how the ḥukm transforms legal relationships.

If he comes as a Muslim before the ruling, it is as if he never apostatized. And if he comes after it, his wealth is with his heirs; he takes it.

According to Muhammad, they are valid as from a sick person, because one who adopts a creed, especially turning away from what he was raised on, rarely abandons it, leading to execution apparently, unlike an apostate woman who is not killed.'
End.
[2] His saying: 'it is as if he never apostatized'; so his mudabbar and umm walad are not freed, nor are his debts forfeited, and he has the right to annul what the heir disposed of. This is in al-Bahr.
[3] His saying: 'his wealth is with his heirs'; there is an indication that he has no right to what he finds from the earnings of his apostasy, because it has become fay' (booty), just as the harbi's wealth is not returned to him if he comes as a Muslim. This is in al-Nahr. Also, if his wealth perishes or the heir removes it from his ownership, he does not take it, unless it remains in its original state due to the validity of the ruling. Also, his mudabbarun and ummahat awlad do not return to slavery, and he cannot own them because the ruling of their manumission was valid, and manumission after its effectiveness cannot be rescinded; however, he has their wala' (patronage). This is in al-Fath.
[4] His saying: 'he takes it'; i.e., he may take it, but by judicial decree or mutual consent, because by the judge's ruling of his joining (dar al-harb), the wealth became the property of his heirs, so it does not return to him except by decree or consent. An analogy is if God returns...
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on an apostate who returns to Islam either before or after the judge's ruling (hukm) that he has joined dar al-harb. The base ruling: if he returns before the ruling, it is as if he never apostatized; if he returns after the ruling, his wealth that was inherited by his heirs can be reclaimed by him, but only through a judicial decree or mutual consent. The commentator distinguishes this from a fornicator or intentional killer who are also liable to execution, because their execution is a penalty for a crime, not due to a corruption of isma (protection), so their transactions remain effective. The passage also includes a further intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Yusuf, the apostate's transactions during apostasy are valid like from a sound person, because he is likely to return; according to Muhammad, they are valid like from a sick person, because the apostate is unlikely to return. The commentary then explains the consequences: if he returns before the ruling, his mudabbars and umm walad are not freed, his debts are not voided, and he can annul dispositions made by the heir. If he returns after the ruling, he cannot claim the earnings from his apostasy period (they become fay'), and the manumissions that were executed by the judge remain effective, though he retains wala'. The phrase 'he takes it' means he can reclaim his original wealth, but only with a judge's decree or the heirs' consent.

Key Terms
الحكم (al-ḥukm): The judge's ruling that the apostate has joined dar al-harb, which changes the status of his wealth.
فَيْء (fay'): Spoils of war taken from non-Muslims without fighting; here, the apostate's earnings during apostasy become fay' and not returned to him.
ولاء (walā'): Patronage right that remains with the former owner after manumission; here, the apostate retains wala' over his freed slaves even after the judge's ruling.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave promised freedom after the master's death; the judge's ruling frees him if the apostate is executed.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who bore her master's child; she becomes free upon the master's death. Here, the judge frees her after the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The mudabbar returns to slavery.
B) The mudabbar remains free, and the apostate has no rights.
C) The mudabbar remains free, but the apostate retains wala' (patronage).
D) The mudabbar remains free, and the apostate can reclaim ownership.

Key Takeaway
The timing of the apostate's return relative to the judge's ruling determines the fate of his wealth and transactions, with a nuanced intra-Hanafi difference on transaction validity.

Male Apostate Returning to Islam: Effect on Property and Transactions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on apostate's property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a male apostate who returns to Islam before or after judgment, and distinguish between Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad al-Shaybani's positions on the validity of his transactions.

Core Idea
Return before judgment erases apostasy; after judgment, property is reclaimable by judgment or consent.

In Hanafi law, apostasy (riddah) carries serious consequences including potential execution and suspension of property rights. However, the ruling changes if the apostate returns to Islam. This lesson examines the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani on the status of the apostate's transactions and property, and the practical implications of each view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its conditional ruling
The act is the return of a male apostate to Islam. The main ruling states: if return occurs before the judge's ruling of execution, it is as if no apostasy occurred; if after, the apostate takes his property from his heirs but only by a new judgment or their consent.
2
Locate the proofs for the main ruling
The main ruling is supported by analogy with adulterers and murderers: their liability is a penalty for crime, not loss of inviolability (isma), so their transactions remain valid. For the apostate, the loss of isma is the basis for suspension of property, but return restores it.
3
Analyze the basis of the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf analogizes the apostate to a female apostate (not killed), so his transactions are valid as from a healthy person. Muhammad analogizes to a sick person (liable to death), so transactions are valid but with restrictions as from a sick person.
The difference hinges on whether the apostate is actually killed or merely liable to death.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
According to Abu Yusuf, the apostate's transactions during apostasy are fully valid. According to Muhammad, they are valid but with potential restrictions (e.g., if he dies as an apostate). The main ruling prefers Abu Yusuf's view for return before judgment.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and its implications
The relied-upon position is the main ruling: if the apostate returns before judgment, all his transactions are valid; if after, he can reclaim his property only by a new judgment or heir's consent. Emancipations carried out by the qadi are irrevocable, but he retains wala'.

(If he comes as a Muslim before the judgment, it is as if he did not apostatize, and if he comes after it, and his wealth is with his heirs, he takes it.) Unlike the adulterer and the intentional murderer who are liable to be killed, because the liability there is a penalty for the crime, not due to corruption of inviolability, so it does not impair the effectiveness of their transactions. He said in al-Hidayah: Except that according to Abu Yusuf, his transactions are valid as from a healthy person, because the apparent is his return to Islam since doubt is removed, so he is not killed, and he becomes like an apostate woman. According to Muhammad, they are valid as from a sick person, because one who adopts a creed, especially exposing himself to what he grew up on, rarely abandons it, and it leads to being killed apparently, unlike the apostate woman who is not killed. End. [2] His statement: "It is as if he did not apostatize." So his mudabbar and umm walad are not freed, his debts are not dissolved, and he has the right to void whatever the heir disposed of. Thus in al-Bahr. [3] His statement: "and his wealth with his heirs." The analogy is if Allah returns someone dead to the world, he would have the right to take what is in the hands of his heirs. Thus in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a male apostate's return to Islam on his previous transactions and property. The main ruling states: if the apostate returns to Islam before the judge's ruling of execution, it is as if he never apostatized (his previous transactions are valid, his debts remain, his mudabbar and umm walad are not freed, and he can void the heir's dispositions). If he returns after the ruling, he takes his property from his heirs, but only by a new judgment or their consent. The commentary clarifies that this differs from adulterers and murderers, whose liability is a penalty for crime, not due to loss of inviolability. The intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf analogizes the apostate to a healthy person (since he is not killed, he is like a female apostate), so his transactions are valid as from a healthy person. Muhammad analogizes him to a sick person (since he may be killed as an apparent consequence, though not actually executed), so his transactions are valid but as from a sick person (with restrictions). The relied-upon position is that if he returns before judgment, all his transactions are valid; if after, his property is held by heirs but he can reclaim it. The commentary also details the implications: the apostate cannot reclaim earnings made during apostasy (they become fay'), and emancipations carried out by the qadi are irrevocable, though the apostate retains wala'.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): A male apostate who abandons Islam and is subject to execution if he does not return.
عصمة (isma): Inviolability or protection of life and property that prevents killing or confiscation.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised emancipation upon the master's death.

Key Takeaway

The Female Apostate: Life and Property Inviolable
Ruling that a female apostate is not killed but imprisoned, her transactions valid, earnings for heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a female apostate and explain the difference with the Shafi'i school regarding execution.

Core Idea
A female apostate's life and property remain inviolable under Hanafi law; she is imprisoned, not killed.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the Hanafi position on female apostates, contrasting with the Shafi'i school. It also clarifies that her transactions and earnings remain valid, and her wealth transfers to her heirs only after her real or legal death.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core ruling: a female apostate (murtadda) is not killed but imprisoned until she repents.
The text begins with 'ولا تقتل مرتدة'.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Note that al-Shafi'i holds she must be killed if she does not repent, based on a general hadith.
The text says 'خلافاً للشافعي'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i rely on the general hadith: 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' This is a command that apparently includes women.
The hadith is quoted in the source.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response uses a specific hadith forbidding the killing of women and children, which restricts the general command to men only.
The rationale: the inviolability of wealth follows the inviolability of life; since her life is not forfeited, her property remains protected.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafi – she is imprisoned, her transactions are valid, and her wealth goes to heirs after death. Shafi'i – she is executed if she does not repent, and her property may be treated differently.
This distinction affects inheritance and financial transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[ 1 ] قوله : خلافاً للشافعي له ; له إطلاق حديث : «من بدل دينه فاقتلوه»
[٢] قوله : وصح تصرفها ; وذلك لأنّ عصمة المال تابعة لعصمة النفس، فبالردّة لا تزول عصمة نفسها ; لأنها لا تقتل، فكذا عصمة مالها فكان كل واحد من الكسبين ملكاً لها، فتصح تصرفاتها من غير توقف، ويكون مالها لورثتها بعد موتها الحقيقي أو الحكمي. كذا في «النهاية» و«العناية»

And an apostate woman is not killed, but she is imprisoned until she becomes Muslim, and her transactions and her earnings are valid for her heirs. [1] His statement: contrary to al-Shafi'i; he has the absolute hadith: 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' [2] His statement: and her transactions are valid; because the inviolability of wealth follows the inviolability of life. By apostasy, the inviolability of her life does not cease, because she is not killed, so likewise the inviolability of her wealth. Thus each of the two earnings is her property, so her transactions are valid without suspension, and her wealth is for her heirs after her real or legal death. Thus in al-Nihayah and al-Inayah. [3] His statement: and her earnings; meaning her earnings during her Islam and her earnings during her apostasy.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage addresses the status of a female apostate, contrasting with the Shafi'i school. The Hanafi ruling: a female apostate is not executed (based on the hadith prohibiting killing women), but she is imprisoned until she returns to Islam. Her transactions and earnings remain valid, and upon her death (real or legal, e.g., joining dar al-harb), her wealth goes to her heirs. The commentary explains the rationale: inviolability of property follows inviolability of life; since her life is not forfeited (she is not killed), her property remains protected. Therefore, she can conduct transactions freely, and her earnings during Islam and apostasy are considered her property. The Shafi'i argue from the general hadith 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him,' which includes women. The Hanafi respond with the specific hadith prohibiting killing women, which they prioritize. The relied-upon Hanafi position is that female apostates are not killed.

Key Terms
مرتدة (murtadda): A female apostate who abandons Islam and is subject to imprisonment until repentance.
عصمة المال (ismat al-mal): The inviolability of property that protects it from confiscation or appropriation.

Child of an Apostate's Slave Woman: Paternity and Inheritance
Ruling on paternity and inheritance of apostate's child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which an apostate's claim of paternity over a child born to his slave woman is valid and determine the child's status (free/inheritance).

Core Idea
Apostate's paternity claim is valid if the child is from his loins, but inheritance depends on the mother's religion and timing of birth.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a specific case: an apostate master claims paternity of a child born to his slave woman. The ruling establishes that the child is free and inherits from the apostate, but with crucial distinctions based on the mother's faith and the timing of birth. Understanding these conditions is essential for grasping Hanafi inheritance and paternity laws in apostasy contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage begins with the ruling: if an apostate's slave woman gives birth and he claims the child, the child is his free son. Note that this applies even though the apostate is in a state of apostasy.
The child's freedom is established, not slavery.
2
Distinguish between the mother's religion
The passage divides the case into two: if the slave woman is Muslim, the ruling is absolute. If she is Kitabi (Christian or Jewish), a condition is imposed: the child must be born within six months of the apostasy.
This timing condition relates to the dissolution of marriage upon apostasy.
3
Understand the inheritance implications
The child inherits from the apostate upon his death, whether real death or legal death (joining dar al-harb). For a Muslim mother, inheritance applies unconditionally; for a Kitabi mother, only if the child is born within six months.
Legal death (mawt hukmi) treats joining dar al-harb as death for inheritance purposes.
4
Examine the basis: istilad
The commentary explains that the validity of istilad (claiming paternity) does not depend on actual ownership of the mother at the time of birth. It is sufficient that the child is from his loins.
Analogous to a licensed slave's istilad over a female slave's child.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If his slave woman gives birth and he claims it, then it is his son, free. [4] His statement: 'If she gives birth... etc.': meaning if the apostate's slave woman gives birth to a child and the apostate claims it, his lineage is established from him, and he is free, inheriting from him if he dies by real death or legal death, which is joining the abode of war. This is absolute and unrestricted in the case of a Muslim slave woman, because the child of a Muslim is Muslim, and a Muslim inherits from an apostate as has passed. If the slave woman is a Kitabi, Christian or Jewish, then the ruling applies only if she gives birth to him in less than six months from the time of his apostasy. And his establishing paternity is valid in both cases because, as passed, the validity of establishing paternity does not depend on actual ownership, just as the establishment of paternity of a licensed slave over a female slave of
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage discusses the status of a child born to an apostate's slave woman when the apostate claims paternity. The ruling: if the apostate claims the child, the child is established as his son and is free. The child inherits from the apostate upon his death (real or legal, such as joining dar al-harb). The commentary distinguishes two cases: (1) If the slave woman is Muslim, the ruling is absolute—the child is Muslim and inherits. (2) If the slave woman is a Kitabi (Christian or Jew), the child only inherits if born less than six months after the apostasy (since after that, the child would be from outside marriage). The basis is that establishing paternity (istilad) does not require actual ownership over the mother at the time of birth; it suffices that the child is from his loins. This is analogous to the case of a licensed slave claiming paternity of a child born to a female slave.

Key Terms
استيلاد (istilad): The act of a master claiming paternity of a child born to his slave woman, establishing the child's free status.
موت حكمي (mawt hukmi): Legal death, such as when an apostate joins the abode of war (dar al-harb), which is treated as death for inheritance purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
An apostate's claim of paternity over his slave woman's child is valid regardless of ownership, but inheritance applies unconditionally for a Muslim mother and conditionally (within 6 months) for a Kitabi mother.

Property of Apostate Who Joins Enemy Territory: Inheritance or Fay'?
Ruling on property of apostate who joins dar al-harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the property of an apostate who joins dar al-harb and explain when a judge's ruling is required versus when it is dispensed with.

Core Idea
An apostate's property becomes inheritance only after a judge's ruling, unless his return and second joining with property confirm his death.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya clarifies the Hanafi position on the property of an apostate who flees to enemy territory. It establishes that the property does not automatically become fay' (spoils) but requires a judicial process. The key is balancing the need for certainty of death with the practical reality of the apostate's actions. Understanding this ruling is essential for applying inheritance and spoils rules in jihad contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage covers three cases: (1) apostate joins without property, (2) apostate returns and joins again with property, (3) apostate is captured after joining. For each, determine the status of his property.
Focus on the condition of 'joining' and the role of the judge's ruling.
2
Understand the role of the judge's ruling
According to zahir al-riwayah, the judge must declare the apostate as having joined dar al-harb for the property to become inheritance. This ruling establishes a death-like status that allows heirs to claim the property.
Without the ruling, the property remains in limbo or becomes fay' according to some.
3
Analyze the exception: return and second joining
If the apostate returns, takes his property, and then joins again, this sequence confirms his death and substitutes for the judge's ruling. The property goes to the heir before division among the Muslim army.
This is a key refinement: the act itself provides the needed certainty.
4
Contrast with the Siyar narrations
Some narrations from al-Siyar treat the property as fay' immediately upon joining, without needing a judge's ruling. Al-Marghinani in al-Fath prefers the zahir al-riwayah view, which gives precedence to the judge's ruling or its equivalent.
This shows an intra-Hanafi dispute and the process of tarjih (preference).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن رجع فلَحِقَ ثانياً بماله فظهر عليه فهو لوارثه قبل قسمته فظهر عليه فهو فيء ، فإن رجع فلَحِقَ ثانياً بماله فظهر عليه فهو لوارثه قبل قسمته [قوله : فهو لوارثه قبل قسمته ) : أي يأخذه وارثه قبل قسمته بين الغانمين، فإن قسم يأخذه بقيمته إن شاء. [١] قوله : فهو ; أي ماله في سيوضع في بيت المال، وليس لورثته فيه نصيب، وأما نفسه فلا يكون فيئاً، فإنّ المرتد لا يسترق بل يقتل إن لم يسلم، ولا يمكن كون المال فيئاً دون النفس؛ لأنّ مشركي العرب كذلك، كذا في «البحر» [٢] قوله : وحكم القاضي باللحاق؛ هذا يشير إلى أنه لو رجع قبل الحكم باللحاق، ثم ذهب مع ماله، فالحكم ليس كذلك، وهو خلاف ظاهر الرواية. قال في «الفتح»: جواب الكتاب: أي «الجامع الصغير» وهو ظاهر الرواية لا يفصل بين أن يكون عوده وأخذه المال بعد القضاء بلحاقه أو قبله، أما إذا كان بعد القضاء فظاهر؛ لأنه تقرر الملك للورثة ثمّ استولى عليه الكافر وأحرزه بدار الحرب. وأما إذا عاد قبله؛ فلأن عوده وأخذه ولحاقه ثانياً يرجح جانب عدم العود ويؤكده، فيتقرر موته، وما احتيج إلى القضاء باللحاق لصيرورته ميراثاً إلا ليترجح عدم عوده، فكان رجوعه وأخذه ولحاقه ثانياً بمنزلة القضاء. وفي بعض روايات «السير»: جعله فيئاً؛ لأنّ بمجرد اللحاق لا يصير المال ملكاً للورثة، والوجه ظاهر الرواية. [٣] قوله : فهو لوارثه قبل قسمته؛ أي يأخذه وارثه قبل قسمته بين الغانمين، فإن قسم يأخذه بقيمته إن شاء. [٤] قوله : كالمالك القديم؛ وقد مرّ أنّ المالك القديم يأخذ ماله إذا وجده بعينه قبل القسمة بين الغانمين مجاناً، وبعد القسمة يأخذه بالقيمة نظراً للجانبين.
Then if he returns and joins a second time with his property and is overcome, it is for his heir before its division. [Note: 'It is for his heir before its division' meaning his heir takes it before division among the spoilers; if division occurred, he takes it at its value if he wishes. [1] He said: 'it' meaning his property is placed in the treasury, and his heirs have no share in it. As for his person, it is not fay', because the apostate is not enslaved but rather killed if he does not repent, and it is not possible that the property be fay' without the person, since the Arab polytheists are like that, as in al-Bahr. [2] He said: 'and the judge ruled the joining' indicating that if he returned before the judge's ruling, then went with his property, the ruling is not like that, and it is contrary to the apparent transmission. He said in al-Fath: The answer of the book, i.e., al-Jami' al-Saghir, which is the apparent transmission, does not distinguish between his return and taking the property after the decree of joining or before. As for after the decree, it is apparent because ownership is established for the heirs, then the disbeliever seized it and took possession in dar al-harb. As for before, because his return and taking and second joining strengthen the side of non-return and confirm it, so his death is established, and the decree of joining was needed for it to become inheritance only to give preponderance to his non-return; thus his return, taking, and second joining are in the position of the decree. And in some narrations of al-Siyar, he made it fay', because by mere joining the property does not become ownership of the heirs, and the correct view is the apparent transmission. [3] He said: 'it is for his heir before its division' meaning his heir takes it before division among the spoilers; if division occurred, he takes it at its value if he wishes. [4] He said: 'like the original owner' and it has passed that the original owner takes his property if he finds it in kind before division among the spoilers for free, and after division he takes it at value in consideration of both sides.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, discussing the property of an apostate who joins dar al-harb. It covers three scenarios: (1) If the apostate joins the enemy without property, his property becomes fay' (spoils for the state) once the judge declares him a murtadd. (2) If he returns and then joins again with property, and is then captured before the spoils are divided, his heir takes the property. (3) If the property is already divided among the Muslim army, the heir takes it at value if he wishes. The key issue is whether a judge's ruling is necessary for the property to become inheritance. The position of zahir al-riwayah (from al-Jami' al-Saghir) holds that the judge's ruling is required to establish death, but if the apostate returns and then joins again with property, that act itself serves as evidence of his death, obviating the need for a new ruling. Some narrations from al-Siyar treat the property as fay' immediately upon joining, but the relied-upon position is the zahir al-riwayah.

Key Terms
لحق بدار الحرب (lahiqa bi-dar al-harb): To join the enemy territory, which for an apostate may result in his property being treated as inheritance or fay'.
فيء (fay'): Property acquired from non-Muslims without battle, which belongs to the Muslim treasury.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known and authoritative narrations from Imam Muhammad's six books.
القضاء باللحاق (al-qada' bi al-lihaq): The judge's decree that the apostate has joined dar al-harb, which triggers the inheritance rules.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the zahir al-riwayah in the Hanafi school, what condition is required for the property of an apostate who joins dar al-harb to become inheritance for his heirs?
A) Immediate joining without any further requirement.
B) A judge's ruling declaring the apostate dead, or its equivalent.
C) The apostate's death in dar al-harb.
D) The apostate's return and then joining again with property only.

Key Takeaway
The property of an apostate who joins dar al-harb becomes inheritance for heirs only after a judge's ruling or its equivalent (return and second joining with property).

Kitabah by Son for Apostate Father's Slave: Father's Rights on Return
Explains ruling and reasoning for kitabah by son on apostate father's slave upon return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the kitabah contract made by the son on the apostate father's slave results in the substitute and wala belonging to the father upon the father's return to Islam, and distinguish this from the case of tadbir.

Core Idea
The son acts as the father's deputy; upon return, the father's ownership revives, making him the true master and emancipator.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in Hanafi fiqh: an apostate father, after being judicially declared dead, has his slave awarded to his son, who then contracts kitabah with the slave. The father later returns as a Muslim. The ruling clarifies that the kitabah remains valid and that the father—not the son—is entitled to the slave's payments (badal) and his wala (clientage). We will unpack the reasoning rooted in agency (wakalah) and the revival of property rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and sequence
List the characters: apostate father (murtadd), his slave, the son, and the judge. Note the sequence: father joins dar al-harb → judge awards slave to son → son makes kitabah with slave → father returns as Muslim before slave completes payment.
2
Understand the legal effect of the judge's ruling
The judge rules based on the father's deemed death (since he joined dar al-harb). This transfers the slave's ownership to the son temporarily. The son's kitabah is valid because it is executed through a valid authority.
3
Grasp deputyship (khilafah) and agency (wakalah)
The son is considered the father's deputy (khalifah) because the father implicitly authorized him by leaving his property. When the father returns, the son's actions are attributed to the father, like an agent (wakil). Thus the kitabah is treated as the father's contract.
This is the key reasoning: the son is not the owner but a representative.
4
Distinguish kitabah from tadbir
Note that if the son had made the slave mudabbar (promising freedom after the son's death), the wala would belong to the son, not the father. Why? Because tadbir is more akin to immediate emancipation and does not admit rescission like kitabah does. The father's return cannot override the son's direct emancipation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قضي بعبد مرتد الحق لابنه فكاتبه، فجاء مسلماً فبدلها والولاء للأب. العبد مضاف إلى المرتد. ولحق : صفة للمرتد : أي لحق بدار الحرب. ولابنه : متعلق بقضى. فكاتبه : أي كاتبه الابن. فجاءَ : أي فجاءَ الأب المرتد. وإنما كان البدل للآبِ والولاء له ؛ لأنَّ الكتابة وقعت جائزة، والابن خليفة الأب، فإذا جاءَ الأبُ مسلماً صارَ الابنُ كالوكيل من الأب، فالبدل له [۱] قوله : فإن قضي.... الخ ؛ يعني إذا لحق المرتدّ بدار الحرب وله في دار الإسلام فقُضي بذلك العبد بكونه لابن المرتدّ بناءً على موته حكماً، فكاتبه الابن على مال، ثم جاء المرتد وهو مولى العبد القديم مسلماً فكالمكاتبة جائزة، فإنّه لا وجه لبطلانها ؛ لنفوذها بدليل منفذ وهو: القضاء بالعبد للابن عبد، وبدل الكتابة، وولاء المكاتب يكون للأب ؛ لأنّ الابن هاهنا كالوكيل من جهته، فإنه لما لحق بدار الحرب صار كأنه سلّط ابنه على ماله ، وجعله خلفاً عنه في التصرف، فلما عاد ثبت له حكم الأحياء، وبطل حكم الموت، كيف وحقـوق العقـد في عقد الكتابة ترجع إلى الموكل ، فيقع العتق عن الأب، والولاء يكون لمن يقع عنه العتق بخلاف ما إذا رجع الأب مسلماً بعد أداء العبد بدل الكتابة ؛ لأنّ الملك الذي كان له لم يبق قائماً ، كذا في «البناية»(۱)، و«العناية»(۲). [٢] قوله : والابن خليفة الأب ؛ فإنّه خلفه في ماله بتسليط من جهته، فصار كالوكيل حين عود أبيه ، وعلى هذا فلا يقدر الأب على فسخ تلك الكتابة لصدورها عن ولاية شرعية، وقد صرّح بهذا الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في تبيين الحقائق شرح كنز الدقائق» وصاحب «البحر الرائق»)، وغيرهما. [١] قوله : والعتق واقع عنه ؛ أي عن الأب لما مرّ، وهذا بخلاف التدبير، فإنّه لو دبره الابن في هذه الصورة، ثمّ جاءَ الأبُ مسلماً ، فإنّ الولاء للابن دون الأب ؛ وذلك لأنّ الكتابة تقبلُ الفسخ بالتعجيز ، فلم تكن في معنى العتق من كل وجه، بخلاف التدبير كذا في «النهر الفائق».
If judgment is given that the slave of an apostate who joined the abode of war belongs to his son, and the son entered into a kitabah contract with him, then the apostate returns as a Muslim, then the substitute (badal) and the wala belong to the father. The slave is attributed to the apostate. 'Lahiq' is an attribute of the apostate: meaning he joined the abode of war. 'Li-ibnihi' is related to 'qudiya'. 'Fa-katabahu' means the son entered into kitabah with him. 'Fa-ja'a' means the apostate father came. The substitute and wala belong to the father because the kitabah contract occurred validly, and the son is the deputy of the father. So when the father came as a Muslim, the son became like an agent of the father, so the substitute is for him. [1] His statement: 'Fa-in qudiya...' etc. Means: When the apostate joins the abode of war and he has in Dar al-Islam a slave, and judgment is given that the slave belongs to the apostate's son based on his deemed death, then the son enters into kitabah with him for a sum, then the apostate, who is the former master of the slave, returns as Muslim, then the kitabah is valid, there is no grounds for its invalidity because it is executed through a valid executor, namely the judgment giving the slave to the son. And the substitute and the wala of the mukatab belong to the father, because the son here is like an agent from his side. Since he joined the abode of war, it is as if he authorized his son over his property and made him his deputy in disposition. When he returned, the ruling of the living is established for him and the ruling of death is nullified. How? The rights of the contract in the kitabah contract revert to the principal, so the emancipation occurs from the father, and wala belongs to the one from whom emancipation occurs. Contrary to the case where the father returns Muslim after the slave has paid the substitute, because the ownership that he had no longer remains. Thus in al-Binaya and al-Inaya. [2] His statement: 'The son is the deputy of the father' means he succeeded him in his property by his authorization, so he became like an agent when his father returned. Based on this, the father cannot rescind that kitabah because it issued from a legal authority, as stated by al-Zayla'i in Tabyin al-Haqaiq and the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and others. [1] His statement: 'The emancipation occurs from him' means from the father, as mentioned. This is contrary to tadbir: if the son had made the slave mudabbar in this scenario then the father returned Muslim, the wala would be for the son, not the father, because kitabah admits rescission through inability, so it is not like emancipation in every respect, unlike tadbir. Thus in al-Nahr al-Fa'iq.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on apostasy (bab al-murtad) in Kitab al-Jihad. It presents a scenario: an apostate man joins Dar al-Harb, leaving a slave in Dar al-Islam. A judge, ruling on the basis of the apostate's deemed death, awards the slave to the apostate's son. The son then enters into a kitabah contract with the slave (the slave agrees to pay a sum for his freedom). Before the slave completes payment, the father returns as a Muslim. The ruling: the substitute (badal) and the wala (clientage) belong to the father, not the son. The reasoning is that the kitabah contract was valid because it was executed through a proper authority (the judge's ruling). The son is considered the deputy (khalifah) of the father, akin to an agent (wakil). When the father returns, his property rights revive, and the kitabah is treated as if the father himself had made the contract. Thus the emancipation of the slave is from the father, and wala follows emancipation (al-wala' liman a'taqa). The passage then distinguishes kitabah from tadbir: if the son had made the slave mudabbar (promising freedom after the son's death) and then the father returns, the wala would belong to the son, because tadbir is more akin to outright emancipation and does not admit rescission like kitabah does. This shows that the Hanafi school considers the father's return to Islam as reviving his ownership and agency retroactively, but only for contracts that remain executory (like kitabah); for completed contracts or those that are more binding (like tadbir), different rules apply.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): A person who has renounced Islam and may be subject to capital punishment unless he repents; in this context, he has fled to dar al-harb and is considered dead by judicial decree.
كتابة (kitābah): A contract between a master and his slave where the slave agrees to pay a specified sum in installments in exchange for freedom upon completion of payment.
ولاء (walāʾ): The legal bond of clientage that ties the freed slave to his emancipator; the emancipator inherits from the freedman and the freedman's wala does not transfer without consent.
خليفة (khalīfah): Deputy or successor; in this context, the son acts as the father's deputy over his property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a judge awards an apostate's slave to his son, the son makes a kitabah contract with the slave, and then the apostate returns as Muslim before payment is completed, who receives the substitute (badal) and wala?
A) The son, because he executed the contract.
B) The father, because the son acted as his agent.
C) The slave, because he is the one being freed.
D) The state treasury (bayt al-mal).

Key Takeaway
When a person acts as a deputy due to a judicial decree, his actions are attributed to the principal upon the principal's return, unless the contract is irrevocably completed (like tadbir).

Blood Money for Killing an Apostate by Mistake from Islamic Earnings
Ruling on diyah for killing apostate before flight, with intra-Hanafi dispute on source of payment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah for killing an apostate by mistake, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on its source of payment, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Diyah for an apostate killed by mistake is wajib, but the source of payment depends on whether the wealth was earned while Muslim.

This passage from `Umdat al-Ri`ayah examines a nuanced ruling in Islamic criminal law: the blood money for an apostate killed unintentionally. While the obligation of diyah is undisputed, a critical intra-Hanafi disagreement arises over which portion of the apostate's wealth may be used to pay it. Imam Abu Hanifa restricts payment to wealth acquired during his Islamic period, while his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, allow both Islamic and apostate earnings. Understanding this dispute clarifies the legal status of apostate property and the boundaries of financial liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act described (killing an apostate by mistake) and its legal classification (diyah is wajib).
2
Locate the dispute
The dispute is about the source of payment: Abu Hanifa limits it to Islamic earnings, while the Sahibayn allow both Islamic and apostate earnings.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the apostate has no Islamic wealth, Abu Hanifa says no diyah is due; according to the Sahibayn, diyah is paid from apostate wealth.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah prefers Abu Hanifa's view, which is the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قتله مرتداً ،خطأ ، فلحق أو قتل، فديته في كسب الإسلام [١] قوله : ومن قتله ؛ أي قبل اللحوق، فإن قتل بعد اللحاق ، ثم جاء تائباً فلا شيء عليه، وكذا لو غصب أو قذف بعد اللحاق ؛ لأنه في حكم أهل الحرب، كذا في «البحر» [۳] قوله : فديته في كسب الإسلام ؛ يعني تقضى دية المقتول من كسبه الإسلامي خاصة عنده، كذا في «الهداية»(4). وظاهره أنه لو لم يكن له كسب في الإسلام ، بل كسب الردّة فقط فلا شيء عليه عنده، وعندهما: تقضى ديته من الكسبين إن كانا، وإلا فمن واحد منهما أياً ما كان، وبهذا صرّح في «الفتح» (5) وقال في «البحر»: هذا سهو، ونقل عن التاتارخانية»: إنّ ديته في كسب الإسلام عنده إن كان وإلا ففي كسب الردّة (٦). ولا يخفى أنه لا يستقيم إلا على رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة له من أن المرتد
And whoever kills him while he is an apostate, by mistake, before he joins or after he joins but before he is killed? Actually the text says: 'Whoever kills him as an apostate, by mistake, before he joins (dar al-harb) or after he joins but before he is killed himself, then his blood money is from his Islamic earnings.' [1] His statement: 'Whoever kills him' means before joining (dar al-harb). If he killed him after joining, then the killer returns repentant, there is nothing upon him. Similarly if he committed usurpation or slander after joining, because he is in the ruling of the people of war. Thus in al-Bahr. [3] His statement: 'His blood money is from his Islamic earnings' means the blood money of the killed is paid from his Islamic earnings only according to him (Abu Hanifa). Thus in al-Hidayah. Its apparent meaning is that if he has no earnings in Islam, but only earnings of apostasy, then there is nothing upon him according to him (Abu Hanifa). According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), his blood money is paid from both earnings if they exist, otherwise from either one whichever it may be. This is stated in al-Fath. And he said in al-Bahr: This is a slip, and he narrated from al-Tatarkhaniya that his blood money is from his Islamic earnings according to him if it exists, otherwise from the earnings of apostasy. And it is not hidden that this is only correct according to the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa that the apostate...
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage deals with the diyah (blood money) for an apostate killed by mistake before he joins Dar al-Harb. The ruling is that the diyah is paid from the apostate's 'Islamic earnings' (kasb al-Islam) — i.e., wealth acquired while Muslim, not from wealth acquired during apostasy. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. The passage then notes an intra-Hanafi disagreement: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that if there are both Islamic and apostate earnings, the diyah is paid from both; if only one type exists, it is paid from that. A further complication arises from a narration by Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa that the diyah is from Islamic earnings if they exist, otherwise from apostate earnings. The commentator remarks that this latter view is inconsistent unless based on that narration. The passage ultimately cuts off, but the core ruling and the dispute are clear: the diyah is obligatory, but the source of payment depends on the imam's view.

Key Terms
كسب الإسلام (kasb al-Islām): Wealth lawfully acquired while the person was Muslim, before apostasy.
دية (diyah): Financial compensation paid to the heirs of a person killed by mistake or quasi-intentionally, as an alternative to qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) From both his Islamic and apostate earnings.

D) No diyah is due because he is an apostate.

Key Takeaway
The diyah for an apostate killed by mistake is wajib, but according to the relied-upon Hanafi position, it must be paid exclusively from wealth earned while the deceased was Muslim.

Diyah from Apostate: Which Earnings Are Liable?
Ruling on mistaken killing by an apostate with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah for a person mistakenly killed by an apostate and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the source of payment.

Core Idea
Diyah for apostate's mistaken killing is from his Islamic earnings only – apostasy earnings are fay'.

When a Muslim is mistakenly killed by an apostate, the normal 'aqilah liability is replaced by the apostate's own wealth because the bond of support (nasra) is severed. But which of his earnings are liable? Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples differ on whether apostacy-earnings count. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the exceptional rule
Normally diyah for mistaken killing falls on the 'aqilah. For an apostate, the 'aqilah does not support him, so the diyah comes from his own wealth. Note this shift.
2
Identify the two types of earnings
The passage distinguishes between kash al-Islam (earnings while Muslim) and kash al-riddah (earnings after apostasy). Understand these two categories.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds diyah from Islamic earnings only. His proof: apostasy earnings are fay' (spoils) for the Muslim treasury, not the apostate's property.
4
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold diyah from both earnings. Their proof: both categories are his property until confiscated.
5
Determine the practical consequence
If the apostate has only apostasy earnings, Abu Hanifa says no diyah is paid; the Sahibayn say it is paid. This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text

وَمِنْ قَتَلَهُ مُرْتَدًّا خَطَأً فَلَحِقَ أَوْ قُتِلَ فَدِيَتُهُ فِي كَسْبِ الْإِسْلَامِ. لِأَنَّ الدِّيَةَ لَا تَكُونُ عَلَى الْعَاقِلَةِ لِعَدَمِ النُّصْرَةِ فَتَكُونُ فِي مَالِهِ. فَعِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ تَكُونُ فِي كَسْبِ الْإِسْلَامِ لِأَنَّ كَسْبَ الرِّدَّةِ فَيْءٌ لِعَامَّةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ. وَعِنْدَهُمَا (أَبِي يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ) تَكُونُ فِي الْكَسْبَيْنِ. وَظَاهِرُهُ أَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُ كَسْبُ إِسْلَامٍ إِنَّمَا كَسْبُ الرِّدَّةِ فَقَطْ فَلَا شَيْءَ عَلَيْهِ عِنْدَهُ، وَعِنْدَهُمَا تُقْضَى دِيَتُهُ مِنَ الْكَسْبَيْنِ إِنْ كَانَا وَإِلَّا فَمِنْ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا أَيًّا مَا كَانَ.
And whoever kills him while he is an apostate by mistake, and then he (the apostate) joins (the enemy) or is killed, his blood money is from his Islamic earnings. Because the blood money is not upon the 'aqilah due to the lack of support, so it is upon his wealth. According to Abu Hanifa, it is from the Islamic earnings because the earnings of apostasy are spoils for the general body of Muslims. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is from both earnings. The apparent meaning is that if he has no Islamic earnings, but only apostasy earnings, then nothing is upon him according to Abu Hanifa, while according to them, his blood money is paid from the two earnings if both exist, otherwise from whichever one exists.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn of al-Wiqaya concerning the blood money (diyah) when a Muslim is mistakenly killed by an apostate (murtadd). The ruling states that the diyah is taken from the apostate's Islamic earnings (kash al-Islam). The commentary explains the reasoning: ordinarily, diyah for mistaken killing is upon the 'aqilah (the killer's male relatives), but in the case of an apostate, the bond of support (nasra) is severed because he has left Islam. Therefore, the diyah falls upon his own wealth. Then the ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa holds that only the earnings he acquired while Muslim are liable, because his apostasy earnings become fay' (spoils) for the Muslim treasury. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) hold that both types of earnings (Islamic and apostate) are liable. If he has no Islamic earnings, Abu Hanifa says nothing is owed, while the Sahibayn say payment is made from whatever earnings exist. This ikhtilaf is rooted in the nature of apostate property: is apostasy earnings his property or public property? The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated here but later authorities prefer Abu Hanifa's view.

Key Terms
كسب الإسلام (kash al-Islam): Earnings acquired by the apostate while he was a Muslim, considered his property.
كسب الردة (kash al-riddah): Earnings acquired by the apostate after his apostasy, considered fay' (spoils) for the Muslim treasury according to Abu Hanifa.
العاقلة (al-'aqilah): The male relatives on the father's side who are responsible for paying diyah in cases of mistaken killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, from which source is the diyah of a person mistakenly killed by an apostate paid?
A) From the apostate's Islamic earnings only
B) From the apostate's apostasy earnings only
C) From both Islamic and apostasy earnings
D) Not payable because apostate has no liability

Key Takeaway
Diyah for an apostate's mistaken killing is only from his Islamic earnings in the relied-upon view; apostasy earnings are not his property.

Debt Payment from Apostate's Earnings for Intentional Cutting
Ruling on debt payment from apostate's earnings with proof and restriction to intentional harm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the source of payment for intentional harm caused by an apostate, and explain the distinction between Islamic and apostasy earnings.

Core Idea
An apostate's debt for intentional harm is paid from his Islamic earnings; his apostasy earnings belong to the community.

This lesson examines a specific Hanafi ruling regarding an apostate who intentionally cuts off someone's hand. The key issue is which of the apostate's assets — his earnings from before apostasy (Islamic earnings) or after apostasy (apostasy earnings) — should be used to pay the compensation. The ruling hinges on the ownership status of these earnings and includes an important exception for accidental harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the actor
The act is intentionally cutting someone's hand. The actor is an apostate (one who has left Islam).
2
Determine the debt obligation
The apostate owes a debt (compensation) for the injury. The question is: from which funds should this debt be paid?
3
Distinguish Islamic earnings from apostasy earnings
Islamic earnings (kash al-Islam) are those acquired while Muslim; apostasy earnings (kash al-riddah) are acquired after apostasy. According to Abu Hanifa, Islamic earnings are the apostate's property, while apostasy earnings are fay' (spoils) for the Muslim community.
This distinction is central to the ruling.
4
Apply the rule
The debt is primarily paid from Islamic earnings. If the apostate does not repent (i.e., dies or remains apostate), and Islamic earnings are insufficient, then apostasy earnings may be used out of necessity.
The condition 'unless he does not repent' triggers use of apostasy earnings.
5
Note the restriction to intentional harm
The ruling applies only to intentional cutting. If the cutting was accidental, the blood money (diyah) falls on the 'aqilah (the male relatives), not the apostate personally.
This shows the importance of intent in Hanafi liability rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْ قَطَعَ يَدَهُ عَمْدًا يُقْضَى دَيْنُهُ عِنْدَهُ مِنْ كَسْبِ إِسْلَامِهِ، إِلَّا أَنْ لَا يَفِيءَ فَمِنْ كَسْبِ رِدَّتِهِ. وَصُحِّحَ فِي الْبَدَائِعِ وَالْوَلْوَالِجِيَّةِ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ دَيْنَ الْمَيِّتِ إِنَّمَا يُقْضَى مِنْ مَالِهِ وَهُوَ كَسْبُ إِسْلَامِهِ، فَأَمَّا كَسْبُ الرِّدَّةِ فَلِجَمَاعَةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ فَلَا تُقْضَى مِنْهُ دُيُونُهُ إِلَّا لِضَرُورَةٍ. وَقَيَّدَ بِعَمْدٍ لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ الْقَطْعُ خَطَأً فَالدِّيَةُ عَلَى الْعَاقِلَةِ.
And whoever cuts his hand intentionally, his debt is paid according to him from his Islamic earnings, unless he does not repent, then from his apostasy earnings. And it was verified in al-Bada'i' and al-Walwalijiyyah based on the fact that the debt of the deceased is only paid from his property, which is his Islamic earnings. As for the earnings of apostasy, it belongs to the community of Muslims, so his debts are not paid from it except out of necessity. And it was restricted to intentional because if the cutting was by mistake, the blood money is upon the 'aqilah.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage addresses the debt liability of an apostate who intentionally cuts off someone's hand. The ruling from Abu Hanifa states that the debt (compensation for the injury) is primarily paid from his Islamic earnings (kash al-Islam). If the apostate does not repent (i.e., dies or remains apostate), then the debt is paid from his apostasy earnings out of necessity. The justification is that a deceased person's debts are paid from his property, and his Islamic earnings are his property. Apostasy earnings are considered fay' (spoils) for the Muslim treasury, so they are not used for personal debts except when necessary. The restriction 'intentionally' is crucial: if the cutting were accidental, the compensation would be on the 'aqilah (male relatives), not the apostate himself. This demonstrates the Hanafi school's precise distinctions based on the nature of the act and the status of the property.

Key Terms
كسب الإسلام (kash al-Islam): Earnings acquired while Muslim; considered the apostate's property for debt repayment.
كسب الردة (kash al-riddah): Earnings acquired after apostasy; considered fay' for the Muslim treasury, used for debt only when necessary.
العاقلة (al-'aqilah): The male relatives responsible for paying diyah in cases of accidental harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From the apostate's apostasy earnings
B) From the apostate's Islamic earnings
C) From the Muslim treasury
D) Not required because apostate is like a combatant

Key Takeaway
An apostate's debt for intentional harm is paid primarily from his Islamic earnings, with apostasy earnings used only if necessary, reflecting the Hanafi distinction between personal property and community spoils.

Ruling on the Apostate's Diyah: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on diyah source for apostate's intentional act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the source of diyah payment for an apostate and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
For an apostate's intentional injury, the diyah comes only from his Islamic earnings (Abu Hanifa), not from apostasy earnings, which belong to the Muslim treasury.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi difference: when an apostate's hand is intentionally cut, who pays the diyah? The 'aqila are not liable because support ties are severed, so the apostate's own wealth is used. But which wealth? Abu Hanifa distinguishes between earnings before and after apostasy, while his two students treat them equally. Understanding this dispute reveals the legal reasoning behind property classification in apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the default ruling
The act is intentionally cutting the hand of an apostate. The diyah (blood money) is wajib, but the 'aqila are not liable because the apostate has severed tribal support. Therefore, the diyah falls on the apostate's own wealth.
2
State the point of disagreement among the Hanafi imams
The dispute is over which part of the apostate's wealth should be used: his earnings acquired before apostasy (kasp al-Islam) or his earnings after apostasy (kasp al-ridda). Abu Hanifa restricts payment to kasp al-Islam; Sahibayn allow both.
This is a pure intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; all three are within the school.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that kasp al-ridda is fay' (property of the Muslim community), not the apostate's personal property. Therefore, personal liabilities like diyah cannot be discharged from it.
This is based on the principle that apostasy earnings are not owned by the apostate for personal obligations.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that both types of earnings are the apostate's ownership during his lifetime. Thus, all his property is available for liabilities, including diyah.
They do not distinguish based on the time of acquisition.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the apostate has only apostasy earnings, Abu Hanifa says diyah cannot be paid from them, while the Sahibayn say it can. The same difference applies to debt payment, as noted in the passage, and later sources (al-Bada'i', al-Walwaljiyya) correct the debt ruling towards Abu Hanifa's view.
The correction suggests a tarjih towards Abu Hanifa, but the matter remains within legitimate ikhtilaf.

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the source of payment for diyah (blood money) when a person cuts off the hand of an apostate. The ruling is that the diyah is not on the 'aqila (the tribal support group) because the apostate has severed ties of support. Therefore, it falls upon the apostate's own wealth. However, there is disagreement over which of the apostate's earnings should be used: his Islamic earnings (acquired before apostasy) or his earnings during apostasy. Abu Hanifa holds that only the Islamic earnings are used because the earnings of apostasy belong to the Muslim treasury (fay'). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that both earnings can be used because both are considered his property during his lifetime. The commentary extends this to the payment of debts, noting that according to Abu Hanifa, debts are paid from Islamic earnings unless the apostate does not repent and die in apostasy, then from apostasy earnings. The Sahibayn use both. This passage also clarifies that the ruling applies specifically to intentional cutting; if accidental, the 'aqila would be liable.

Key Terms
كسب الإسلام (kasp al-Islam): Earnings acquired by the apostate during his period of being a Muslim (before apostasy).
كسب الردة (kasp al-ridda): Earnings acquired by the apostate after leaving Islam.
العاقلة (al-'aqila): The group of male agnates who are obliged to pay blood money for accidental killings or injuries.

B) The diyah is paid from the apostate's Islamic earnings only.
C) The diyah is paid from both Islamic and apostasy earnings.
D) The diyah is not required at all because the apostate is not protected.

Key Takeaway
For an apostate's intentional injury, Abu Hanifa restricts diyah payment to Islamic earnings, while the Sahibayn allow both; the key is the legal classification of apostasy earnings as fay'.

Half Diyah for Cutting a Muslim's Hand Before Apostasy
Ruling and proof for diyah on cutting before apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah when a Muslim's hand is cut, then the victim apostatizes and dies, and explain the reasoning using the concept of mahall ma'sum and siraaya.

Core Idea
Liability is based on the status at the time of the act, not the subsequent death.

When a Muslim's hand is cut, the cutter is liable for the diyah of the hand. But what if the victim later apostatizes and dies from the injury? Does the cutter owe full diyah for death or only for the hand? This lesson examines the Hanafi position, grounded in the principle that the protected status of the limb at the time of cutting governs liability, not the unprotected status at the time of death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize the sequence: a Muslim cuts the hand of another Muslim; after the cut, the victim apostatizes and dies from that injury (or joins Dar al-Harb, then returns to Islam and dies).
The death is causally linked to the cutting (siraaya).
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The cutter owes only half the full diyah (i.e., diyah of the hand) in his own wealth to the heir. Full diyah of the soul is not due.
3
Locate the dalil
The justification: 'because the cutting occurred on a protected limb (mahall ma'sum), and the progression to death occurred on an unprotected limb (ghayr ma'sum), so the cutting is considered, not the progression.'
This is a form of istihsan based on the status of the victim at each stage.
4
Analyze the proof (wajh al-dalalah)
Liability is attached to the act of cutting because at that moment the limb was protected (the victim was Muslim). The subsequent death, though caused by the cut, happened when the person was an apostate (unprotected). Therefore, only the injury on a protected subject is compensated.
Contrast with a case where no apostasy intervenes: full diyah for death would be due due to siraaya.
5
Note applicability and exceptions
The ruling applies whether the apostate dies during apostasy or after returning to Islam from Dar al-Harb. An alternative reasoning from al-Hidayah: joining Dar al-Harb legally severs the causal chain; subsequent Islam is a new life.
No liability if the apostasy occurred before the cutting (the apostate is a legitimate target).

Book of Jihad / Chapter on the Apostate
493
He apostatizes - and we seek refuge with Allah - and dies from it, or joins the land of war, then comes as a Muslim and dies from it, the cutter guarantees half the blood money in his wealth for his heir. He apostatizes - and we seek refuge with Allah - and dies from it, or joins the land of war, then comes as a Muslim and dies from it, the cutter guarantees half the blood money in his wealth for his heir; because the cutting occurred on a protected limb, and the death (by extension) occurred on an unprotected limb, so the cutting is considered, not the death, so half the blood money is due. And it is only due in his wealth because the intentional act is not borne by the 'aqila.
[1] His saying: 'apostatizes'; i.e., after the cutting. If he apostatizes and then his hand is cut, there is no liability on the cutter, just as there is no liability on his killer, because he is deserving of destruction.
[2] His saying: 'and dies'; i.e., the apostate dies during his apostasy from that cutting, such that no recovery occurred between them.
[3] His saying: 'the cutter guarantees'; its summary: that in both cases the cutter owes only the blood money of the hand, which is half the blood money of the soul, and he does not owe the blood money of the soul based on the fact that death occurred from his cutting, unlike if a Muslim cuts the hand of a Muslim and he dies from that cutting without apostasy intervening, then the full blood money is due for the death by extension.
[4] His saying: 'because the cutting...' etc.; its summary: the cutting occurred when the limb was protected, because it is assumed that apostasy occurred after the cutting, and the progression to death happened when it was unprotected, so the cutting is considered without the death, so the blood money of the hand is due, not the blood money of the soul.
[5] His saying: 'an unprotected limb'; this applies only in the first case: when he dies from the cutting during his apostasy, not in the second, i.e., when he dies as a Muslim.
And it is mentioned in al-Hidayah: to explain the second case, that when he is judged to have joined (the land of war), he becomes dead in legal estimation, and death cuts off the progression, and his Islam is a new life in legal estimation, so the ruling of the first injury does not return, and this is a good point that the commentator did not notice.
[6] His saying: 'and it is only due'; a response to the objection: that the blood money for killing and cutting limbs is upon the 'aqila, so how is it judged here as due in the cutter's wealth?
(1) al-Hidayah (2:490).
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya elucidates the ruling when a person cuts off the hand of a Muslim, after which the victim apostatizes and either dies during apostasy or dies after returning to Islam (e.g., after joining Dar al-Harb and then returning as a Muslim and dying from the injury). The ruling is that the cutter owes only half the blood money (diyah of the hand) in his own wealth, not the full diyah of the soul. The reasoning is that the cutting itself occurred when the limb was protected (since the victim was a Muslim at the time of cutting), but the subsequent death (siraaya) occurred when the body was unprotected (during apostasy or after legal death due to joining the enemy). The principle: the act is judged by the status at the time of the act; the protective status at the time of cutting is considered, but the progression to death is attributed to a time when protection was absent. Therefore, only the diyah of the hand (half of the whole body diyah) is due. The diyah is paid from the cutter's own wealth because intentional acts are not borne by the 'aqila. The commentary also clarifies that if the victim apostatizes before the cutting, there is no liability at all, as the apostate is a legitimate target. An alternative explanation from al-Hidayah is provided: when the apostate joins Dar al-Harb, he is considered dead legally, and his subsequent Islam is a new life, severing the causal chain.

Key Terms
محل معصوم (mahall ma'sum): A limb or body that is legally protected (i.e., belonging to a Muslim or dhimmi) at the time of injury.
سراية (siraaya): The progression of an injury to death; if it occurs, the liability may shift from injury to killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the reason the cutter owes only half diyah when a Muslim's hand is cut and then the victim apostatizes and dies?
A) The cutting was not intentional.
B) The death occurred when the victim was an apostate (unprotected).
C) The victim's heir is not entitled to full diyah.
D) The cutter is poor and cannot pay full diyah.

Key Takeaway
Liability for an injury is determined by the protection status at the time of the act, not at the time of death; thus, only half diyah is due when the death occurs after the victim loses protection.

Mukatib Apostate's Estate: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Ownership of Apostate Earnings
Ruling on distribution of a mukatib apostate's estate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the distribution of a mukatib apostate's estate and evaluate Abu Hanifa's distinction that preserves the kitabah payment.

Core Idea
A mukatib's earnings remain his property despite apostasy because the kitabah contract, unlike freedom, is not voided by apostasy.

This passage tackles a specific scenario at the intersection of slavery, apostasy, and inheritance. A mukatib (a slave with a kitabah contract) apostatizes, flees to enemy territory, earns wealth, is captured and executed. The text rules that his master receives the kitabah payment from his estate, and the remainder goes to his heirs. This ruling appears straightforward but conceals a deep intra-Hanafi dispute over whether apostasy can sever ownership rights. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that an apostate's earnings are his property; Abu Hanifa objects for free persons but makes a critical exception for the mukatib. Understanding this distinction reveals a key principle: the source of ownership matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
State the facts precisely: a mukatib apostatizes, flees to dar al-harb, earns property during apostasy, is captured with that property, and is executed for refusing to return to Islam.
2
State the ruling from the matn
The master receives the kitabah payment (the remaining amount owed for freedom) from the estate, and any surplus goes to the mukatib's heirs.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) accept this ruling because they consider an apostate's earnings his property. Abu Hanifa, however, does not consider a free apostate's earnings as his property, so he questions how a mukatib apostate can own earnings.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's distinction
Abu Hanifa resolves the issue by drawing a distinction: a free apostate owns nothing because his apostasy alone severs his capacity to own, but a mukatib owns by virtue of the kitabah contract, which is not voided by apostasy. Thus, his earnings are his property.
The commentary in al-Binayah supports this distinction.
5
Apply the distinction to confirm the ruling
Since the kitabah contract remains valid, the mukatib's earnings are his property. Therefore, the master must be paid the kitabah from the estate, and the heirs inherit the remainder. This aligns with the Sahibayn's view and is accepted by Abu Hanifa for this specific case.
The ruling is thus sahih (valid) according to all three imams once the distinction is made.

Source Text

بالموت. وأما على رأي أبي حنيفة له فمشكل ، فإنّه لا يجعل كسب الردّة ملكاً للحرّ،
فكيف يكون ملكاً للمكاتب حتى يؤدى منه بدل الكتابة، ويجري فيه التوريث؟ ووجه الفرق عنده : إنّ المكاتب إنما يملك إكسابه بالكتابة، والكتابة لا تتوقف بالردة، فكذا إكسابه، ألا ترى أنه لا يتوقف تصرّفه بالأقوى وهو الرق، فكذا بالأدنى بالطريق الأولى. كذا في «البناية»(۳).
:
A mukatib apostatized and fled, was taken with his property and killed, so his kitabah payment is given to his master, and what remains is for his heirs. [2] (A mukatib apostatized and fled, was taken with his property and killed, so his kitabah payment is given to his master, and what remains is for his heirs.) (1) His statement: 'because apostasy...' etc. is the cause for the statement of Muhammad, and its summary is that his apostasy after the cut nullified the ruling of spreading, because it is a constructive death, so liability for the soul is not required, but liability for the cut spread his cut to destruction of the soul, whether he died in a state of disbelief or Islam, or he did not spread. And for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the injury occurred on a protected place and completed in a protected place, so it is as if no apostasy intervened. This is because consideration of protection is not given for the remaining of the injury; rather, consideration is given for the time of the cause and at the time of the establishment of the ruling. And the state of remaining is irrelevant to all of this. It is like the existence of ownership during the remaining of the oath: it is not considered; rather, its existence at the time of the conditional clause and at the time of the establishment of the ruling is considered, which is the time of the existence of the condition. For example, if he said to his wife, 'You are divorced if you enter the house,' and he divorced her (talaq), then he married her again, then she entered, she is divorced. This is in al-Fath. [2] His statement: 'mukatib...' etc. means if the mukatib apostatized and joined the abode of war, acquired property during his apostasy, was taken with his property and refused to convert and was killed, then he fulfills his master's kitabah payment, and what remains after paying it is for the mukatib's heirs. This is apparent according to their principle because the earnings of apostasy are his property according to them if he is free; likewise if he is a mukatib. And the kitabah does not become void by apostasy, just as it does not become void by death. As for the opinion of Abu Hanifa, it is problematic because he does not make the earnings of apostasy property for the free person; so how can it be property for the mukatib so that the kitabah payment is paid from it and inheritance takes place? The distinction according to him: the mukatib owns his earnings by virtue of kitabah, and kitabah is not suspended by apostasy; likewise his earnings. Do you not see that his transaction is not suspended by the stronger (i.e., slavery), so by the weaker (apostasy) it is even more so. This is in al-Binayah.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn about a mukatib (a slave with a kitabah contract) who apostatizes and flees to dar al-harb, earns wealth, is captured and executed. The ruling: his master receives the kitabah payment (the remaining amount owed for freedom) from that wealth, and the rest goes to the mukatib's heirs. The commentary first explains the reasoning of Muhammad regarding a previous case (the diyah dispute), then returns to this case. It notes that this ruling is consistent with the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) who hold that an apostate's earnings are his property even if he is free, and by extension for a mukatib. The kitabah contract remains valid despite apostasy, unlike for a free person. Abu Hanifa's view is problematic because he denies ownership of apostate earnings to a free person; however, a distinction is drawn: the mukatib's ownership originates from the kitabah contract, which is not voided by apostasy, so he can own earnings. Thus, the ruling is justified by qiyas: just as slavery (the stronger) does not suspend the kitabah contract, so apostasy (the weaker) does not.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has a kitabah contract with his master to earn his freedom by paying a specified amount.
بدل كتابة (badal kitabah): The payment or its value that the mukatib owes to his master under the kitabah contract; in this case, paid from the estate.
كسب الردة (kasb al-riddah): Earnings acquired by an apostate during the state of apostasy.
