B) The kitabah is paid from the mukatib's estate to the master.
C) The kitabah becomes the property of the state.
D) The kitabah is divided among the heirs.

Key Takeaway
A mukatib's kitabah contract protects his ownership of earnings even if he apostatizes, because the contract is not voided by apostasy.

Mukatib Apostate: Acquisition and Estate Distribution
Ruling on mukatib apostate's acquisition and estate after capture and killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the distribution of a mukatib apostate's acquisitions and explain the intra-madhhab disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The mukatib's kitabah contract survives apostasy; thus his acquisitions remain his property, and his estate is distributed accordingly.

A mukatib (a slave under a manumission contract) is in a unique legal position: he owns his acquisitions despite being a slave. What happens when he apostatizes, flees to enemy territory, acquires wealth there, and is later killed? This lesson unpacks the ruling on the fate of his wealth and the internal Hanafi debate about the effect of apostasy on ownership of acquired property. Understanding this case sharpens your grasp of the interplay between kitabah, apostasy, and the capacity to own property in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the scenario
The case involves a mukatib who apostatizes and joins dar al-harb. He acquires wealth there, is captured, refuses to return to Islam, and is executed. Determine who is involved: the mukatib, his master, and his heirs.
The mukatib's kitabah contract is still in effect; he owes payments to his master.
2
State the Sahibayn's ruling and their principle
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the master is paid the outstanding kitabah amount from the acquired wealth, and the remainder goes to the mukatib's heirs. This is based on the principle that even a free apostate owns his acquisitions during apostasy, so a mukatib (who owns his own acquisitions via the kitabah contract) also owns them.
The Sahibayn's view is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's objection and his distinction
Abu Hanifa holds that a free apostate does not own his acquisitions. However, he distinguishes the mukatib: the mukatib's ownership arises from the kitabah contract, which is not invalidated by apostasy. Thus, the mukatib does own his acquisitions, and the same distribution applies.
Abu Hanifa's distinction avoids contradicting his own stance on free apostates.
4
Analyze the subsidiary issue: serayyat and apostasy
The passage also discusses a related point: if the apostate had previously committed a crime (amputation), his subsequent apostasy and death affect the diyah. Imam Muhammad argues apostasy nullifies serayyat, so only the diyah for amputation is due. The Sahibayn argue the crime's inviolability at its commencement determines liability, not the later apostasy.
This digression illustrates subtle differences in applying the principle of serayyat.
5
Apply the reasoning to a similar case
Consider a scenario: a mukatib apostatizes, acquires a house, then is killed. The master is still owed 100 dirhams under the kitabah. The house is sold: first, the master receives 100 dirhams; the surplus goes to the mukatib's heirs. This follows the Sahibayn's principle.
The practical outcome is the same under both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, though the reasoning differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۱) قوله : لأن الارتداد...الخ ؛ علة لقول محمد، وحاصله : إن ارتداده بعد القطع أهدر أي أبطل حكم السراية ؛ لكونه موتاً حكميّاً، فلا يجب ضمان النفس، بل ضمان القطع سرى قطعه إلى إهلاك النفس، فهلك حالة كفره أو إسلامه أو لم يسر. ولهما : إنّ الجناية وردت على محل معصوم، وتمت في محل معصوم، فصار كما إذا لم تتخلّل ردّة ؛ وهذا لأنه لا اعتبار لقيام العصمة حال بقاء الجناية، وإنما المعتبر قيامها حال انعقاد السبب، وفي حال ثبوت الحكم وحالة البقاء بمعزل من ذلك كله. وصار كقيام الملك حال بقاء اليمين فإنّه لا يعتبر بل المعتبر قيامه حال التعليق ، وحال ثبوت الحكم، وهو حال وجود الشرط، حتى إذا قال لزوجته : أنت طالق إن دخلت الدار فأبانها ، ثم تزوّجها ، ثم دخلت طلقت. كذا في «الفتح»). [٢) قوله : مكاتب...الخ ؛ يعني إذا ارتد المكاتب ولحق بدار الحرب، واكتسب مالاً حال ردّته، فأُخِذَ بماله وأبى أن يُسلِمَ فقُتِل، فإنّه يوفّى مولاه بدل كتابته، وما بقي بعد أدائه فهو لورثة المكاتب، وهذا ظاهر على أصلهما بناءً على أن كسب الردّة ملكه عندهما إذا كان حُراً، فكذا إذا كان مكاتباً، وإن الكتابة لا تبطل بالردة كما لا تبطل بالموت. وأما على رأي أبي حنيفة فمشكل ، فإنّه لا يجعل كسب الردّة ملكاً للحرّ، فكيف يكون ملكاً للمكاتب حتى يؤدى منه بدل الكتابة، ويجري فيه التوريث؟ ووجه الفرق عنده : إنّ المكاتب إنما يملك إكسابه بالكتابة، والكتابة لا تتوقف بالردة، فكذا إكسابه، ألا ترى أنه لا يتوقف تصرّفه بالأقوى وهو الرق، فكذا بالأدنى بالطريق الأولى. كذا في «البناية»(۳).
A mukatib apostatized and joined (dar al-harb), so his property was taken and he was killed. So its counterpart (the kitabah payment) is for his master, and what remains is for his heir. (1) His saying: 'because apostasy... etc.' is the cause for Muhammad's statement; its summary: his apostasy after the amputation nullified, i.e., abrogated the ruling of serayyat, because it is a death hukumiyy, so no indemnity for the life is due; rather the indemnity for the amputation spread to the destruction of the life, so he perished in a state of disbelief or Islam or it did not spread. For them both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the injury occurred upon an inviolable location and was completed in an inviolable location, so it is as if no apostasy intervened; and this is because there is no consideration for the existence of inviolability during the continuation of the injury; rather what is considered is its existence at the time of the commencement of the cause, and at the time of establishment of the ruling; and the state of continuance is apart from all that. It is like the existence of ownership during the continuation of the oath, for it is not considered; rather its existence at the time of the condition is considered, and at the time of establishment of the ruling, which is the time of existence of the condition; such that if he said to his wife: 'You are divorced if you enter the house,' and he divorced her (renaunciated her) then remarried her, then she entered, she is divorced. Quoted in al-Fath. (2) His saying: 'mukatib...' means that if the mukatib apostatized and joined dar al-harb, and acquired wealth during his apostasy, and his wealth was taken and he refused to accept Islam and was killed, then his master is paid the kitabah payment, and whatever remains after that is for the heirs of the mukatib. This is apparent according to their (Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad's) principle, based on the fact that the acquisition of apostasy is his property according to them if he is free; similarly if he is a mukatib; and that kitabah is not invalidated by apostasy just as it is not invalidated by death. As for the view of Abu Hanifa, it is problematic, for he does not consider the acquisition of apostasy to be property for a free person; so how can it be property for a mukatib so that the kitabah payment is paid from it and inheritance is applied? And his distinction: the mukatib only owns his acquisitions through kitabah, and kitabah is not invalidated by apostasy; so likewise his acquisitions; do you not see that his disposal is not invalidated by the stronger (i.e., slavery), so likewise by the weaker (i.e., apostasy) by primary inference. Quoted in al-Binayah.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling for a mukatib (a slave under contract of manumission) who apostatizes from Islam, flees to dar al-harb (enemy territory), and acquires wealth there. He is later captured, his wealth taken, and he is killed after refusing to return to Islam. The text first discusses a subsidiary issue: if the apostate had previously committed a crime, the effect of his apostasy on the diyah (blood money) is disputed. According to Imam Muhammad, the apostasy nullifies the principle of serayyat (the spread of the crime), so no full diyah is due; only the diyah for amputation applies, regardless of whether he dies in apostasy or Islam. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argue that the crime was committed against an inviolable person, and the apostasy does not affect the liability because what matters is the inviolability at the time the cause (the injury) was initiated, not at the time of death. The text then returns to the main ruling: the mukatib's master receives the kitabah payment from the acquired wealth, and the remainder goes to the mukatib's heirs. This is the position of the Sahibayn, who hold that a free apostate owns his acquisitions during apostasy, and thus a mukatib (who owns his own acquisitions through kitabah) also owns them. Abu Hanifa disagrees regarding a free apostate, but here he distinguishes the mukatib case: kitabah is a contract of ownership, so apostasy does not nullify it; thus the mukatib still owns his acquisitions. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) follows the Sahibayn, as this is the view in the authoritative later works.

Key Terms
مُكَاتَب (mukātab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah with his master, whereby he purchases his freedom by paying installments.
الرِّدَّة (ar-riddah): Apostasy from Islam; abandonment of the faith.
السَّرَايَة (as-serāyah): The legal principle that a crime spreads from a limb to the whole body, affecting the diyah.
كِتَابَة (kitābah): A contract between a master and a slave whereby the slave purchases his freedom by paying a sum in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is confiscated by the Bayt al-Mal.
B) It is given entirely to his former master.
C) The master receives the unpaid kitabah amount; the rest goes to the mukatib's heirs.
D) It is divided equally between the master and the state.

Key Takeaway
The mukatib's kitabah contract survives apostasy, so his acquisitions are his property, and his estate is distributed like a free person's after satisfying the kitabah debt.

The Grandchild of Apostates: Compulsion to Islam – Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on grandchild compulsion to Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the zahir al-riwaya position on compulsion of the grandchild of apostate parents and identify the four issues where al-Hasan treats the grandfather like the father.

Core Idea
In the Hanafi school, a grandchild does not follow the grandfather in Islam; only the immediate child is compelled to Islam.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya deals with the status of children born to apostate parents in dar al-harb. The central question is whether the grandchild is compelled to Islam like the immediate child. The text presents a dispute within the Hanafi school: the zahir al-riwaya holds the grandchild is not compelled, while a riwaya from al-Hasan holds he is. The dispute is part of a broader pattern where al-Hasan considers the grandfather equivalent to the father in certain rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two children
The passage describes a married couple who apostatize and flee to dar al-harb. They have a child, and then that child has a child (a grandchild). When Muslims conquer, both children become booty (slaves).
The first child is the immediate offspring; the second is the child's child.
2
Distinguish the ruling for the child vs. the grandchild
In the zahir al-riwaya, the first child is compelled to Islam, but the grandchild is not. However, the grandchild remains a slave (booty).
The reason given: the child follows his parents in Islam and apostasy, but the grandchild does not follow his grandfather.
3
Understand the principle: the follower does not follow another
The commentary states: 'the follower does not follow another' (التبع لا يتبع غيره). The child is a follower (of his parents), but the grandchild is a follower of his father (the child), and therefore does not become a follower of the grandparents.
This principle prevents an infinite regress of following.
4
Recognize that this is one of four issues where al-Hasan treats the grandfather like the father
The passage lists four matters where al-Hasan's riwaya equates the grandfather with the father, contrary to zahir al-riwaya. These include compulsion to Islam, sadaqat al-fitr, drawing wala', and will to relatives.
This contextualizes the dispute within a broader pattern of ikhtilaf.

Source Text

If a married couple apostatize and join the enemy, then she gives birth, then the child [is born] and they are overcome, then the two children are booty, and the first is compelled to Islam, but not his child. And in the riwaya of al-Hasan, his child's child is also compelled. This is based on the fact that the child's child does not follow the grandfather in Islam in the zahir al-riwaya, but follows him in the riwaya of al-Hasan. [1] His saying 'they apostatized' is restricted by their apostasy; because if a Muslim dies leaving a pregnant woman, and she apostatizes and joins them and gives birth there, then they are overcome, then that child is not enslaved and inherits from his father because he is a Muslim following his father, and he does not follow his mother in slavery due to the absence of confirmed ownership over her at the time of his birth. Unlike if she gave birth after captivity; for if she was captured and then gave birth in Dar al-Islam, he is a Muslim following his father, enslaved following his mother, so he does not inherit from his father due to his slavery. Thus in al-Bada'i' and Manh al-Ghaffar. [2] His saying 'then she gave birth' means the wife gave birth in Dar al-Harb to a child, whether its conception was also in Dar al-Harb or in Dar al-Islam. [3] His saying 'the two children are booty' means they become like their origin. This is apparent regarding their own child, for the child follows its mother in slavery and freedom. As for the child's child, he does not follow his grandfather nor his grandmother nor his father because he is a follower, and a follower does not follow another. He is booty because he is in the ruling of the people of war. [4] His saying 'he is compelled to Islam' because he follows his parents in Islam and apostasy, and they are compelled, so likewise he. As for the second child, i.e., the child's child, he is not compelled because the child does not follow the grandfather and grandmother in Islam and apostasy. [0] His saying 'and he follows him' this is one of the four issues regarding which al-Hasan narrated that the grandfather is like the father. The second [issue]: The minor child, if his grandfather is wealthy and he has no father, or his father is poor or a slave, then it is obligatory on the grandfather to pay sadaqat al-fitr for him, as it is obligatory on the father in the riwaya of al-Hasan. In the zahir al-riwaya, sadaqat al-fitr is not obligatory on the grandfather for the child, but only on the father. The third: If a freed slave woman marries a slave who has a father, and she gives birth to a child from him, then the child is free following his mother, and his wala' belongs to the manumitters of his mother. If the father is freed, he draws his wala' to his manumitters by agreement. If the grandfather is freed, he does not draw the wala' of his son's son from the manumitters of the mother to his manumitters in the zahir al-riwaya, but draws it in the riwaya of al-Hasan. The fourth: If a man makes a will to his relatives or to his closest relatives, the grandfather is included like the father in the riwaya of al-Hasan, but not in the zahir al-riwaya. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. It is mentioned in al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir and Manh al-Ghaffar and others that the grandfather is like the father except in thirteen matters in the zahir al-riwaya, five of which are in inheritance, and the rest in other matters.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute explaining the ruling on children of apostate parents who flee to dar al-harb. The author first states the ruling: if a married couple apostatize and join the enemy, then the wife gives birth, and later the Muslims overcome them, both the child and the grandchild become booty (slaves). However, the first child (the immediate child) is compelled to accept Islam, but his own child (the grandchild) is not compelled. This is the position of the zahir al-riwaya. The commentary clarifies that the first child follows his parents in Islam and apostasy, so he is compelled like them. The grandchild does not follow the grandfather in this matter because the follower (the child) does not become a follower of another. Then the author mentions a riwaya from al-Hasan (Imam Muhammad's disciple) in which the grandchild is also compelled because he follows the grandfather. This disagreement is part of four issues where al-Hasan holds that the grandfather is like the father in certain rulings, contrary to the zahir al-riwaya. The commentary then lists these four issues: (1) the current issue of compulsion to Islam, (2) obligation of sadaqat al-fitr on the grandfather for the minor, (3) drawing of wala' when the grandfather is freed, and (4) inclusion of grandfather in a will to relatives. In all these, al-Hasan's view treats the grandfather like the father, but the zahir al-riwaya does not. The passage also contrasts two scenarios: if the child is born after the mother's apostasy but before capture, he is Muslim and inherits; if born after capture in dar al-Islam, he is Muslim but enslaved (due to mother's slavery), so he does not inherit.

Key Terms
زوجان ارتدا (zawjān irtaddā): A married couple who both apostatize from Islam.
فيء (fay’): Spoils of war taken from the enemy without fighting; here refers to the children being treated as booty/slaves.
ظاهر الرواية (zāhir al-riwāyah): The authoritative transmitted rulings of Imam Muhammad in his six major works.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the zahir al-riwaya, what is the ruling on the grandchild of apostate parents who was born in dar al-harb?
A) He is compelled to Islam like his parent.
B) He is not compelled to Islam and remains a slave.
C) He is free and not enslaved.
D) He inherits from his grandfather.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a grandchild does not follow the grandfather in matters of Islam and apostasy; only the immediate child is compelled to Islam.

Validity of Apostasy of a Discerning Minor
States the validity of apostasy for a discerning minor and defines the condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a discerning minor's apostasy, identify the condition of discretion, and apply the consequence regarding inheritance.

Core Idea
A minor's apostasy is valid only if he is discerning, as shown by the age-seven prayer command.

This passage from al-Hidayah tackles a sensitive yet legally important issue: can a minor's departure from Islam be legally recognized? The Hanafi school answers yes, but only if the minor possesses discretion (ya'qil). The ruling is tied to a specific age threshold derived from the Prophet's command to order children to pray at seven. Understanding this ruling clarifies when a child's religious declarations carry legal weight.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its scope
Identify that the matn says 'apostasy of a discerning minor is valid.' Note that this applies whether the minor was Muslim by birth or conversion.
The consequence mentioned: such a minor does not inherit from a Muslim relative after his apostasy.
2
Identify the key condition (shart)
The text restricts validity to a 'discerning' minor. Discretion is defined as understanding that Islam is the cause of salvation and being able to distinguish good from bad, sweet from bitter.
Lack of discretion (e.g., insanity, intoxication) invalidates the apostasy.
3
Locate the proof (dalil)
Al-Mujtaba sets the age of discretion at seven, citing the hadith 'Command your children to pray when they are seven years old.' This hadith is used to establish that a seven-year-old is capable of understanding religious obligations, which is the same capacity needed for apostasy.
The hadith is ahad (single-narrator) but widely accepted.
4
Recognize the legal consequence
If a discerning minor apostatizes, his apostasy is valid. Consequently, if a Muslim relative dies, the apostate minor does not inherit, just as an adult apostate would be barred.
This shows that the ruling has practical effects in inheritance law.
5
Note cross-madhhab disagreement
Some other schools hold that a minor's apostasy is never valid because a minor lacks full legal capacity. The Hanafi school makes an exception for the discerning minor.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf rooted in different definitions of legal capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح ارتداد صبى يعقل (٢) 1) قوله : وصح؛ يعني لو كان الصبي مسلماً تبعاً، أو حقيقة فارتد يصح ارتداده فلو مات له قريب مسلم بعد ردّته لا يرث منه. [٢] قوله : يعقل ؛ قيَّد به لأنه لو لم يكن عاقلاً ومميزاً لا يصح ارتداده ؛ لأنّ إقراره لا يدل على تغيير العقيدة، وكذا المجنونُ والسكرانُ الذي لا يعقل . كذا في «الهداية) (۳) وذكر الطرسوسي في «أنفع الوسائل) : إن الصبي العاقل هو الذي يعقل أن الإسلام سبب النجاة ، ويميّزُ الخبيث من الطيب، والحلو من المرّ. وقدَّرَ في «المجتبى»: بالسن بأن يكون ابن سبع، وهو مأخوذ من حديث : «مروا صبيانكم بالصلاة وهم أبناء سبع (0) (۱) «فتح القدير» (۵ : ۳۲۸). (۲) «الأشباه والنظائر (۳ : ۲۸۱). (۳) (الهدایة ( ۲ : ۱۹۹ - ۱۷۰). (٤) «أنفع الوسائل»(ص٥٨). (٥) في «المستدرك» (۱) : (۳۸) ، و (سنن أبي داود (۱ : ٢۳۰) ، وسنن البيهقي الكبير» (١ : ٢٣٠) ،
And the apostasy of a discerning minor is valid. (2) 1) His saying 'and is valid' means that if the minor is Muslim by following [his parents] or in reality and he apostatizes, his apostasy is valid. Then if a Muslim relative of his dies after his apostasy, he does not inherit from him. [2] His saying 'discerning' restricts it because if he is not rational and discriminating, his apostasy is not valid, since his declaration does not indicate a change of belief. Likewise [apostasy is not valid] from a madman or an intoxicated person who does not understand. Thus in al-Hidayah. Al-Tarsusi mentioned in Anfa' al-Wasa'il that the discerning minor is one who understands that Islam is the cause of salvation, and distinguishes the bad from the good, and the sweet from the bitter. And in al-Mujtaba he estimated it by age: that he be seven years old, and this is taken from the hadith 'Command your children to pray when they are seven years old.'
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on the validity of apostasy by a minor, with its condition and proof. The matn states that the apostasy of a discerning minor is valid. The commentary explains that this applies whether the minor was Muslim by birth (following parents) or by own conversion. If such a minor apostatizes, it is valid, and the consequence is that he cannot inherit from a Muslim relative who dies after his apostasy. The key condition is 'discerning' (ya'qil). The commentary clarifies that a child who is not rational and discriminating cannot validly apostatize because his statement does not reflect a genuine change of belief. Similarly, the apostasy of a madman or an intoxicated person who lacks understanding is invalid. The commentator then cites al-Tarsusi's definition of a discerning minor: one who understands that Islam is the path to salvation and can distinguish between good and bad, sweet and bitter. Additionally, al-Mujtaba estimates the age of discretion as seven years, based on the famous hadith that commands ordering children to pray at age seven. This serves as a textual proof for the age threshold. The passage thus establishes both the ruling and its evidentiary basis.

Key Terms
صبى يعقل (ṣabī ya'qil): A minor who is discerning: rational and able to understand the consequences of actions, distinguish good from bad, and know that Islam is the path to salvation.
ارتداد (irtidād): Apostasy from Islam; leaving the religion by word or deed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Ten years
B) Seven years
C) Five years
D) At the age of puberty

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates the apostasy of a discerning minor (age seven or above) based on the hadith on prayer, and it carries consequences like loss of inheritance.

The Validity of a Minor's Conversion: Hanafi Proof from Ali's Childhood
Hanafi proof from Ali's childhood conversion against Shafi'i objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a discerning child's conversion to Islam and explain the proof from the hadith of Abu Rafi, distinguishing it from the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
A discerning child's conversion is valid — Ali's childhood Islam is the decisive proof.

This passage presents the Hanafi argument for accepting a minor's conversion to Islam, using the historical example of Ali ibn Abi Talib. It also addresses objections raised by Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i regarding potential harm. You will learn how the Hanafi school weighs spiritual benefit over secondary harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
The Hanafi school holds that a discerning child's conversion is valid. The passage uses Ali's childhood conversion as the primary transmitted proof.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith of Abu Rafi: 'The Prophet prayed on Monday, Khadijah at its end, and Ali on Tuesday.' This indicates Ali was the first child to convert.
The dalil is ahad and its thubut and dalala are both zanni.
3
Understand the objection
Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i argued that a child's conversion brings worldly harm (e.g., apostasy risk) and lacks legal capacity.
The objection is based on potential harm (maddarah) and incomplete legal capacity.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The primary purpose of Islam is happiness; harms are secondary. Apostasy is pure evil and cannot be presumed; discernment makes the act effective.
The response uses the principle that primary goals override secondary harms.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Hanafis: immediate legal effects (e.g., treated as Muslim, prayers valid). Shafi'is: no effect until puberty.
Practical consequence: a child convert may perform acts of worship immediately in the Hanafi school.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا : إن عليّاً له أسلم في صباه. وأما تقرير المضرّة الذي ذكره أبو يوسف في «باب الارتداد»، ووضفر [؟] والشافعي في إسلامه أيضاً، فالجواب عنه على ما في «الهداية وحواشيها: إن المقصود الأصلي بالإسلام هو الناس قحط، فانطلق بنا إليه فلنخفف من عياله، آخذ من بنيه رجلاً، وتأخذ أنت رجلاً، فنكفهما عنه. فانطلقا حتى أتياه وأجزأه بما أرادا، فقال: إذا تركتما لي عقيلاً، ويقال: وطالباً فاصنعا ما شئتما، فأخذ المصطفى علياً فلم يزل معه حتى بعثه الله، فاتبعه وآمن به وصدقه، وأخذ العباس جعفراً، فلم يزل عنده حتى أسلم. فعلى هذا يكون أول من أسلم من الرجال أبو بكر، ويكون علي أول صبي أسلم، لأنه كان صبياً لم يدرك؛ ولذا قال علي: ما حكى أن معاوية كتب إليه يا أبا حسن إن لي فضائل: أنا صهر رسول الله، وكاتبه، فقال علي: والله ما أكتب إليه إلا شعراً فكتب: محمد النبي أخي وصهري وحمزة سيد الشهداء عمي وجعفر الذي يضحي ويمسي يطير مع الملائكة ابن أمي ولحمي وبنت محمد سك وسبطا أحمد ابني وسبقتكم إلى الإسلام صغيراً ما بلغت أوان حلم
And for us: Indeed Ali converted to Islam in his childhood. As for the establishment of harm mentioned by Abu Yusuf in 'The Chapter of Apostasy' and [?] and al-Shafi'i also regarding his conversion, the answer to it is as in 'al-Hidayah and its glosses': The primary purpose of Islam is religious and worldly happiness, and harms are of the category of secondary matters; many things are established subordinately but not primarily. As for apostasy, although it is pure harm, since disbelief is pure evil whose evil is not canceled by any state or excuse, there is no meaning to not accepting it and not attaching its rulings while its reality exists from him with his discernment and understanding. And here is another detail we have mentioned in our commentaries on 'al-Hidayah', so consult them if you wish. His statement: 'And for us... etc.'; this is a transmitted proof for accepting his conversion. Ahmad al-Qastalani said in 'al-Mawahib al-Laduniyyah' and Muhammad ibn Abd al-Baqi al-Zurqani in his commentary, in 'The Investigation of the First to Believe': The first to believe in Allah and testify was the truthful of women Khadijah, may Allah be pleased with her; Ibn Ishaq, Musa ibn Uqbah, al-Waqidi, and others said this. Al-Nawawi said: This is correct according to a group of researchers. Al-Suhayli, al-Thalabi, and Ibn Abd al-Barr reported consensus on it. Ibn Ishaq said: Neither man nor woman preceded her by the consensus of Muslims. The first male to believe after her was the truthful of the Ummah Abu Bakr. And it is said: Ali ibn Abi Talib converted after Khadijah but before al-Siddiq; Ibn Ishaq and others definitively stated this, citing the hadith of Abu Rafi: The Prophet prayed on Monday, Khadijah prayed at the end of it, and Ali prayed on Tuesday, narrated by al-Tabarani. 'And it was' among Allah's favors upon him, as mentioned by Ibn Ishaq and others, 'that he was in the care and guardianship of the Prophet; because Quraysh suffered a severe famine, and Abu Talib had many dependents. He said to al-Abbas, who was among the wealthier of Banu Hashim: O Abbas, your brother Abu Talib has many dependents and people have suffered drought; let us go to him and lighten his burden; take a man from his sons and you take a man, and relieve him. So they went until they came to him and he agreed to what they wanted. He said: If you leave me Aqeel (and it is said: and Talib), do as you wish. So the Chosen One took Ali and he continued with him until Allah sent him, so he followed him and believed and testified; and al-Abbas took Ja'far and he continued with him until he converted. On this basis, the first to convert from men was Abu Bakr, and Ali was the first child to convert, because he was a young boy not having reached puberty. For this reason Ali said, regarding what was narrated that Muawiyah wrote to him: O Abu Hasan, I have virtues: I am the son-in-law of the Messenger of Allah and his scribe. Ali said: By Allah, I will only write poetry to him. So he wrote: Muhammad the Prophet is my brother and son-in-law, and Hamza the master of martyrs is my uncle, and Ja'far who flies with the angels is my maternal cousin and my flesh and blood, and the daughter of Muhammad is my wife, and the two grandsons of Ahmad are my sons, and I preceded you to Islam as a young boy before I reached the age of puberty.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school on the validity of a minor's conversion to Islam, using the example of Ali ibn Abi Talib. The text begins by asserting the Hanafi position that Ali's childhood conversion is accepted. It then addresses an objection raised by Abu Yusuf (within the Hanafi school) and al-Shafi'i: that a child's conversion brings worldly harm (because apostasy would then be possible with severe consequences). The Hanafi response, drawn from al-Hidayah and its glosses, is that the primary purpose of Islam is spiritual and worldly happiness; any potential harm is secondary. Moreover, apostasy is pure evil, and since the child has discernment, his actions should take effect. The author then cites a 'transmitted proof' (hujjah naqliyyah) from various scholars confirming that Khadijah was the first to believe, followed by Abu Bakr as the first man, and Ali as the first child. The hadith of Abu Rafi is presented as evidence for the timeline. The story of Ali's upbringing by the Prophet further supports that he converted while still a young boy. Thus, the Hanafi school holds that a discerning child's conversion is valid, and Ali's case is the primary evidence.

Key Terms
الصبي (al-sabi): A male child who has not reached puberty.
المميز (al-mumayyiz): A child who has reached the age of discernment (around 7 years) and can understand basic religious concepts.
الإسلام (al-Islam): The act of entering the religion of Islam by testifying to the oneness of Allah and the prophethood of Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which evidence is used to prove that a child's conversion to Islam is valid?
A) The conversion of Abu Bakr as-Siddiq as a child
B) The conversion of Ali ibn Abi Talib as a child
C) The conversion of Khadijah as a child
D) The conversion of the Prophet himself as a child

Key Takeaway
The conversion of a discerning child is valid and effective immediately, as evidenced by the case of Ali ibn Abi Talib.

Fighting Rebels (Bughah): Definition, Condition, and Proof
Definition and ruling on rebels with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of rebels, identify the condition that permits initiating combat against them, and explain the Hanafi proof from Quran 49:9 in contrast to the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Rebels remain Muslims, but once they gather as a group with a doubt, the Imam may initiate fighting.

This lesson opens the Hanafi chapter on rebels (bughah). We will examine who qualifies as a rebel in Islamic law, when the Imam may initiate combat against them, and why the Hanafi school holds a distinct position from al-Shafi'i based on a direct Quranic command.

Guided Path
1
Define the legal subject
Identify who is a rebel (baghi) according to the Hanafi definition: a Muslim group that leaves obedience of the true Imam due to a doubt.
Rebels are not apostates; they remain Muslims.
2
Identify the ruling
State the Hanafi ruling: it becomes permissible to initiate fighting against rebels when they gather as a separate group.
The text says 'حلّ لنا قتالهم بدءاً' – it is permissible to fight them initially.
3
Locate the condition
Determine the condition that triggers the permissibility: the rebels must 'تحيزوا مجتمعين' – assemble and gather together.
Mere leaving obedience is not enough; they must organize as a group.
4
Examine the Shafi'i objection
Understand the opposing view: al-Shafi'i holds that killing a Muslim is not permissible initially, so fighting must be defensive.
The Hanafi response relies on the Quranic verse.
5
Apply the Quranic proof
Analyze how Quran 49:9 is used as evidence: the imperative 'faqatilu' indicates obligation/permissibility to fight the transgressing party, and the Hanafi interpret this as allowing initiation.
The verse provides a specific override to the general prohibition of killing a Muslim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب البغاة
قوم مسلمون خرجوا عن طاعة الإمام إلى العود وكشف شبهتهم، فإن تحيزوا مجتمعين، حل لنا قتالهم بدءاً
(قوم مسلمون" خرجوا عن طاعة الإمام إلى العود، وكشف شبهتهم، فإن تحيزوا مجتمعين، حلّ لنا قتالهم بدءاً) : أي إن انحازوا يعني مالوا إلى فئة من المسلمين ؛ ليستعينوا بهم واجتمعوا ، أو اتَّخذوا حيزاً : أي مكاناً واجتمعوا فيه ،
حل لنا قتالهم بدءا خلافا للشافعي (۲) له فإن قتل المسلم لا يجوز ابتداء. [١] قوله : باب البغاة ؛ - بضم الباء الموحدة - : جمع الباغي، كالقضاة جمعُ
القاضي، من البغي : وهو الخروج عن طاعة الإمام الحق لشبهة عرضت لهم. [۲] قوله : مسلمون ؛ قيَّد به لأن الكفار ليسوا ببغاة اصطلاحاً.
[٣]قوله : خرجوا ؛ بأن ادعوا كون الحق معهم لا مع الإمام، فإن خرجوا لظلم صدر من الإمام فهم ليسوا بباغين، كذا في «البناية»(۳).
[٤]قوله : فإن قتل المسلم ؛ بيان لعلّة ما ذهب إليه الشافعي ، وحاصله : إنّ البغاة مسلمون، فإنّ نفس الخروج عن طاعةِ الإمام الحق بشبهة من غير إنكار الأمور الضرورية الإيمانية ليس بكفر.
ويشهد له قوله : {وَإِن طَائِفَتَانِ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ اقْتَتَلُوا فَأَصْلِحُوا بَيْنَهُمَا فَإِنْ بَغَتْ
إحْدَتْهُمَا عَلَى الْأُخْرَى فَقَتَتِلُوا الَّتِي تَبْغِي حَتَّى تَفِيءَ إِلَى أَمْرِ الله ) (1) ، والمسلم لا يجوز قتله إلا إذا
Chapter on Rebels: A Muslim group that leaves the obedience of the Imam to return [to obedience] and [requires] removing their doubt. If they assemble and gather, it becomes permissible for us to fight them initially. (A Muslim group that leaves the obedience of the Imam to return [to obedience] and [requires] removing their doubt; if they gather collectively, it becomes permissible for us to fight them initially) – meaning: if they deviate, i.e., incline towards a group of Muslims to seek help from them and gather, or take a place (i.e., a location) and gather therein, it becomes permissible for us to fight them initially, contrary to al-Shafi'i, because for him killing a Muslim is not permissible initially. [1] His statement: 'Chapter on Rebels' – with dammah on the letter ب – plural of 'al-baghi', like 'al-qudat' plural of 'al-qadi', from 'al-baghy' meaning: leaving the obedience of the true Imam due to a doubt that occurred to them. [2] His statement: 'Muslims' – he restricted it because disbelievers are not called rebels terminologically. [3] His statement: 'they left' – by claiming that the truth is with them and not with the Imam. If they leave due to oppression from the Imam, they are not rebels, as stated in al-Binaya. [4] His statement: 'for killing a Muslim' – an explanation of the reason for al-Shafi'i's view, and its summary: rebels are Muslims, for merely leaving the obedience of the true Imam due to a doubt without denying essential matters of faith is not unbelief. And evidence for him is His statement: {And if two groups of believers fight, then make peace between them; but if one of them oppresses the other, then fight the one that oppresses until it returns to the command of Allah} (49:9), and a Muslim is not permissible to kill except if...
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on rebels (bughah). It defines a rebel as a Muslim group that leaves the obedience of the legitimate Imam due to a doubt and refuses to return until the doubt is removed. The Hanafi ruling is that if such rebels gather as a distinct group, it becomes permissible for the Imam to initiate combat against them, contrary to Imam al-Shafi’i. The Shafi’i position is based on the general prohibition of killing a Muslim; they would only fight if the rebels initiate violence. The Hanafi response argues that the Quranic verse (49:9) explicitly commands fighting the transgressing party until it returns to Allah’s command, and this indicates permissibility of starting the fight. The passage also clarifies that rebels are still considered Muslims because their rebellion, based on a doubt, does not amount to apostasy. Additionally, those who leave due to oppression from the Imam are not classified as rebels.

Key Terms
البغاة (al-bughah): Plural of al-baghi, meaning rebels who leave the obedience of the true Imam due to a doubt.
البغي (al-baghy): Rebellion: leaving the obedience of the legitimate Imam based on a doubt.
طاعة الإمام (ta'at al-imam): Obedience to the legitimate Imam (ruler) who has authority.
شبهة (shubhah): A doubt or plausible misinterpretation that justifies the rebel's action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, when does it become permissible to initiate fighting against rebels?
A) As soon as they express disagreement with the Imam
B) After they attack and kill a Muslim
C) When they gather as a separate group and refuse to return
D) Only when the Quranic verse is recited to them

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, rebels are still Muslims, but once they gather as a group based on a doubt, the Imam may initiate combat against them as commanded by the Quran.

Fighting Rebels (Bughat): Hanafi Rules of Engagement
Hanafi rules on fighting bughat with Shafi'i disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on initiating combat against rebels and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Rebels are fought not because they attack, but because they gather — the cause is their assembly and defiance.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah presents a clear inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on three specific rules of engagement against rebels (bughat). The Hanafis hold that once rebels gather as a group (tahayyuz), it becomes permissible to initiate combat, finish their wounded, and pursue their fugitives if they have a group to join. Al-Shafi'i disagrees on all three, arguing that since rebels are Muslims, fighting them is only defensive. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of how the cause (illah) drives the ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the three rulings the Hanafi school adopts: initiating combat on rebels if they gather, finishing their wounded, and pursuing their fugitives if they have a group.
The text says 'حل لنا قتالهم بدءاً' (it is permissible to fight them initially) and 'ونجهز على جريحهم' (we finish off their wounded).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that al-Shafi'i opposes all three rulings because he views fighting rebels as purely defensive; therefore, no initiation, no killing wounded, and no pursuit after flight.
The commentator notes 'خلافا للشافعي' (contrary to al-Shafi'i).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand al-Shafi'i's basis: rebels are Muslims, and killing a Muslim is not permissible initially; fighting is only allowed to defend against an actual attack.
The text quotes his reason: 'فإن قتل المسلم لا يجوز ابتداء' (for killing a Muslim is not permissible initially).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine how the Hanafis rebut this: the ruling is tied to its cause (al-hukm yaduru ma'a al-'illah), which is their gathering and refusal to obey the Imam, not actual combat. Waiting could make harm unavoidable; wounded and fugitives can regroup.
The text says 'الحكم يدار على دليله، وهو تعسكرهم واجتماعهم' (the ruling revolves around its evidence, which is their encampment and gathering).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference: Hanafis allow the Imam to attack first and take proactive measures; Shafi'is require waiting until rebels attack and then only defensive action.
This affects military strategy and the obligation on the Imam.

Source Text

حل لنا قتالهم بدءا خلافا للشافعي (۲) له فإن قتل المسلم لا يجوز ابتداء. [١] قوله : باب البغاة ؛ - بضم الباء الموحدة - : جمع الباغي، كالقضاة جمعُ

منهم، وأعرض عن القتال.
(1) ينظر: «المنهاج» (٤) : (۱۲۷) ، و(التنبيه»(ص١٤١)، وغيرها. (۲) ينظر: «مغني المحتاج» (٤) : (۱۲۷) ، و(التنبيه»(ص١٤١)، وغيرهما.
Chapter on rebels. A Muslim people who have left the obedience of the Imam to return and dispel their doubt; if they gather together, it is permissible for us to fight them initially. (1) "A Muslim people who have left the obedience of the Imam to return and dispel their doubt; then if they gather together, it is permissible for us to fight them initially" means: if they lean towards a faction of Muslims to seek help from them and gather, or take a place and gather there, it is permissible for us to fight them initially, contrary to al-Shafi'i (2) because for him, killing a Muslim is not permissible initially. [1] He said: "Chapter on rebels" — with damma on the ba — plural of al-baghi, like al-qudat plural of al-qadi, from al-baghay which is leaving the obedience of the rightful Imam due to a doubt that occurred to them. [2] He said: "Muslims" — he restricted it because unbelievers are not rebels terminologically. [3] He said: "left" — meaning they claim that the truth is with them not with the Imam; if they left due to injustice from the Imam, they are not rebels, as in al-Binaya. [4] He said: "for killing the Muslim" — explanation of the basis of what al-Shafi'i held; its gist: rebels are Muslims, for the mere leaving of obedience of the rightful Imam due to a doubt without denial of necessary matters of faith is not disbelief. And witness is His saying: {And if two parties among the believers fight, make peace between them; then if one of them transgresses against the other, fight the one that transgresses until it returns to the command of Allah} (1), and a Muslim is not permissible to kill except if he fights and fights. And it includes that a Muslim is permissible to kill if harm is feared from him, like highway robbers and others, so his killing is not contingent on beginning the fight. [1] He said: "And we say" etc.; its gist: the ruling revolves around its evidence, which is their encampment and gathering; if the Imam waits until they begin, perhaps repelling their harm is not possible. [2] He said: "for if he waits" etc.; explanation of the benefit of beginning the fight and the harm of leaving it until they begin. [3] He said: "not possible" — because of their strengthening, increase of their associates, and multiplicity of their assembly. [4] He said: "and we finish off their wounded" — meaning we kill them completely; and therein is disagreement of al-Shafi'i also. [5] He said: "and we pursue their fugitives if they have a group" — meaning if they have a group to join; and therein is disagreement of al-Shafi'i also. And he fought and fought, and it includes that a Muslim is permissible to kill if harm is feared from him, like highway robbers and others, so his killing is not contingent on beginning the fight. [1] He said: "And we say" etc.; its gist: the ruling revolves around its evidence wherever it is found; and the evidence for fighting, which is gathering and refusal to follow the Imam, exists, so fighting is permissible even if actual fighting from them is not found. [2] He said: "for if he waits" etc.; explanation of the benefit of beginning the fight and the harm of leaving it until they begin. [3] He said: "not possible" — because of their strengthening, increase of their associates, and multiplicity of their assembly. [4] He said: "and we finish off" — in the first person plural, and likewise we pursue from ijhaz and ittiba'; meaning we finish off their wounded and pursue from them he who turns his back and flees; because the purpose of fighting them is repelling their harm, and it is feared that the wounded might recover and fight us, and it is feared that the fugitive might join a group and become a fighter, so their harm is not repelled except by completing their killing and hastening their destruction. [5] He said: "and therein is disagreement of al-Shafi'i", so his disagreement in this and in following the fugitive is based on what is established with him that fighting them is only permissible defensively; so when fighting from them ceases by turning away or wounding, need for defense is gone; because harm has ended by their leaving the fight; and its answer has passed. [6] He said: "their fugitive" — in the form of the active participle from tawliya: meaning he who turns his back and turns away from fighting. (1) See: al-Minhaj (4:127), and al-Tanbih (p.141), and others. (2) See: Mughni al-Muhtaj (4:127), and al-Tanbih (p.141), and others.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on three rulings concerning rebels (bughat). The Hanafi position is that if rebels gather as a group (tahayyuz), it becomes permissible to initiate combat against them, whereas al-Shafi'i holds that since they are Muslims, fighting them is only permissible in self-defense. The Hanafi reasoning is based on the Quranic verse (49:9) which commands fighting the transgressing party, and on the principle that the ruling is based on the cause (al-hukm yaduru ma'a al-'illah). The cause here is their gathering and refusal to obey the Imam, not actual combat. Therefore, waiting for them to strike first may make it impossible to repel their harm. Similarly, the Hanafis permit finishing off the wounded and pursuing fugitives if they have a group to join (fata). Al-Shafi'i objects because once they cease fighting, the need for defense ends. The Hanafis respond that the wounded may recover and fight again, and fugitives may regroup, so repelling harm requires these actions. The passage also notes that rebels are not considered unbelievers because they act due to a misunderstanding (shubha). The relied-upon position is the Hanafi view, as this text is a commentary on Al-Wiqayah which reflects the Hanafi school's established rulings.

Key Terms
البغاة (al-bughāh): Plural of al-bāghī: Muslims who leave the obedience of the rightful Imam due to a misunderstanding (shubha), not out of denial of faith.
التحيز (al-taḥayyuz): Gathering or leaning towards a faction; taking a place and assembling there.
الإجهاز (al-ijhāz): Finishing off a wounded person, i.e., killing him completely.
المولّي (al-muwallī): One who turns his back and flees from battle; a fugitive from among the rebels.
الفئة (al-fi’ah): A group or faction, specifically the group of defenders that the fugitive might join.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school in this passage, what is the primary condition that makes it permissible to initiate combat against rebels (bughat)?
A) They must first attack the Imam's forces.
B) They must have gathered together in a group (tahayyuz).
C) They must have killed a Muslim.
D) They must have declared themselves non-Muslim.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits initiating combat against rebels, finishing their wounded, and pursuing fugitives if they have a group, based on the cause of their assembly and potential harm.

Ruling on Killing Wounded and Pursuing Fleeing Bughat with a Fiah
Hanafi vs. Shafi'i on dispatching wounded and pursuing fleeing rebels with a group

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on dispatching wounded rebels and pursuing fleeing ones when they have a fiah, and explain the basis of the disagreement with al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
The ruling follows the cause: as long as rebels maintain a fiah, the threat remains, so killing is permitted.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah examines a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the treatment of wounded and fleeing rebels (bughat). The Hanafi school permits finishing off the wounded and pursuing those who flee as long as the rebels have a group (fiah) to which they can return. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that once combat has ceased, the need for defense ends.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the Hanafi ruling
Determine what specific actions are being ruled upon: completing the killing of wounded rebels (ijhaz) and pursuing those who flee (ittiba'). The Hanafi ruling is that these are permissible provided the rebels have a fiah.
The condition of having a fiah is crucial and distinguishes this from regular warfare.
2
Understand the Hanafi 'illa (legal cause)
The Hanafi position is based on the effective cause: the rebels' assembly and refusal to obey the Imam. As long as this cause exists, the threat persists and fighting is justified, even if the individual rebel is wounded or fleeing.
The cause is their continued existence as a group, not the act of combat itself.
3
Examine al-Shafi'i's opposing view
Al-Shafi'i holds that fighting rebels is purely defensive. Once the rebel withdraws or is incapacitated, the need for defense ceases, so killing is no longer allowed.
This is a fundamentally different understanding of the purpose of fighting bughat.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the evil is not necessarily removed by wounding or flight. The wounded may recover and rejoin the fight, and the fleeing may regroup with their fiah. Thus, the cause remains and the ruling remains.
The Hanafi school prioritizes the potential future threat over the cessation of current combat.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, a Hanafi commander may order the finishing of wounded rebels and pursuit of fleeing ones if they have a fiah, while a Shafi'i commander would not permit either action.
This difference has direct battlefield implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥٠٤  ونُجهز على جريحهم ، ونَتَّبع موليهم إن لهم فئة [1] ونحن نقول : الحكم يدارُ على دليله، وهو تعسكرهم واجتماعهم، فإن صبر" الإمام إلى أن يبدأوا ، فربما لا يمكن دفع شرّهم. [E] ونجهز على جريحهم) أجهز على الجريح : أي أتم قتله، وفيه خلافُ الشَّافِعِي" (1)107 ه أيضا. : ونَتَّبِعُ مُولّيهم " إن لهم فئة : أي إن كان لهم فئة، وفيه خلاف الشافعي" (۲) الله أيضاً حارب وقاتل، وفيه أنّ المسلم يجوز قتله إذا خيف من مفسدته ؛ كقاطع الطريق وغير ذلك فلا يتوقف قتله على البدء بالقتال. [١] قوله : ونحن نقول ... الخ ؛ حاصله : إنّ الحكم يكون دائراً مع دليله أينما وجد وجد، ودليل القتال وهو الاجتماع والامتناع عن اتباع الإمام موجود، فيحل القتال ، وإن لم يوجد منهم القتال حقيقة. [٢] قوله : فإن صبر ... الخ ؛ بيان لفائدة ابتداء القتال ومفسدة تركه إلى أن يبدأوا به. [٣]قوله : لا يمكن ؛ لتقويهم، وتكثر شركائهم، وتعدد جمعهم. [٤] قوله : ونُجْهِرُ ؛ بصيغة المتكلّم مع الغير، وكذلك نتبع من الإجهاز والاتباع ، يعني أجهز على جريحهم واتبع منهم من ولى دبره وفرّ ؛ وذلك لأن المقصود من قتالهم دفع شرهم، والجريح يخافُ منه أن يبرأ فيقاتلنا ، والفار يُخافُ منه أن يتحيَّر إلى فئة ، فيصيرُ فاراً فلا يندفع شرّهم إلا بإتمام قتلهم وإسراع إهلاكهم. [0]قوله : وفيه خلافُ الشّافعي الله ، فخلافه فيه وفي اتباع المولي مبني على ما تقرر عنده من أنه لا يجوز قتالهم إلا دفعاً ، فإذا فات منهم القتال بالتولية أو الجراحة لم تبق الحاجة إلى الدفع ؛ لأنه ذاعَ الشَّرُّ بتركهم القتال، وقد مر جوابه. [٦] قوله : موليهم ؛ على صيغة اسم الفاعل من التولية : أي من ولى دبره منهم، وأعرض عن القتال.
We dispatch their wounded, and we follow their one who turns away if they have a fiah. And we say: The ruling revolves around its evidence, which is their encampment and assembly. If the Imam waits until they begin, perhaps it will not be possible to repel their evil. 'We dispatch their wounded' means we complete his killing. There is disagreement of al-Shafi'i here as well. 'And we follow their one who turns away if they have a fiah' means if they have a fiah. There is also disagreement of al-Shafi'i. ... The summary: The ruling revolves with its evidence wherever it exists. The evidence for fighting is their assembly and refusal to follow the Imam, so fighting is permissible even if actual combat has not occurred from them. ... The disagreement of al-Shafi'i is based on his established view that fighting them is only for defense, so if the combat has left them by turning away or injury, there is no need for defense because the evil has ceased by their abandoning fighting. And its answer has passed.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary expanding on the ruling of dispatching wounded rebels and pursuing those who flee. The text identifies a disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi position is that the legal ruling is tied to its effective cause ('illa): the fact that the rebels have assembled as a group (fiah) and refuse to follow the Imam constitutes the basis for fighting them. Even if they have not initiated actual combat, the danger exists. Therefore, it is permissible to kill their wounded and pursue their fleeing because the wounded could recover and rejoin the fight, and the one fleeing might rally with another group. Al-Shafi'i holds that fighting rebels is purely defensive; once they withdraw or are wounded, the need for defense disappears, so killing them is no longer allowed. The Hanafi response, as alluded to in 'wa qad marra jawabuh', is that the evil is not necessarily removed by their retreat—they may regroup and attack again. Hence, the Shafi'i objection is rejected.

Key Terms
إجهاز على الجريح (ijhāz ʿalā al-jarīḥ): Completing the killing of a wounded person, i.e., finishing him off.
مُوَلِّي (muwallī): The one who turns his back and flees from battle.
فئة (fiʾah): A group or party to which rebels can rally and return for support.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis allow pursuing a fleeing rebel who has a fiah, while Shafi'is do not?
A) Because the rebel remains a threat as he may rejoin his group.
B) Because the rebel is no longer a Muslim once he flees.
C) Because the Imam commands it regardless of circumstances.
D) Because Shafi'is consider the act of fleeing as repentance.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the permissibility of killing wounded and pursuing fleeing bughat is contingent on the existence of a fiah, reflecting the principle that rulings follow their effective causes.

Ruling on Bughat Without a Fiah and Prohibition of Killing Children

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for rebels without a fiah, identify the prohibition on killing children, and explain the evidence from the practice of 'Ali (ra).

Core Idea
Rebels without a group are inviolable in their wounded and fleeing states; children and property are protected until repentance.

In dealing with rebel groups (bughat), the Hanafi school distinguishes based on whether they have a rallying group (fiah). This lesson examines the ruling when there is no such group, drawing on the practice of 'Ali (ra) during the Battle of the Camel. The passage also confirms the protection of children and the holding of property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on two acts: dispatching a wounded rebel and pursuing a fleeing rebel. Determine the hukm for each when the rebel has no fiah.
The ruling is prohibition (haram).
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites the practice and commands of 'Ali ibn Abi Talib (ra) at the Battle of the Camel. Identify the specific narrations and their content.
The core command: لا تتبعوا مدبراً، ولا تجهزوا على جريح، ومن ألقى سلاحه فهو آمن
3
Understand the underlying rationale (sirr)
The commentary explains that fighting bughat is allowed only to repel evil. If the objective is achieved without killing, there is no need. Identify how this logic applies when there is no fiah.
The presence of a fiah implies a continuing threat; without it, the threat is neutralized.
4
Apply the ruling to children and property
The passage extends the protection to children (and women) and orders holding property until repentance. Trace the connection to the same rationale.
Children are not combatants; property is held to encourage repentance.

٥٠٥  (ومن لا فلا) : أي مَن لا فئة له لا يُجهز عليه حال كونه جريحاً، ولا نتبعه حال كونه مولياً ، لأنه لا يخافُ أن يَلْحَقَ بالفئةِ فَلا ضرورة في قتله، فلا يقتل لكونه (ولا نسبي ذريتهم، ونحبس ما لهم إلى أن يتوبوا [1]قوله : ومن لا فلا ؛ يعني البغاة الذين ليست لهم فئة يرجعون إليها ويستعينون بها لا يجوز إهلاك مجروحهم ، واتباع فارّهم ؛ لعدم الحاجة إليه، وحصول المقصود بدونه. والسِّرُّ في هذا الباب : إنّ قتال البغاة إنّما حلّ مع كونهم مسلمين ؛ دفعاً للشر وقمعاً للفساد ؛ بكسر شوكتهم ودفع قوتهم، فأينما حصل بدون القتال لم يحتج إليه ؛ ولهذا يستحب للإمام تقديم دفع شبهتهم ؛ رجاءً أن يعودوا إلى الطاعة بإزالة ما شبه عليهم الحق بالباطل. [٢]قوله : ذريتهم ؛ أي أطفالهم الصغار، وكذا نساؤهم، والأصل في هذا الباب ما شرعه علي المرتضى الله حين قاتل البغاة غير مرة مرة بأحزاب عائشة رضي الله عنها، وهو المعروفُ بحرب الجمل، وكان ذلك بالبصرة، ومرة بأحزاب معاوية له وهو المعروف بحرب صفّين، وهو اسم موضع وقع الحرب فيه، ومرة بالخوارج الذين اجتمعوا بموضع حروراء. فقد قال عليه يوم الجمل : لا تتبعوا مدبراً ، ولا تجهزوا على جريح، ومن أَلْقَى سلاحه فهو آمن ) ، أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة. وفي رواية له : «إنّه أمر مناديه فنادى : أن لا يقتل مقبل ولا مدير، ولا يفتح باب، ولا يستحل فرج ولا مال). وفي رواية له: «لا يتبع مدير، ولا يقتل أسير، ومن أغلق بابه أو ألقى سلاحه فهو آمن، ولم يأخذ عليه من متاعهم شيئاً (۳) ، وفي الباب أخبار أخر أيضاً.
And whoever has no [fiah], he is not dispatched while wounded, nor followed while fleeing, because there is no fear that he will join a fiah, so there is no need to kill him. And we do not kill their children, and we imprison their property until they repent. The commentator says: By 'and whoever has no [fiah]' it means the bughat who have no fiah to return to and seek aid from; it is not permissible to destroy their wounded or follow their fleeing, because there is no need for it and the objective is achieved without it. The secret in this chapter is that fighting the bughat was only permitted despite their being Muslims to repel evil and suppress corruption by breaking their strength and repelling their power. Wherever that is achieved without fighting, it is not needed. Hence it is recommended for the Imam to first remove their doubts, hoping they will return to obedience by eliminating what confuses them between truth and falsehood. The commentator says: 'their children' means their young children, and similarly their women. The basis for this chapter is what 'Ali al-Murtada (may Allah be pleased with him) legislated when he fought the bughat multiple times: once with the parties of 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) known as the Battle of the Camel, once with the parties of Mu'awiya (may Allah be pleased with him) known as the Battle of Siffin, and once with the Khawarij who gathered at Harura'. He said on the day of the Camel: 'Do not pursue one who turns away, do not dispatch a wounded person, and whoever lays down his weapon is safe.' This was narrated by Ibn Abi Shayba. In another narration: 'He ordered his herald to announce: Do not kill one who approaches or one who retreats, do not open a door, do not consider a private part or wealth permissible.' In another narration: 'Do not pursue one who retreats, do not kill a prisoner, and whoever closes his door or lays down his weapon is safe, and he did not take anything from their belongings.' There are other traditions on this topic as well.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling for rebels who have no group (fiah) to return to. The Hanafi position is that in such a case, killing their wounded or pursuing their fleeing is not permissible because the threat is neutralized: they cannot regroup. The essence of fighting rebels is to repel evil; once that is achieved without killing, there is no need. The text then affirms that children (and by extension women) are not to be killed, and their property is to be held until they repent. The proof cited is the practice of 'Ali ibn Abi Talib (ra) during the battles against rebels. The transmitted commands explicitly prohibit pursuing those who flee, killing the wounded, and protect those who lay down arms. This serves as the foundational evidence for the Hanafi school in this chapter.

Key Terms
فئة (fiʾah): A group or party to which rebels can rally and return.
ذريّة (dhurriyyah): Children, especially young ones; also includes women as per the context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits killing wounded or pursuing fleeing rebels when they have no group to return to, and extends this protection to children and property, based on the practice of 'Ali (ra).

Ruling on Finishing Wounded Rebels Without a Group
Ruling with proof from Ali's practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on finishing off wounded and pursuing fleeing rebels without a group (fia), identify the evidence from Ali (RA), and explain the underlying rationale of necessity.

Core Idea
A rebel without a group to return to is not to be killed when wounded or fleeing—because the goal of stopping rebellion is already achieved.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the chapter on rebels (baghy). The Hanafi school distinguishes between rebels who have an organized group (fia) and those who do not. When a rebel has no group, finishing off the wounded or pursuing the fleeing is prohibited. The proof is drawn from the practice of Ali ibn Abi Talib (RA) at the Battle of the Camel. Understanding this ruling reveals the deeper principle that fighting rebels is permitted only to repel evil; once that objective is attained without bloodshed, further killing becomes unlawful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what specific actions are being ruled upon and their legal classification. The passage addresses finishing off a wounded rebel (ijhaz ala jarih) and pursuing a fleeing rebel (ittiba' mudbir). The ruling is haram (prohibited) when the rebel has no fia.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
Find the proof cited in the text. The author references the statement and practice of Ali (RA) during the Battle of the Camel: 'Do not pursue a fleeing one, do not finish off a wounded one...' This is a sunnah (action of a Companion, considered a hukm of the Prophet via tacit approval).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal implication)
Explain how the evidence yields the ruling. The explicit imperative 'la tattabi'u' and 'la tujhizu' indicate prohibition. The rationale is that when there is no fia, the rebel poses no further threat, so killing is unnecessary and thus haram.
4
Understand the rationale (darura)
The text states: 'because he is not feared to join the group, so there is no necessity to kill him.' The underlying principle is that fighting is only to break power and prevent corruption; if that is already achieved, bloodshed is not justified.
5
Apply to related cases
The passage also mentions not taking their children captive and holding their property until repentance. These follow from the same principle: when the danger is gone, harsh measures are not needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن لا فلا : أي مَن لا فئة له لا يُجهز عليه حال كونه جريحاً، ولا نتبعه حال كونه مولياً ، لأنه لا يخافُ أن يَلْحَقَ بالفئةِ فَلا ضرورة في قتله، فلا يقتل لكونه ولا نسبي ذريتهم، ونحبس ما لهم إلى أن يتوبوا [1]قوله : ومن لا فلا ؛ يعني البغاة الذين ليست لهم فئة يرجعون إليها ويستعينون بها لا يجوز إهلاك مجروحهم ، واتباع فارّهم ؛ لعدم الحاجة إليه، وحصول المقصود بدونه. والسر في هذا الباب : إن قتال البغاة إنما حل مع كونهم مسلمين ؛ دفعاً للشر وقمعاً للفساد ؛ بكسر شوكتهم ودفع قوتهم، فأينما حصل بدون القتال لم يحتج إليه ؛ ولهذا يستحب للإمام تقديم دفع شبهتهم ؛ رجاء أن يعودوا إلى الطاعة بإزالة ما شبه عليهم الحق بالباطل. [2]قوله : ذريتهم ؛ أي أطفالهم الصغار، وكذا نساؤهم، والأصل في هذا الباب ما شرعه علي المرتضى الله حين قاتل البغاة غير مرة مرة بأحزاب عائشة رضي الله عنها، وهو المعروف بحرب الجمل، وكان ذلك بالبصرة، ومرة بأحزاب معاوية له وهو المعروف بحرب صفين، وهو اسم موضع وقع الحرب فيه، ومرة بالخوارج الذين اجتمعوا بموضع حروراء. فقد قال عليه يوم الجمل : لا تتبعوا مدبراً ، ولا تجهزوا على جريح، ومن ألقى سلاحه فهو آمن ، أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة. وفي رواية له : إنه أمر مناديه فنادى : أن لا يقتل مقبل ولا مدير، ولا يفتح باب، ولا يستحل فرج ولا مال. وفي رواية له: لا يتبع مدير، ولا يقتل أسير، ومن أغلق بابه أو ألقى سلاحه فهو آمن، ولم يأخذ عليه من متاعهم شيئاً ، وفي الباب أخبار أخر أيضاً.
And whoever has no (group): meaning whoever has no group, he is not to be finished off while wounded, nor are we to pursue him while fleeing, because he is not feared to join the group, so there is no necessity to kill him, so he is not killed; and we do not take their children captive, and we hold their property until they repent. [1] His saying: 'And whoever has no (group)': meaning the rebels who have no group to return to and seek help from, it is not permissible to finish off their wounded or pursue their fleeing ones, due to lack of need and attainment of the objective without it. The secret in this chapter: that fighting against rebels was only permitted while they are Muslims, to repel evil and suppress corruption, by breaking their power and repelling their strength. So wherever it is achieved without fighting, there is no need for it. Hence, it is recommended for the imam to first refute their doubts, hoping that they return to obedience by removing what they confused of truth with falsehood. [2] His saying: 'their children': meaning their young children, and likewise their women. The basis in this chapter is what Ali al-Murtada (may Allah be pleased with him) legislated when he fought against rebels several times: once with the parties of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her), known as the Battle of the Camel, which was in Basra; once with the parties of Muawiyah (may Allah be pleased with him), known as the Battle of Siffin, which is a place name where the battle occurred; and once with the Kharijites who gathered at a place called Harura. He (peace be upon him) said on the day of the Camel: 'Do not pursue a fleeing one, do not finish off a wounded one, and whoever throws down his weapon is safe' — reported by Ibn Abi Shayba. In another narration: 'He commanded his herald to proclaim: Do not kill one advancing or retreating, do not open a door, do not violate a private part or property.' In another narration: 'Do not pursue a retreating one, do not kill a prisoner, and whoever closes his door or throws down his weapon is safe, and he did not take anything from their goods.' There are other reports in this chapter as well.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This is a ruling accompanied by its proof. The passage addresses rebels (baghy) who do not have a group (fia) to return to and seek support. For such rebels, it is prohibited to finish off their wounded (ijhaz ala jarih) or to pursue them when they flee, because there is no fear that they will regroup and continue fighting. The objective of fighting rebels is to break their power and prevent corruption; if that is achieved without bloodshed, there is no need. Therefore, the imam is recommended to first clarify their doubts, hoping they return. The text then explains that 'their children' means young children and women, who are not to be taken captive. The proof comes from the practice of Ali (RA) in his battles against rebels: at the Battle of the Camel, he ordered not to pursue fleeing, not to finish off wounded, that whoever dropped weapons was safe. Other narrations add not to kill a prisoner, not to open closed doors, and not to take property. Thus, the Hanafi ruling is based on the sunnah of Ali (RA).

Key Terms
فئة (fia): A group to which rebels return and from which they draw strength; used here to determine whether killing a wounded or pursuing a fleeing rebel is permissible.
إجهاز (ijhaz): Finishing off a wounded person by killing him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, why is it prohibited to finish off a wounded rebel who has no group (fia)?
A) Because the rebel is Muslim and cannot be killed under any circumstances.
B) Because there is no necessity; the objective of breaking their power is already achieved.
C) Because the wounded rebel has already repented.
D) Because the imam lacks authority over them.

Key Takeaway
When rebels have no group (fia), finishing off wounded or pursuing fleeing is haram because the goal of suppressing rebellion is already met; killing becomes unnecessary and thus forbidden.

Using Rebel Weapons and Absence of Qisas in Baghy
Ruling on temporary use of rebel arms and no qisas for rebel-on-rebel killing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using rebel property when needed and explain why no qisas applies when a rebel kills another rebel after the just party prevails.

Core Idea
Rebel property may be used for necessity, but no retaliation for rebel-on-rebel killing due to lack of imam's authority.

This lesson examines two distinct but related rulings in the law of rebellion (baghy). First, the Hanafi school permits the temporary use of rebel weapons and horses when needed, based on the practice of Ali (RA). Second, if a rebel kills another rebel and the just party later overcomes, no qisas or compensation is due, because at the time of killing the imam had no authority over the rebels. We will explore the proofs and the Shafi‘i objection to the first ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on rebel property
Identify the ruling: the text says 'we use their weapons and horses when needed.' This is a dispensation, not ownership.
Only weapons and horses are mentioned; other property is excluded even in need.
2
Understand the Shafi‘i objection
Al-Shafi‘i argues that the property belongs to Muslims, so it cannot be used without consent.
This is based on the sanctity of Muslim property.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response using Ali's precedent
The Hanafi response cites Ali distributing weapons among his companions at the Battle of the Camel out of necessity, and returning them after the war.
This shows use is for need, not ownership.
4
Apply the same reasoning to the qisas issue
The text states that if a rebel kills another rebel and the just party overcomes, no qisas or compensation is required.
Reason: at the time of killing, the imam of the just party had no authority over the rebels.
5
Contrast with killing in dar al-harb
The ruling is analogous to killing in enemy territory where no Islamic authority supervises.
Thus no liability arises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونستعمل سلاحهم وخيلهم عند الحاجة، خلافاً للشافعي ولا يجب شيء بقتل باغ مثله إن ظهر عليهم [1] وفي كتاب «الخراج» لأبي يوسف : «الذي صحّ عندنا من الأخبار عن عليّ ه أنه لم يقاتل قوماً قط من أهل القبلة ممن خالف حتى يدعوهم، وأنه لم يتعرض بعد قتاله لهم وظهوره عليهم بشيء من مواريثهم ولا نسائهم، ولا لذراريهم، ولم يقتل منهم أسيراً، ولم يذفف منهم على جريح، ولم يتبع منهم مديراً. فأما ما كان من أمر عسكرهم مما أجلبوا به إليه ، فقد اختلف علينا فيه : فمنهم من قال : قسم ما أجلبوا به عليه بعد أن خمسه. وقال بعضهم: رده على أهله ميراثاً بينهم). [1] قوله : سلاحهم وخيلهم ؛ لا غيره من أموالهم ، ولو وجدت الحاجة، كذا في «السراج الوهاج». (2) قوله : خلافاً للشافعي له ؛ له : إنه مال مسلم ، فلا يجوز الانتفاع به إلا برضاه. ولنا : إنّ عليّاً له قسم السلاح فيما بين أصحابه يوم البصرة على ما في «مصنف ابن أبي شَيْبة» (3) ، وكانت قسمته للحاجة لا للتمليك، فإنّه ردّها عليهم حين وضعتِ الحرب أوزارها ، كذا في «البناية»(3). [3]قوله : إن ظهر ؛ بصيغة المجهول، أي غلب عليهم، يعني إذا قتل باغ باغياً لا يقتص من القاتل، ولا يجب عليه شيء إذا غلب أهل العدل عليهم ؛ لأنه لا ولاية لإمام أهل العدل عليهم حين القتل، فلم يقع موجباً لشيء فصار كالقتل في دار الحرب.
And we use their weapons and horses when needed, contrary to al-Shafi‘i; and nothing is obligatory for killing a rebel by one like him if they are overcome. [1] In the book 'al-Kharaj' by Abu Yusuf: 'That which is authentic to us from reports about Ali, that he never fought any people of the qibla who disagreed until he called them, and that after fighting them and overcoming them, he did not interfere with anything of their inheritances, nor their women, nor their children, nor did he kill a prisoner among them, nor finish off a wounded one among them, nor pursue a fleeing one among them. As for what was of the matter of their army that they brought against him, there is disagreement among us: some said he divided what they brought against him after taking khums, and some said he returned it to its owners as inheritance among them.' [1] His saying: 'their weapons and horses'; not other belongings of theirs, even if need exists. So in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. (2) His saying: 'contrary to al-Shafi‘i'; his argument: it is the property of a Muslim, so it is not permissible to benefit from it except with his consent. And for us: Ali divided the weapons among his companions on the day of Basra, as in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, and his division was for need, not for ownership, for he returned them to them when the war laid down its burdens. So in al-Binaya. [3] His saying: 'if they are overcome' in the passive voice, meaning they overpowered them; that is, if a rebel kills a rebel, the killer is not subject to qisas, and nothing is obligatory upon him if the people of justice overcome them, because there is no authority of the imam of the people of justice over them at the time of killing, so it does not become a cause for anything, thus it becomes like killing in dar al-harb.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on two issues: (1) using the weapons and horses of rebels when needed, and (2) the absence of qisas (retaliation) when a rebel kills another rebel after the just party prevails. The first ruling is established contrary to Imam al-Shafi‘i, who argued that since the property belongs to Muslims, it cannot be used without their consent. The Hanafi response is based on the practice of Ali (RA) at the Battle of the Camel: he distributed weapons among his companions out of necessity, not ownership, and returned them after the war. Therefore, temporary use for need is permitted. The second ruling addresses a rebel killing another rebel: if the just party overcomes, no qisas is required because at the time of the killing, the imam of the just party had no authority over the rebels, so it is analogous to killing in dar al-harb (enemy territory). The text also cites Abu Yusuf's 'al-Kharaj' detailing Ali's practice: he did not pursue fleeing rebels, kill prisoners, or take property; there is disagreement on whether he divided the war booty or returned it. The Hanafi position is that weapons and horses alone may be used when needed, and no qisas applies in this scenario.

Key Terms
باغ (bagh): A rebel who fights against the legitimate Muslim ruler with a ta'wil (interpretation).
إمام أهل العدل (imam ahl al-adl): The legitimate ruler or his representative who fights against rebels.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the killer acted in self-defense.
B) Because the victim was a rebel and thus not entitled to protection.
C) Because at the time of the killing, the imam of the just party had no authority over the rebels.
D) Because the killing occurred in dar al-harb.

Key Takeaway
Rebel property may be used temporarily for need, and rebel-on-rebel killing incurs no liability when the just party prevails, due to lack of imam's authority at the time.
