Using Rebels' Weapons and Horses in Necessity: A Hanafi Ruling
Ruling on permissibility of using rebel property out of necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using rebel property during war and explain the proof from Caliph Ali's action.

Core Idea
Rebels' weapons and horses may be used out of necessity but not taken as booty.

This passage addresses a specific question of jihad: may the just army use the weapons and horses of the rebels during battle? The Hanafi school permits it when needed, based on the practice of Caliph Ali at the Battle of Basra. The Shafi'i school disagrees, holding that a Muslim's property is inviolable without consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is using the weapons and horses of rebels (bughat) during war. The Hanafi ruling is that it is permissible (mubah) when there is a need.
The ruling applies only to weapons and horses, not other property.
2
Understand the Scope
The permissibility is limited to weapons and horses; other property of rebels may not be used even if there is need, as stated in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.
This restriction shows that the ruling is specific and not a general license.
3
Recognize the Ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds that rebels' property, being that of Muslims, is inviolable and cannot be used without their consent.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on differing principles.
4
Examine the Hanafi Proof
The Hanafi proof is the action of Caliph Ali at the Battle of Basra: he distributed the weapons of the defeated rebels among his companions out of need.
The proof is taken from Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah and is considered a qawl sahabi.
5
Interpret the Proof
Ali's distribution was for use (not ownership) because he returned the weapons to the rebels when the war ended, as recorded in al-Binayah.
This response shows that the action was necessity-based, not a claim of ownership.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Does a Rebel Inherit from His Just Victim?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on inheritance when rebel kills just relative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf on inheritance by a rebel who kills his just relative, and explain the reasoning behind each position, citing the key concepts of ta'wil fasid and man'ah.

Core Idea
A rebel who kills with a false interpretation and power inherits because the killing is not prohibited; Abu Yusuf dissents.

In this passage, we examine a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding inheritance when a rebel kills a just person. The disagreement hinges on whether the rebel's killing is considered 'prohibited' (mahzur) given his false interpretation (ta'wil fasid) and his possessing power (man'ah). Understanding this dispute clarifies how the Hanafi school balances the prohibition of killing with rulings that accommodate political rebellion.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold: the rebel inherits from the just victim.
This is the majority view within the school.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Who objects? Abu Yusuf disagrees: the rebel does not inherit.
Abu Yusuf's view is a minority intra-Hanafi opinion.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Abu Yusuf's dalil: the rebel is a Muslim obligated by Islamic law, so his killing is prohibited killing (qatl mahzur), which bars inheritance.
The key is that the killing is prohibited because the rebel is subject to Islamic laws.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Al-Sarakhsi rebuts: since the rebel incurs no retaliation or diyah, the killing is not treated as prohibited; thus inheritance is not barred.
The absence of punitive measures indicates the killing is not considered a legal wrong.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Abu Hanifa/Muhammad allow inheritance; Abu Yusuf does not. The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
Al-Sarakhsi’s tarjih in Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir settles the dispute.

Source Text

(وإن غلبوا على مصر فقتل رجل من أهلِهِ آخر منه) فظهر عليهم قُتِلَ به ) : هذا إذا لم تجر البغاة في ذلك المصر أحكامهم فحينئذ لم تنقطع ولاية الإمام عن ذلك المصر فيجري أحكامه. (وباغ قتل عادلا مدعياً حقيته يرثه)(1) قوله : فقتل ؛ يعني قتل رجل من أهل ذلك المصر عمداً رجلاً آخر من أهل ذلك المصر في زمان حكومة البغاة. (2) قوله : قتل به ؛ أي القاتل أي اقتص الإمام من القاتل. (3) قوله : إذا لم تجر ؛ بأن أخرجهم إمام العدل من ذلك المصر قبل تقرر حكمهم واستحكام ولايتهم فإن أجروا فيه أحكامهم انقطعت ولاية الإمام عن أهل ذلك المصر. [4] قوله : وباغ قتل عادلاً ؛ هو ضدّ الباغي قال في «ضوء السراج شرح الفرائض السراجية»: العادلُ إذا قتل مورثه الباغي لا يحرم عن الميراث بالاتفاق ؛ لأنه قتل بحق، والباغي إذا قتل مورثه العادل فكذا عند أبي حنيفة له ومحمد ه ؛ لأنّ التأويل الفاسد إذا انضم إليه المنعة كان ملحقاً بالتأويل الصحيح إلا أن أبا يوسف له يقول هاهنا : لا يرثه ؛ لأنّ الباغي مسلم فخوطب بأحكام الإسلام، فكان قتله العادل قتلاً محظوراً، وحرمان الميراث جزاء القتل المحظور. وقال شمس الأئمة السرخسي في «شرح السير الكبير»: لكن ما قاله أبو حنيفة ومحمد الله أصح ؛ فإنّ القتل الموجود من الباغي لا يجب عليه فيه قصاص ولا دِيَة ؛ لوجود التأويل والمنعة. انتهى. [0] قوله : مدعياً ؛ أي حال كون الباغي القاتل مدعياً كونه على حق.
And if they (rebels) gain dominance over a city and a man from its people kills another (from the same city), then upon their (just army's) victory, he is killed for it. And a rebel who kills a just person, claiming his own rightfulness, inherits from him because the authority of the Imam is severed from them. (And if they gain dominance over a city and a man from its people kills another from it, then upon their victory, he is killed for it): this applies if the rebels have not implemented their rulings in that city, for then the Imam's authority is not severed from that city and his rulings apply. (And a rebel who kills a just person, claiming his rightfulness, inherits from him) (1) His saying: so he killed; meaning a man from that city intentionally killed another man from that city during the time of rebel rule. (2) His saying: he is killed for it; i.e. the killer, meaning the Imam executes retaliation against the killer. (3) His saying: if they have not implemented; meaning if the Imam of justice expelled them from that city before their rule was established and their authority consolidated; if they implemented their rulings, the Imam's authority over that city is severed. [4] His saying: And a rebel who kills a just person; he is the opposite of the rebel. He said in Daw' al-Siraj Sharh al-Fara'id al-Sirajiyyah: The just person, if he kills his inheritor the rebel, is not deprived of inheritance by consensus, because his killing was with right. And the rebel, if he kills his inheritor the just person, so likewise according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because a false interpretation (ta'wil fasid) when accompanied by power (man'ah) is attached to a correct interpretation, except that Abu Yusuf says here: he does not inherit from him, because the rebel is a Muslim and was addressed by the laws of Islam, so his killing of the just person is a prohibited killing (qatl mahzur), and deprivation of inheritance is the penalty for prohibited killing. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said in Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir: But what Abu Hanifa and Muhammad said is more correct, because the killing by the rebel does not entail retaliation or blood-money due to existence of interpretation and power. End. [0] His saying: claiming; i.e. the state of the rebel killer claiming to be upon the truth.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage discusses two scenarios: First, if rebels take over a city but have not yet implemented their own legal rulings, the Imam's authority remains, and if a person kills another during that period, retaliation is carried out after the just army regains control. Second, the main issue: whether a rebel who kills his just father inherits from him. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say yes, because the killing is not considered prohibited (mahzur) due to the rebel's interpretation (ta'wil) and power (man'ah). They analogize to a just person killing a rebel, which by consensus does not bar inheritance. Abu Yusuf disagrees: the rebel is a Muslim and should abide by Islamic law; his killing is therefore prohibited killing, which bars inheritance. Al-Sarakhsi sides with Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, noting that since the rebel does not incur retaliation or diyah, the killing is not treated as a prohibited killing, so inheritance is not barred. The commentary emphasizes that the rebel must be claiming righteousness (mudda'iyan haqqiyatahu). The authority weight for the preferred position is from Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir, which is part of Zahir al-Riwayah commentary.

Key Terms
باغ (bāghī): Rebel with a false interpretation (ta'wil fasid) and power (man'ah).
عادل (ʿādil): Just person; the opposite of a rebel in this context.
تأويل فاسد (taʾwīl fāsid): A plausible but incorrect interpretation of Islamic law that the rebel uses to justify rebellion.
منعة (manʿah): Power or strength that enables the rebels to stand against the just Imam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, does a rebel (baghi) who kills his just father inherit from him?
A) No, because killing a Muslim is prohibited and bars inheritance.
B) Yes, because the killing is not treated as prohibited due to the rebel's ta'wil and man'ah.
C) Yes, only if the rebel repents.
D) No, because retaliation is applicable.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a rebel with a false interpretation and power inherits from his just victim because the killing is not considered prohibited; this is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi).

Inheritance Between a Rebel and a Just Person
Rulings on rebels taking over a city and inheritance between rebels and just persons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on inheritance when a rebel kills a just person, explain the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and apply the ruling to given scenarios.

Core Idea
A rebel who kills a just person under a false interpretation inherits from him, because the killing is not deemed prohibited when supported by power and ta'wil.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya deals with the legal consequences when rebels overpower a city and kill a just person. While the first ruling concerns the application of qisas by the imam, the more nuanced discussion revolves around inheritance: does a rebel who kills a just person while claiming to be right inherit from that person? The Hanafi school is divided on this issue, with Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani affirming inheritance, and Abu Yusuf denying it. Understanding this disagreement illuminates how the Hanafi school balances textual obligations with circumstantial leniency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the main Hanafi ruling
Determine the factual situation: rebels take over a city; a just person is killed by a rebel who claims to be right. State the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the rebel inherits from the just person.
Note that the just person inherits from the rebel by consensus, because his killing was by right.
2
Examine the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Abu Yusuf disagrees: he argues that the rebel does not inherit because the killing is prohibited (haram) under Islamic law, and prohibited killing bars inheritance. Al-Shafi'i agrees with Abu Yusuf.
This disagreement hinges on whether the false interpretation (ta'wil fasid) combined with power (man'ah) removes the prohibition from the killing for inheritance purposes.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response and tarjih
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that the lack of qisas and diyah for this killing indicates it is not treated as a prohibited killing for inheritance, because the need to remove deprivation (hajah daf' al-hirman) applies to inheritance as well as liability. Al-Sarakhsi in Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir prefers their view.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that the rebel inherits if he claimed to be right.
4
Consider exceptional and analogical rulings
If the rebel admits he was upon falsehood, he does not inherit. Also, selling weapons to rebels is disliked (makruh), but selling raw materials like iron is not. A similar principle applies to musical instruments.
These rulings demonstrate that the legal effect depends on the presence or absence of shubhah (plausible interpretation).
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a scenario where a rebel kills a just person but later confesses that he had no right, what is the ruling? Answer: No inheritance, because he lacks the claiming of right.
The conditions for inheritance include the rebel's claim to be right at the time of killing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجهاد / باب البغاة
وإن غلبوا على مصر فقتل رجل من أهلِهِ آخرَ منه ، فظهر عليهم قُتِلَ به. وباغ قتل عادلاً مدعياً حقيته يرثه. هذا إذا لم تجر البغاة في ذلك المصر أحكامهم، فحينئذ لم تنقطع ولاية الإمام عن ذلك المصر، فيجري أحكامه.
قوله: فقتل؛ يعني قتل رجل من أهل ذلك المصرِ عمداً رجلاً آخر من أهل ذلك المصر في زمان حكومة البغاة.
قوله: قتل به؛ أي القاتل أي اقتص الإمام من القاتل.
قوله: إذا لم تجر؛ بأن أخرجهم إمام العدل من ذلك المصر قبل تقرر حكمهم واستحكام ولايتهم، فإن أجروا فيه أحكامهم انقطعت ولاية الإمام عن أهل ذلك المصر.
قوله: وباغ قتل عادلاً؛ هو ضدّ الباغي، قال في «ضوء السراج شرح الفرائض السراجية»: العادلُ إذا قتل مورثه الباغي لا يحرم عن الميراث بالاتفاق؛ لأنه قتل بحق، والباغي إذا قتل مورثه العادل فكذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد؛ لأنّ التأويل الفاسد إذا انضم إليه المنعة كان ملحقاً بالتأويل الصحيح إلا أن أبا يوسف يقول هاهنا: لا يرثه؛ لأنّ الباغي مسلم فخوطب بأحكام الإسلام، فكان قتله العادل قتلاً محظوراً، وحرمان الميراث جزاء القتل المحظور.
وقال شمس الأئمة السرخسي في «شرح السير الكبير»: لكن ما قاله أبو حنيفة ومحمد أصح؛ فإنّ القتل الموجود من الباغي لا يجب عليه فيه قصاص ولا دِيَة؛ لوجود التأويل والمنعة. انتهى.
قوله: مدعياً؛ أي حال كون الباغي القاتل مدعياً كونه على حق.
كعكسه، فإن أقر أنه على الباطل لا. وبيع السلاح هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، وعند أبي يوسف والشَّافِعِي لا يرث الباغي العادل، سواء ادعى حقيته، أو أقر أنه على الباطل، (كعكسه): أي كما يرث العادل الباغي إذا قتله، فإن أقر أنه على الباطل لا): أي إن أقر أنَّه على الباطل لا يرثه.
قوله: لا يرث؛ لأن التأويل الفاسد إنّما يعتبر في باب دفع الضمان لا في باب استحقاق الإرث، فيحرم الميراث مطلقاً؛ لأنه قتله بغير حق، ولهما: إن الحاجة إلى دفع الحرمان عن الإرث أيضاً كما يعتبر في دفع الضمان إذا؛ لقرابة مسبب الإرث.
قوله: كعكسه؛ أي إذا قتل العادلُ الباغي لم يحرم من الميراث؛ لأنه قتل بحق، وموجب الحرمان عن الميراث إنّما هو القتل بغير حق.
قوله: لا يرثه؛ لعدم الشبهة، وهي التأويل باعتقادِ كونه على حق.
قوله: وبيع السلاح؛ وكذا هبته وسائر أسباب التمليك، وأما بيع ما يتخذ منه السلاح كالحديد فلا يكره، ونظيره أنّ بيع المعازف مكروه؛ لأنّ المعصية تقام بعينها، ولا يكره بيع الخشب الذي تتخذ منه المعازف، وعلى هذا يصح بيع العصير، ويكره بيع الخمر، كذا في «الفتح».
The Book of Jihad / Chapter on Rebels. If they overpower a city and a man from its people kills another from it, then when [the imam] gains victory over them, he (the killer) is killed in retaliation. And a rebel who kills a just person, claiming to be right, inherits from him. This is when the rebels have not implemented their rulings in that city; then the authority of the imam over that city is not cut off, so his rulings apply. His saying: 'kills' means a man from that city intentionally kills another man from that city during the rule of the rebels. His saying: 'is killed in retaliation' means the killer, i.e. the imam executes retaliation on the killer. His saying: 'when they have not implemented' is explained by the just imam expelling them from that city before their rule is established and their authority strong; if they have implemented their rulings, then the imam's authority over that city is cut off. His saying: 'a rebel who kills a just person' is the opposite of the rebel. He said in 'Dhaw al-Siraj Sharh al-Fara'id al-Sirajiyyah': The just person, if he kills his inheritor who is a rebel, is not deprived of inheritance by consensus; because it is a killing by right. And if the rebel kills his inheritor who is just, then similarly according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because the false interpretation when combined with power (man'ah) is attached to the correct interpretation. Except that Abu Yusuf says here: He does not inherit him; because the rebel is a Muslim and addressed by the rulings of Islam, so his killing of the just person is a prohibited killing, and deprivation of inheritance is the penalty for prohibited killing. And Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said in 'Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir': But what Abu Hanifa and Muhammad said is more correct; because there is neither retaliation nor blood-money due from the rebel for the killing, due to the existence of interpretation and power. End. His saying: 'claiming' means the condition of the rebel killer claiming to be in the right. The opposite of it: if he admits that he is upon falsehood, then no. And selling weapons: this is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; according to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, the rebel does not inherit the just person, whether he claims to be right or admits to be upon falsehood. 'The opposite of it' means just as the just person inherits the rebel if he kills him. 'If he admits that he is upon falsehood, then no' means if he admits that he is upon falsehood, he does not inherit him. His saying: 'does not inherit' because false interpretation is only considered in the chapter of removing liability (daman), not in the chapter of entitlement to inheritance, so he is deprived of inheritance absolutely; because he killed him without right. And according to them (Abu Yusuf? Actually 'lahuma' refers to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad? The text says 'wa lahuma' which likely means the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) say: The need to remove deprivation from inheritance is also considered just as in removing liability, due to the kinship causing inheritance. His saying: 'the opposite of it' means if the just person kills the rebel, he is not deprived of inheritance; because he killed by right, and the cause of deprivation of inheritance is only killing without right. His saying: 'does not inherit him' due to lack of doubt (shubhah), which is the interpretation of believing oneself to be in the right. His saying: 'and selling weapons' and likewise gifting and all other means of ownership; but selling what weapons are made from, like iron, is not disliked. And similar to it is that selling musical instruments (ma'azif) is disliked because sin is established in their very entity, but selling wood from which musical instruments are made is not disliked. On this basis, selling grape juice is valid, and selling wine is disliked. Thus in 'al-Fath'.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage from the supercommentary 'Umda al-Ri'aya on Sharh al-Wiqaya addresses two main rulings concerning rebels (bughat) within the Hanafi school. The first ruling deals with a situation where rebels take over a city and a resident kills another. The commentary explains that if the rebels have not yet established their own legal system, the authority of the just imam remains intact, and the imam executes qisas (retaliation) against the killer. However, if the rebels have implemented their own laws, the imam's authority over that city is cut off.

The second and more extensive ruling concerns inheritance between a rebel and a just person. The base text states that a rebel who kills a just person while claiming to be in the right inherits from that person. The commentary explains the rationale: because the rebel acted under a false interpretation combined with strength (man'ah), this is treated analogously to a correct interpretation, thus the killing does not entail qisas or diyah, and the rebel is not barred from inheritance. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that the rebel is a Muslim bound by Islamic law, and his killing of a just person is prohibited, so he is barred from inheritance as a penalty. The text also notes that al-Shafi'i agrees with Abu Yusuf. The preferred position (mufta bihi) according to al-Sarakhsi is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Furthermore, if the just person kills the rebel, the just person inherits by consensus because the killing is by right. But if the rebel admits that he was upon falsehood (i.e., does not claim a rightful interpretation), then he does not inherit. Finally, the commentary addresses the ruling on selling weapons to rebels: it is disliked (makruh) according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but selling raw materials like iron is not disliked. A similar principle applies to selling musical instruments vs. wood used to make them.

Key Terms
باغ (baghi): A rebel who fights against the legitimate Muslim ruler due to a mistaken interpretation (ta'wil) and has collective power (man'ah).
عادل (adil): A just person who is not a rebel; here specifically one who is on the side of the legitimate ruler.
منعة (man'ah): Collective power or strength that enables the rebels to resist the imam; it is a condition for their actions to be treated with leniency.
تأويل فاسد (ta'wil fasid): A false or incorrect interpretation of Islamic law that the rebels sincerely believe, combined with man'ah, lessens their culpability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority view of the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), when does a rebel inherit from a just person he killed?
A) Always, regardless of the rebel's claim.
B) Only if the rebel claims to be upon the truth (i.e., has a plausible interpretation).
C) Only if the just person also inherits from the rebel.
D) Never, because killing bars inheritance in all cases.

Key Takeaway
A rebel who kills a just person while claiming to be right inherits from that person according to the majority Hanafi position, due to the mitigating effect of false interpretation and collective power.

Jihad — Armed Conflict Rules
intermediate
6 min
Ruling on Selling Weapons to People of Fitnah
Clarifies the ruling on selling weapons to rebels and criminals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling weapons to known rebels/criminals and identify the condition that changes the ruling.

Core Idea
Selling weapons to known people of fitnah is makruh tahrimi because it aids sin.

In times of civil strife, a Muslim merchant may face the dilemma of selling weapons to a buyer who might use them unlawfully. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling: if the seller knows the buyer is from the people of fitnah, the sale becomes prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi); otherwise it remains permissible. This lesson unpacks the conditions and reasoning behind this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Read the opening phrase: 'If he knows that he is from the people of fitnah, it is disliked; otherwise no.' This sets the conditional framework.
The matn only says 'disliked' (karih); the type of dislike is clarified later.
2
Expand the scope of 'ahl al-fitnah'
The commentary explains that 'people of fitnah' includes rebels (bughat), highway robbers (qutta' al-tariq), thieves (lusus), and similar criminals. Understand the breadth of the category.
This ensures the ruling applies to all forms of unlawful rebellion and criminal disorder.
3
Determine the type of dislike
The commentary cites al-Bahr al-Ra'iq stating that the dislike is prohibitive (karahah tahrimiyyah), not merely tanzihiyyah. This means it is nearly haram.
The distinction is important: makruh tahrimi requires avoidance but is not sin if done under necessity.
4
Understand the legal reasoning
The reason for the prohibitive dislike is that selling weapons to such people constitutes 'i'ana 'ala al-ma'siyah' (aiding in sin), which is forbidden.
This principle is general in Hanafi fiqh: any act that facilitates sin is at least makruh tahrimi.
5
Note the exception when knowledge is absent
If the seller does not know the buyer's status, the sale is not disliked because the element of aiding sin is absent.
This highlights the subjective element: the ruling depends on the seller's knowledge, not the buyer's actual status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من رجل إن عَلِمَ أنه من أهل الفتنة كره، وإلا فلا.

قوله : من أهل الفتنة ; يشمل البغاة وقطَّاعَ الطَّريق واللصوص ونحوهم. قوله : كره ; قال في «البحر» : ظاهرُ كلامهم أنَّ الكراهة تحريمية ; لتعليلهم بالإعانة على المعصية. قوله : وإلا فلا ; يعني إن لم يعلم أنه من أهل الفتنةِ لم يكره بيعُ السّلاح . لعدم وجود الإعانة على المعصية فيه.
From a man: if he knows that he is from the people of tribulation, it is disliked; otherwise no. He said: 'from the people of tribulation' includes rebels, highway robbers, thieves, and the like. He said: 'it is disliked'; it is stated in 'al-Bahr': the apparent meaning of their words is that the dislike is prohibitive (tahrimiyyah), because they give the reason as aiding in sin. He said: 'otherwise no' means if he does not know that he is from the people of tribulation, the sale of weapons is not disliked, due to the absence of aiding in sin.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on a matn ruling regarding the sale of weapons. The base text states: 'If a man knows that the buyer is from the people of fitnah (tribulation), selling to him is disliked; otherwise it is not.' The commentator first clarifies that 'people of fitnah' includes not only rebels (bughāt) but also highway robbers (quttā' al-tarīq), thieves (lusūs), and similar criminals. Then he explains the nature of the dislike: citing al-Bahr al-Rā'iq, he states that the dislike is of the prohibitive type (karāhah taḥrīmiyyah) according to the apparent meaning of the scholars' statements. The reason for this severity is that selling weapons to such individuals constitutes assistance in sin (i'ānah 'alā al-ma'siyah), which is forbidden. Finally, he explains the exception: if the seller does not know that the buyer is from the people of fitnah, then the sale is not disliked, because the element of aiding sin is absent. This ruling is based on the general principle that assisting in sin is prohibited.

Key Terms
أهل الفتنة (ahl al-fitnah): People involved in tribulation, including rebels, highway robbers, thieves, and similar criminals.
كراهة تحريمية (karāhah taḥrīmiyyah): Prohibitive dislike; an act that is closer to haram than to halal but is not definitively forbidden.
إعانة على المعصية (i'ānah 'alā al-ma'siyah): Aiding or assisting in sin; the reason for the prohibitive dislike.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is selling weapons to someone known to be from the people of fitnah considered prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi)?
A) Because it weakens the state's military power
B) Because it involves aiding in sin (i'ānah 'alā al-ma'siyah)
C) Because it encourages rebellion
D) Because the buyer might use the weapon against the seller

Key Takeaway
Selling weapons to known people of fitnah is prohibitively disliked because it aids sin; ignorance of the buyer's status lifts the dislike.
